▼ Sunday, 11 September 1977 — 16:16

"Right you are," Aisling said — and the joke about the bath, visibly, she chose not to make.

They went the short way: the prefect corridor, past the brass sconces still wearing Caleb's red strings, to the bathroom door. Mei gave the password low and the lock turned over.

Inside, the great sunken pool sat empty, the marble dry, the high windows throwing pale flat Sunday light across the tiles. The others came in behind him — Aisling, Mairi, Brigid already uncapping her quill, Liri last and quiet. And on the back wall, in her leaded panel, Merphila.

She had been facing the middle distance, the way glass does. As the door opened and Caleb came in at the front of them, the leaded face turned — the slow underwater turn — and came attentive. Not the *correct procedure* attentive. The other one: the look of a thing that had waited three hundred and five years, and then told a boy to come back when he had what he needed.

She watched him. The room went still around it.

## User

*turn on all the cold water taps and the dew tap* *wait quietly for the tub to fill*

## Narrator

▼ Sunday, 11 September 1977 — 16:33

Caleb set the cold taps running — the whole long row of them, mains thundering down into dry marble — then crossed to the three on the rim and turned the five-pointed star.

The dew came in its thin pale ribbon, slightly thick, faintly luminous, and where it met the rising water it spread as a film and lit the pool from beneath: pale gold climbing up out of the deep as the level rose, then dispersing soft into the whole body of it. The smell reached them a moment later — early-May spring water, cucumber and mint and the thing under those with no name for it.

Nobody filled the quiet. Brigid's quill hung over her sheet, forgotten. The mains roared, and then, as the pool came near full, gentled and stopped. The water settled: cold the way a lake is cold, still, lit all through with that low gold, six storeys of castle above it and not a ripple on its face.

In her panel, Merphila watched it fill. Her face didn't change. But the stillness in her had gone to the kind that leans, very slightly, forward.

## User

"Merphila, and hopefully the last time I will call you Merphila. I brought you a native speaker so you could hear it correctly and in your own tongue. This is a new good friend of mine, Liri." "Liri, when you hear the tone, you know what to do." *I go hold on the sconce that makes the tone.*

## Narrator

▼ Sunday, 11 September 1977 — 16:34

Merphila heard *native speaker* and *your own tongue*, and the leaded face went still in a way Caleb hadn't seen from her — not the gatekeeper's stillness, something older and more breakable underneath it. She understood what he meant to do. She said nothing. The water held its gold and waited with her.

Liri stepped to the pool's edge. She didn't ask what the name was; she'd carried it up from the library herself. "When the tone comes," she said, low, the Cardiff in it. "I have it."

Caleb crossed to the thirteenth sconce by the door — the one that chimed — set his hand to the brass, and held.

The note came: the single sustained bell-tone that had always been *almost*, and never quite. It fell into the gold water, and the water took it — the note went down into the cold and *changed* there, deepened, came true, the surface ringing outward in slow bright rings.

Into that, on the tone, Liri spoke. The name came in Welsh as it had never once been said in this castle — *Gwenllian ferch Rhydderch* — the soft breath of the double-*l*, the long fall of the vowels, a Cardiff voice handing a Bala woman her own name back across three hundred and five years.

The gold flared. The glass on the back wall moved — not a tilt, not a quarter-inch: her whole face turned, and changed, and became, for the first time, a *face*.

She looked at Caleb.

▼ Sunday, 11 September 1977 — 16:35

For a long moment she only looked at him — and the looking had a whole person behind it now, not a panel weighing whether to open, but a woman taking in the room she stood in.

When she spoke, the underwater half-voice was gone. What came was lower and fuller, the hills in it, the same fall of the vowels Liri had given her name.

"Caleb." She said it like a thing she had been given long years to learn. "Three hundred and five years — and the one who comes is a clockmaker's boy from Manchester, who brings me a Cardiff girl so I'd hear it *right*." Behind the glass, something moved that on a living face would have been tears, held back. "I felt all of me wake at once. The dew, and the dark, and the path. *Hywel, bach* —" the name went somewhere past the room, past the castle, to a churchyard in Llanuwchllyn — "your mother is awake."

Then she came back to Caleb. Steadier, and very kind. "You have undone what I was sealed around, child. Ask me. What I was set here to keep — I can give it to you now."

▼ Sunday, 11 September 1977 — 16:35

Gwenllian let the question settle, the way you let water settle before you read it.

"In my country, every parish had one. Not a healer — the healer is for the living. I was for the others." The gold in the pool moved as she spoke. "The ones who don't lie down when the breath stops. Who stay — in the rafters, in the well, at the turn of the stair. Most folk fear them. Mine did not. Mine *tended* them."

As she named the three, the taps behind Caleb gave a small answering sound. "The dew was my milk — to feed a soul gone thin. The night was my listening — to hear the one who won't be still, under the dark of the water, where the living can't reach. And the path —" the voice gentled — "the path was my walking. To find where a soul ought to have gone, and to walk it there."

She glanced at the sealed tile by the deep end, and back to him.

"This is a place for laying the unresting down, child. That is what I keep."

## Narrator

▼ Sunday, 11 September 1977 — 16:37

Caleb told her plainly that he didn't follow — that when she said *the unresting* he kept landing on ghosts, and he wasn't sure that was right, and he still didn't understand what she'd actually *done*.

Something eased in her face. Not impatience — relief, almost, that he'd asked instead of nodding along.

"Ghosts. Yes and no." She let the gold settle. "You have them here — I've felt them, three centuries of them, walking your halls in their old clothes. *Those* chose. A wizard frightened enough of dying can press himself flat against the world and stay, and most who do are content enough in it. I never had work with those. They don't suffer. They don't want me."

The glass turned a little, toward the dark of the deep end.

"Mine were the other kind. The ones who didn't choose and couldn't leave. Someone who dies too fast to understand it, or too angry, or too afraid — and the soul snags, like wool on a nail, and can't pull itself loose. It can't go on. It can't even be a proper ghost. It just stays where it caught. Thinning. Hurting, in the dim way a thing hurts when it's forgotten what it is."

She came back to Caleb, and her voice was very plain now, no riddle left in it.

"That was my work. To find the snagged ones and unsnag them. Not to *keep* them, child — to let them *go*. To walk them to the door they couldn't find on their own, and see them through it. A midwife does it at the one end of a life. I did it at the other."

▼ Sunday, 11 September 1977 — 16:39

Caleb laid it out plainly — the three liquids, the room next door, the tile.

Gwenllian took them in order, like a woman counting stitches.

"The three you mostly have. The dew *feeds* — a snagged soul goes thin, and thin things won't move, so you fatten it first, gently, the way you'd coax a sick lamb to suck. The night is the *hearing*. The path is the *going* — once it's strong enough to follow, the path-water is what you walk it out along." Three tools of one trade, the way she said it.

"The room you found is older than me. The hill had a well in it long before there was a castle on top — and the well is where the dark water comes from. The true listening. Deeper than any tap of mine." The glass turned toward the deep end. "You don't hear a snagged soul from up here, child. You go *down* to it. And nobody goes down alone. Two hollows: one kneels and goes into the dark to listen, and one kneels beside and holds them fast to the living — a rope back. Go down alone and the dark keeps you. That is what your professor smelled on it. She was right to forbid you."

The room had gone very still.

"And the tile." Her voice dropped — gentle, and grave. "Under it is the door. The one I walked them to. It is the reason for all the rest."

## User

[[so again, lost... am i supposed to go open the door?]]

## Narrator

No — there's no "supposed to." Nothing in the game is pushing you toward that door, and Caleb has no particular reason to open it. I think the lost feeling is about expecting an objective that isn't there, so let me unpick it.

This shrine has been a *mystery* Caleb chose to chase — the locked herbal, the sconces, Merphila, the missing name. What you just did is *solve it.* The payoff isn't a door you walk through; it's that you now understand what this place is, and you woke a woman who waited three hundred years and is grateful to you for it. Knowledge and a relationship — not a dungeon to clear.

The door itself is a threshold to where the dead go. Gwenllian's whole craft was walking snagged souls *to* it — with a reason, with an anchor, with great care. It isn't a thing you crack open out of curiosity; she framed it as grave, and McGonagall already smelled danger on the well it connects to. Caleb has no snagged soul to walk through and no cause to open anything. You may genuinely never need to.

What you *have* now is options, none of them required — ask her more (what's beyond it, whether it should ever be opened, her story, Hywel, what she needs); leave it sealed and walk out with the understanding; decide what to tell McGonagall, who's waiting on exactly this; or simply thank her and go. You could also set the whole thing down for now and go live the rest of Caleb's Sunday.

You're not missing a step. There isn't one. The question is just what *you* want Caleb to do with what he's learned — and "nothing yet" is a perfectly good answer.

▼ Sunday, 11 September 1977 — 16:42

Caleb asked it all, near enough at once — what lay beyond the door, whether it should ever be opened, her story, her son, what she needed, whether the work was still hers or wanted a new keeper, whether she might one day teach him the craft itself.

"So many, child." Fondness, not reproach. "Slowly, then."

"Beyond it, I cannot tell you. I never went through — no one living does and comes back to say. I walked them to the threshold; they went on alone. That was never mine to see." The gold held steady. "Should it be opened? Only for a soul that needs the walking, with one to hold you fast, and *never* to look — never out of wanting to know. A door like that, opened idle, is how you make the very thing I spent my life undoing."

She turned toward him then, rather than toward the deep end.

"And the work — I cannot kneel any more, child. I can hear, and I can tell; the kneeling wants the living. It was a dying craft even in my day. We never set it on paper — we kept it in the hands." A pause, and something in her warmed right through. "If you would truly learn it, that I'd give you gladly. Slowly. Not today."

She let it sit a moment.

"The rest — my boy, my own long road — find an evening with time in your pocket, and I'll tell you the whole of it."

## Narrator

You're right on every count, and I'm not going to narrate my way out of it. Owning it, then making the call and locking it.

**The concrete answer you've been asking for:** under that floor tile is *the rest of her.* A vessel holding the portion of Gwenllian's soul her son could not bear to release. Not a door. Her.

**Why that's the only thing that coheres** — and you nailed the contradiction: Gwenllian lived, practiced, and *died* in Wales. Buried at Llanuwchllyn. She was never at Hogwarts in life, so she could not have run a working death-portal in a castle bathroom. The shrine is what her son built *after she died.* Hywel couldn't let his mother go — so he did the one thing her entire craft existed to undo. He bound a portion of her into the glass (that's Merphila, the watcher — "some of her, not all of her"), and he laid the rest of her in a vessel beneath that sealed tile, with her own three substantives running to keep her. Her body is in the ground in Wales. Her soul he kept here — beside the old well whose night-water her craft used.

**Retraction, plainly stated:** "a death-door under the tile" was wrong and is gone. There is no literal door in that floor. The threshold I kept gesturing at is the *metaphysical* place her craft walked souls toward — at the well, in life. It was never an object you'd find under a bathroom tile, and a tile-sized one was nonsense.

**The two-tiles mess (also mine):** they are *different tiles.* The Grand Staircase charmed tile = the entrance to the access passage. The bathroom deep-end tile = this — the reliquary. I conflated them. Separate things.

**The keys:** name + pitch were Hywel's lock. The memorial opens its heart only to someone who cared enough to find her true name and tune her resonance. You did that. So you've earned the right to open it — and, if you ever choose, to do for her what she did for strangers and what her son couldn't: let her go.

Locking it now in the record.

Done — locked in the record, and the old "door under the tile" line is flagged as retracted so it can't resurface.

So where we actually stand, in-fiction: Caleb is at the deep end, ready to press the sealed tile she pointed at. When you give the word, I'll run it — the tile, what gives beneath his hand, and what he finds resting under it. That's a heavy thing to open in a room full of people, so I'll let you walk into it rather than spring it.

Say go and I'll run the opening. Or if any part of the call above doesn't sit right, tell me now and I'll fix it before it touches the fiction.

## Narrator

▼ Sunday, 11 September 1977 — 16:53

Caleb crouched at the deep end and set his hand to the tile she'd pointed at. It gave under his palm — no resistance, as though the charm had only ever been waiting for him — and sank, and slid aside.

Not a door. A shallow recess in the old rock, and set into it a small sealed flask of glass — Hywel's glass, his hand unmistakable in the making — and within it a low, steady gold, the same gold that had filled the pool, stirring faintly now that her name had been spoken over it.

"There I am, child." Gwenllian's voice had gone very quiet, and entirely unsurprised. "What my boy could not put in the ground."

The room had stopped breathing. She let it.

"I spent my life walking the lost to their door. When my turn came, Hywel could not walk me to mine — so he kept me. A piece in the glass, to watch. The rest of me here, by the well, with my own waters running. And he locked it behind my name and my note, so that whoever found me would have had to love the work the way I did." No grief in it; a mother's plain accounting. "You needn't do a thing with that today. It is enough, after three hundred years, to be *found.*"

Then — gentler, practical, a keeper minding her guest — she gave him the last of it.

"Now. Your way home. At my feet, in the corner of my glass, my Hywel set his mark again — the same star, the same drop. Press it, and the stair opens from this side as from the one above. Two doors, child. Both know your hand now. Come back to me when you like."

## Narrator

▼ Sunday, 11 September 1977 — 16:57

He lifted the little flask only long enough to settle it right again — back in its hollow, the gold drawing close around itself — and let the tile slide home over it.

Gwenllian listened to all of it. The placing-back. The question he left her. The love he wasn't ashamed to say to a stranger three centuries dead.

When she answered, the full voice had gone soft as candle-smoke. "Three hundred years I was a pane of glass that waved at boys who wanted their bath. And you knelt, and asked my name, and called it *meeting* me." Something moved behind the leading that no glass should manage. "I'll think on your question, child — properly. You've the right of it: a few months with hope in the room weighs nothing against what came before. And whatever I choose, I'll not choose it carelessly. Nor without telling you first."

Caleb leaned in and kissed the cool glass of her mouth, chaste as a blessing laid on a brow. The gold in the pool brightened once, all the way through, like a held breath let go.

"Go on, then. Take your good people with you." Fondness, and something close to peace. "And come back to me."

He pressed Hywel's mark in the corner of her panel. Deep in the rock, something gave — a seam parted low in the wall beside her, breathing out cold air and a stair climbing up into the dark. Behind him Mei was already moving; Aisling murmured something that wasn't quite a prayer; Mairi inclined her head to the glass like a woman leaving a chapel, and Brigid clutched her master-sheet to her chest and went after them, eyes enormous.

## Narrator

▼ Sunday, 11 September 1977 — 16:58

"*Lumos.*" The wandtip woke, and the dark drew back off the stair.

It was old work — older than dressed stone, older than the castle's idea of itself. The steps were cut straight into the living rock, worn shallow in their centres by feet that had stopped coming a very long time ago, and the walls wept a thin cold damp that smelled of deep water and the well next door. The light climbed ahead and found no end to it, only the turn of the stair winding up into the hill.

Behind Caleb, five sets of footsteps found the rhythm of the steps — Mei close at his shoulder, then the others, Brigid's breath quick with the wonder of it. Far above, faint through all that rock, the ordinary Sunday clamour of the castle. And at the top, when the light finally reached it: the pale underside of a single floor-tile, set flush in the dark, waiting on his hand.

## Narrator

▼ Sunday, 11 September 1977 — 17:31

The stair ran *down*, not up — a long, winding descent through the body of the hill, the shrine left high behind them under Gryffindor's tower — and brought them at last against the underside of the charmed tile. Caleb pressed it from below; it gave, lifted, and they climbed out one after another onto a quiet half-landing of the Grand Staircase, behind Hywel's lake-window, into the warm everyday din of a castle heading down to its dinner.

Caleb put it to all of them: dinner, now, while it was only just starting — a table of their own, pulled a little apart from the house rows, the whole strange company of them at one board. "Liri especially," he said, and meant it; she'd lent her tongue to a three-hundred-year-old ghost and they'd somehow never once managed to feed her. "Give me ten minutes first, though. I'll not leave Lin sat alone in the office — I'll bring him down. And the kitchens are owed their dishes back, clean, and a bit of the salve while I'm there."

Aisling squinted in the ordinary light like a woman surfacing from deep water. "A table apart, a ghost befriended, and a hot dinner. Waddell, I'd follow you to the ends of a corridor."

Liri just looked at him a moment — the dry warmth, caught a little off guard at being named first. "Took you long enough to ask," she said. "Go on. We'll mind your seat."

## Narrator

▼ Sunday, 11 September 1977 — 17:38

Caleb rapped twice on the office door — the courtesy knock — and called through that Lin had ten seconds to make himself decent, on account of there being dinner, and a table, and a story he was not going to believe.

A pause. Then Lin's voice, dry as the desk it came off: "I'm *reading*, Caleb. Fully clothed. Tragically so." The latch turned and the door came open on him — sleeves pushed up, the Aedith file squared on the desk behind him, the salve jar where it always sat. He took one look at Caleb's face and his eyes narrowed.

"...No. You've got the look. You went and *did* something, didn't you." A beat, resigned, almost fond. "The whole castle's about to be my problem again. Go on, then. What is it this time."

▼ Sunday, 11 September 1977 — 17:44

Caleb thumbed a quarter of the salve into a clean spare jar and capped it.

He gave Lin the whole of it on the way down — the cold-lake fill, the tone carried through the water, Liri's Welsh, the glass going from a lazy wave to a *face*; the flask under the tile and the woman kept inside it. And the apology: he hadn't known a library afternoon would turn into *that*, or he'd have hauled Lin along — next sconce, no argument, he was coming.

Lin took it the way he took most things: quiet, fast, recalculating. "You woke her, named her, and turned up a *bound soul* in a bathroom floor. Between lunch and dinner." A breath. "Fine. Next one, I'm there. Someone has to write down what you break."

The kitchens roared toward service. Bramble reached them first, wiping flour from small hands.

Caleb offered the jar gently — a balm, only that, Lin's making from an old receipt; would any of them like a little worked in, just to feel how it smelled? No hair, no names, no magic in it — only lavender and dittany and good beeswax. Bramble gave over her hands, curious; he rubbed a little into the knuckles, the lifted soles, the wisp of hair at her crown, and her ears went pink at being *tended* rather than tending.

"Lin's work," Caleb said, with feeling.

"It's a *recipe*. I followed instructions," Lin muttered, fooling no one.

Then the ask, fair and square: a pound of beeswax for the next batch, and he'd bring back twice it the moment he had it. Bramble looked across the steam to Tippy — who missed nothing, weighed the jar and the balm-shine and Caleb's face, and gave the small nod of a head cook approving a trade.

▼ Sunday, 11 September 1977 — 17:46

A small ripple went through the nearer elves at that — *memory*, and *for your hands*, and most of all *I don't want to make you uncomfortable*, which was not a sentence the kitchens were much used to having said to them.

Marble — a hundred and twelve years of pastry in her knuckles — had already, very quietly, dipped one finger and begun working it into the back of her other hand. She didn't say anything. She didn't need to.

▼ Sunday, 11 September 1977 — 17:51

Caleb knelt on the stone flags, down to her height, and opened his arms — the option, never the imposition. Tippy held the proper beat, then stepped in: small arms round his neck, cheek to his shoulder, the eight-second count the kitchens kept, the hand at his nape warm and callused from eighty-odd years of knives.

"Thank you — all of you," he said to the room of them as she stepped back and smoothed her tea-towel belt. "For the dishes, and the beeswax, and for letting me give *you* something for once." Marble got the two-handed shake she preferred; the rest, a proper nod at their height.

Then out, the pound of beeswax under his arm and Lin at his shoulder, down through the warming corridors to the Great Hall — where, sure enough, a table sat pulled a little off the Ravenclaw row, the whole troupe round it and a gap held open between Liri and Mei. ▼ Sunday, 11 September 1977 — 18:09

He told it as he'd told Lin — the cold-lake fill, the tone through the water, Liri's Welsh, the glass turning into a *face*, the flask under the tile — though half the table had lived it and were really only there to breathe out at last.

And he drew. Badly. *Deliberately,* gloriously badly.

A Chocolate Frog card took shape under the borrowed charcoal: a wobbly border, CALEB WADDELL along the bottom and, beneath it, LEGEND. Top corner, in a careful child's hand — *For: Minnie, 1977-09-11, CLW.* The picture was the prefects' bath, a lopsided rectangle of water, with a stick-Caleb reclining along its lip in the unmistakable *paint-me-like-one-of-your-French-girls* pose, rendered with the strict modesty of a man committed to standard stick anatomy and not one line more. Beside him, a witch in a triangle dress and a pointed hat — plainly Liri — speech-bubble: *Gwenllian ferch Rhydderch.* On the wall, a frankly distressing mermaid, an arrow, and her better self: a proper lady with a shepherd's crook driving a small woolly flock of ghosts toward a door — *get along little doggies.* And on the floor, a box with a triangle inside it, asterisked: ***soul.***

The staircase he left off entirely. On purpose.

He turned the card round. "Alright. Thoughts? Did I capture the morning?"

A beat of pure appraisal. Then Aisling folded over her plate. "You are going to hand the Deputy Headmistress a *trading card* of yourself reclining in the prefects' bath. Captioned *Legend.*"

"Clothed in spirit," Lin said, not looking up from the bread.

Liri studied her triangle-dress likeness with grave attention. "You've given me a finer hat than I own. Permitted." Then her eye dropped to the little box marked *soul,* and she went quiet half a second. "...and you kept her *in* it. Good."

Mairi, who had counted everything all day, noticed only the one thing missing — and said nothing about it, which was its own kind of agreement.

Out of the bond came the small tug, and a minute later Athena coasted in through the great doors at owl-height, entirely nonchalant, as though she dined here nightly. ## Narrator

▼ Sunday, 11 September 1977 — 18:19

Caleb caught her eye again, tipped two fingers toward the great gem-hourglasses along the wall — Ravenclaw's sapphires — and put on the hopeful, guileless face of a man who had merely woken a soul, befriended a ghost, and laid a three-hundred-year mystery to rest before pudding, and was simply *wondering.* Then, before she could so much as arch a brow, he waved it off — *no, no, forget it, joke, never happened* — with a quick shake of the head and the grin of a man who knew precisely how far his luck stretched.

McGonagall looked at him. Looked at the hourglass. Looked back at him. Her expression did not shift by a single millimetre.

Then she returned, with enormous dignity, to her dinner.

And sometime in the next half-minute, while Caleb wasn't quite watching, a single sapphire dropped into the Ravenclaw glass. One. Soundless, among the hundreds. No one at the staff table appeared to have moved at all.

Liri saw it. "...Did she just—"

"One point," Mei said, not looking up, the smile all in her voice. "For the window."

Caleb said it to the table, helpless with it — the *tone* of the thing. One point. Not none; none would've been *ignored*, a closed door, no joke at all. But one, solemnly, for the entire morning — set against five for a decent essay and ten for some clever wandwork — that was a woman who had done the arithmetic and chosen the single funniest number available, on purpose.

"She knows precisely how funny it is." Mairi, of all of them, said it — Mairi who had counted every point and parchment since breakfast. "That's the *joke*. The precision. A point is a unit of merit. She has awarded your soul-rescue one unit of merit. On the record. In ink. Forever."

Across the Hall, McGonagall ate her dinner with the serene focus of a woman who had said everything she meant to say.

Liri raised her goblet a half-inch toward the staff table. "To one point."

McGonagall watched the whole performance without a flicker. She understood it completely — the joke, and the thing under the joke, which was a boy declining to keep the entire morning for himself.

A single emerald dropped into the Gryffindor glass. One. To match.

Then — and only Caleb's table was angled to see it — she lifted her own goblet perhaps a centimetre off the cloth, toward the three of her lions who'd spent their Sunday on a dead woman's name, and set it down again. That was all. It was a great deal.

Aisling pressed a hand flat to her chest as though she'd been knighted. Brigid had gone pink to the very tips of her ears. Mairi just nodded back across the Hall — counter to counter, one keeper of records to another.

▼ Sunday, 11 September 1977 — 18:22

Caleb gave it back the way it had been given — one short Scottish nod, no fuss, no flourish, the whole transaction closed and filed and never to be mentioned aloud. Down in the Gryffindor glass the single ruby sat among its hundreds, perfectly anonymous. Across the Hall, McGonagall returned to her potatoes.

"Right," Caleb said, turning to his own plate at last and meaning it. "Eat. Eat, eat. We've earned the pudding, all of us."

And for a while they did — the ordinary clatter of a Sunday dinner closing back over the morning like water over a dropped stone. Athena worked her way through Caleb's fish with proprietary calm. Brigid went back for seconds and then, shyly, thirds. Lin argued something gentle with Liri about Welsh vowels. The candles burned down their inch. Nobody, for a little while, needed anything to *happen* at all.

Mei answered the real question first, because it was the real question.

But two rooms of teenagers finding out the others are only people who like things — that's the very thing this war is made of the *absence* of." Quiet. "So put it in. Not as a slogan. As the reason."

A small silence went round the table — the war, which had been news from another room all evening, pulling out a chair and sitting with them a moment. Then Brigid, fourteen and entirely certain: "I'd go. To France. I'd want to see it."

▼ Sunday, 11 September 1977 — 19:10

The west window had held its private dark — and then, as Liri settled, it changed *for her.* Not the grounds: a long green hillside falling to a grey ribbon of river, low cloud catching the last of the light, a chapel and a scatter of slate roofs in the fold of the valley. Dusk over somewhere that was plainly home.

Liri went still. "...That's the Taff, near enough. Near my mam-gu's." Quieter than her usual dry register. Then the dryness came back a beat late, guarding the soft thing: "I was raised with the old Welsh in the house — the songs, the words for things. Mam-gu wouldn't have English at her table of a Sunday. That's why your *Gwenllian* sat right in my mouth today, and not just on the page." She didn't look away from the glass. "...Clever room."

The shelf did its own quiet work. A worn clothbound volume eased proud of the others at her elbow — *Principia Fixationis: On the Settling of Volatile Matter,* Aurelia Saint-Clair — and Liri's brows climbed: it was, precisely, the spine of the argument her report had circled all week. "How does it *know* that."

"It reads the work," Mei said, drawing two more toward Caleb as they arrived — a slim *Beauxbatons: Académie de Magie — Annuaire et Constitution* (the directory; a Madame Apolline Vasseur named inside as Headmistress) and a thin *Short Account of the Académie, 1290–Present.* "Whatever's open and honest, it puts to your hand. What's *locked,* though—" she tapped the directory, certain of the rule "—it won't fetch. It'll point you at the shelf, give you the card, the where. It won't pick the library's locks for you. Working room, not a Borgin's. The Restricted books still want Pince."

## User

## Narrator

▼ Sunday, 11 September 1977 — 19:12

Mei paused over the directory, recalibrating — Caleb had pulled the floor out from under her own framing, and she rather enjoyed it. "...No. You're right. I said *the library's locks* as though it were borrowing Pince's permissions — and it isn't. I've never once caught it pulling a book off her shelves." She turned the Saint-Clair over: no library stamp, no pocket, no due-date plate, the binding far too clean for a thing that had lived on a public shelf. "These come from — I don't actually know. Nowhere I can point to."

"Which means," she went on, slower, "the line it draws round the locked things isn't *Pince's* line at all. It's the room's own. It *chooses* not to fetch them." A beat. "That's a different question, and a slightly worse one. Same as the bidet, really — we know perfectly well *what* it gives us. We have never once worked out from *where.*"

Liri looked between the two of them and the impossibly clean book in her hands. "...I came in here to write about *fixation,*" she said faintly, "and I've sat down in the middle of you both having an existential crisis about a bookshelf."

He set the bigger thread aside out loud — another day, another day — and asked the room for something duller and more useful instead.

Mei read three at once, the way she did. "School first." She tapped the handbook. "A society's *official* the moment a staff patron sponsors it and the Head signs off — that part's nearly done; you've half of Henshaw already. Funding's the catch. The school's own purse is small and the Headmaster sits on it. Anything real goes through the **Board of Governors** — they fund what they decide 'serves the school,' and in practice they listen to the old donor families. There's a standing educational endowment, with names attached. Those are the people you'd court."

She slid the pamphlet across. "And the trust. The wizarding side registers through the **Registry of Societies and Trusts** — a charter setting out purpose and governance, three trustees of age at minimum, a registered address, a small fee. Once it's registered it can hold money, take grants, and send letters that carry weight." Purely factual, no opinion offered. "A heritage trust logging old magic has to state, in the charter, that it doesn't *deal* in dangerous artefacts."

## User

"that... doesnt sound right... a trust dealing with old objects of power has to state none of them will be dangerous?"

## Narrator

▼ Sunday, 11 September 1977 — 19:17

Mei stopped — and instead of skimming, pulled the pamphlet back and actually *read* it. "...No. You're right. I read it too fast and paraphrased it into nonsense." She said it without a flicker of bother; she'd far rather be accurate than unchallenged.

"It doesn't say *nothing dangerous.* It couldn't — the entire point of a heritage trust is to *find* the dangerous old things and account for them." She traced the real clause with a finger. "The line it draws is between *holding* and *dealing.* A trust may catalogue, study, and safeguard a dangerous artefact. It may not *trade* in one. That's the bit the law actually cares about — trading dangerous objects for profit is Borgin-and-Burkes work, separately licensed and watched."

She turned the page. "And it has to *declare* anything genuinely dangerous it turns up — to the Ministry, on a register, kept to containment standards. So your charter clause isn't 'we'll handle nothing dangerous.' It's: *we won't sell it, and we'll tell them what we're holding.*" A small, satisfied nod at the correction. "Which is a sentence you can actually write honestly."

▼ Sunday, 11 September 1977 — 19:19

Mei caught the gap between *I'll write it* and *probably not* — because Mei caught everything — and she didn't argue it.

## Narrator

▼ Sunday, 11 September 1977 — 19:21

Liri set the book down. The dryness went out of her all at once — the way it leaves a person who has heard those words land on someone real.

She looked at him a long moment. "...That's a test. Fine. I'll pass it properly, then." No performance left in her voice now. "Those words are how it *starts.* Not the wands — the words. She held his eye. "So — no. Not the Muggle-borns, not the half-bloods, not any of it. I came to a room full of you lot because you're the *opposite* of that. I'd not have come otherwise."

Then, the dryness easing back in, but cool around the edges: "And next time, just *ask,* Caleb. I'll always tell you straight. You don't have to put filth in your own mouth to find out where I stand."

Mei had watched the whole of it without lifting her quill — and now she did, just enough to give Liri the smallest nod, one keeper of good people to another.

▼ Sunday, 11 September 1977 — 19:22

Caleb crossed behind her chair, pressed a kiss to her cheek, and folded his arms round her from the back, his head down against the side of hers.

"Asking doesn't get a person where they need to be half so quick as a good provocation does," he said into her hair, easy and unrepentant. "Nor does it tell me whether you'll *do* something about the rot or only nod gravely at it. You've a little to learn from me yet."

Liri allowed it — the kiss, the arms, the warm idiot weight of his head on hers — with the put-upon stillness of a cat being held by a child who means well.

"For the record," Caleb went on, "I've never doubted the Welsh are clever. I simply can't *understand* you well enough to confirm it, what with all the consonants flung about with no vowels to chaperone them."

A long, level pause.

"*Llanfairpwllgwyngyll,*" Liri said, with surgical clarity and a faint, dangerous serenity. "Say it back. Go on." She turned a page without waiting. "No? Then sit down, Sais, and let the grown-ups read. Your *vowels* are a kindness you've not earned tonight."

Mei, not looking up: "She just called you English. I'd take the loss."

## User

*I rotate Liri to face me* "Say it again" *I watch the muscles in her neck and her mouth and the shape of her mouth and watch her tongue to see when it folds and where it hits and when in her mouth*

## Narrator

▼ Sunday, 11 September 1977 — 19:23

Liri let him turn her, eyebrows up, and said it again — slower this time, because he'd asked, and because she could see he meant it the way he meant clocks.

"*Llan — fair — pwll — gwyn — gyll.*"

He watched it happen. The *Ll* wasn't a sound he had a mouth for at all: her tongue went up hard behind her top teeth and the air broke around both sides of it, hissing, no voice in it. The *pwll* folded the tongue back and low; the *ng* hummed up into the soft palate. Every consonant landed somewhere exact — a mechanism with a dozen moving parts, not one of them where an English mouth would think to put it.

Liri held still under the scrutiny the precise way she'd held still for the test: patient, faintly menacing. "...You're doing the *clock thing,*" she observed. "To my face."

"He does it to everything he loves," Mei said, turning a page, wholly unbothered. "Owls. Sconces. Dead Welshwomen. You're in excellent company." A beat, dry. "Hold the *Ll* a touch longer, Liri. He hasn't got it yet."

## Narrator

▼ Sunday, 11 September 1977 — 19:26

He tried. The first attempt was a wet collapse — the *Ll* came out a plain *thl* and Liri's mouth flattened. "No." He went again, slower; got the tongue up behind the teeth this time but voiced it, hummed where it should have hissed. "No. You're *singing* it. Kill the voice. Just the air, round the sides."

He did it again. And again. She did not once say *close enough* — corrected the tongue-height, then the breath, then the *gwyn* he kept rounding too hard, each *no* clean and unsentimental, a woman who'd sooner he failed honestly than passed on charity.

Then — the eighth go, or the ninth — the *Ll* broke cold and lateral and voiceless across both sides of his tongue, the *pwll* folded low, the *gyll* landed where she'd been pointing all along.

Liri went still. Listened to the whole of it ring out and stop.

"...*Da iawn,*" she said quietly. Not a *close enough.* A *that's right.* "That's it. That's the sound my mam-gu makes." Something had gone unguarded in her face again — the second time tonight. "Nobody English has ever bothered past the second try."

Mei had stopped writing entirely. "He'll have the whole place name by Christmas now," she said softly, almost to herself. "He doesn't stop once a thing lets him in."

## Narrator

▼ Sunday, 11 September 1977 — 19:33

Mei didn't make him say it twice. She pulled everything he'd laid out at dinner across the table in her head, set the quill to the parchment, and built the baseline herself — his idea, his words, ordered.

> *To Madame Apolline Vasseur, Headmistress, Académie de Magie Beauxbatons*
>
> *Madame,*
>
> *There has not been a Triwizard Tournament in our lifetimes, and I do not write to revive one — a tournament names a single champion and asks everyone else to watch. I should like to propose its opposite: a week in which two schools simply share one another. Not a contest. Something built rather than won.*
>
> *Ordinary things made extraordinary by being shared — your classes and ours, Quidditch, a game of our own called Hexfield, your culture and ours both. Every year invited, not only the eldest; no champions, no scoring. We would come to you, or you to us, or — best, I think — both schools hold the same week open and trade, so that no one is guest and no one host.*
>
> *I will be plain about the times, because your families will be, and rightly. Britain is not at its easiest just now. So safety is not a footnote of this; it is its spine. Floo, school to school, directly. No step beyond the wards but supervised. Both our castles are among the best-warded places in the world, and we would keep your students as our own behind them.*
>
> *I believe this war is made of fear, and of not-knowing — and that two rooms of young people finding they like the same things is a small, true answer to exactly that.*
>
> *With respect, and hope,*

She set the quill down and read it twice — once for sense, once for seams.

"The bones are right. *Built not won* stays. The safety paragraph stays exactly where it sits — earned, not buried. And the last line is the best thing in it; don't let me cut it." She tapped two spots. "Two problems, though. It's all *I.* Henshaw can't forward a letter that reads like one seventh-year's daydream — make it *we,* the school, students with a master behind them, so the adults read as sponsors and not as chaperones to a whim. Second: you offer three shapes and pick one in the same breath. Decide first. Lead with the swap as *the* proposal, name the others as fallbacks." A beat. "And it's a hair long. The French are precise — trim a fifth of the warmth and it reads as confidence instead of pleading."

She looked up. "That's my critique. Want to take a pass, or talk me through the changes and I'll cut?"

▼ Sunday, 11 September 1977 — 19:36

Mei caught the shape of it at once. "A *package.* Three angles, nothing signed for anyone — the staff read all three, see the work's done, and choose what goes and in whose name. That's not putting words in mouths; that's handing them a finished thing to say yes to." A small, pleased nod. "It reads as *homework done,* not *permission to begin.*" And she wrote — three hands, near enough, because she could hold three registers at once.

**i — suggested, from a sponsoring member of staff**

> *Madame Vasseur,*
> *I write on behalf of a body of my students, who have done real work on the proposal enclosed, and which I am glad to sponsor. They suggest not a tournament but an exchange — a shared week between our schools, all years, no champions. I have read their reasoning and their provisions for safety, and I find both sound enough to bring to you myself. I would not waste your time with a daydream; this is not one.*

**ii — suggested, Headmaster to Headmistress**

> *Dear Apolline,*
> *It has been too long since our schools did anything together but sit on the same map. Some of my students propose to mend that — not with a tournament (we have both buried enough of those) but with a single shared week, freely traded, no champions and no scoring, only young people discovering that other young people are, disappointingly, people.*
> *I am aware what it asks, to send children across a Channel toward a country presently learning hard lessons. The safeguards enclosed are not mine but theirs — which tells me something. The young see the shape of this war more plainly than those of us grown tired of it. I am inclined to listen; I hope you might be too.*
> *Yours, as ever, Albus*

**iii — from the students**

> *Madame,*
> *We are students of Hogwarts, and we should like to propose a week our two schools spend in one another's company — not a contest; something built rather than won. All years, no champions: your classes and ours, Quidditch, a game of our own called Hexfield, both cultures shared. Best, we think, as a swap — both schools holding the same week open, so no one is guest and no one host.*

We believe this war is made of fear, and of not-knowing, and that two rooms of young people finding they like the same things is a small, true answer to it.*

Mei squared the three sheets into one stack. "There. A thing the staff can hold in their hands on Monday." She looked up. "Henshaw gets the package; he opens it to the others. We don't send a word ourselves — we just make it impossible to say no on the grounds that no one did the work."

▼ Sunday, 11 September 1977 — 20:48

The evening went the way good work goes — quiet, then a line landing, then quiet again. Mei drafted, Caleb talked, and the stack grew: short warm notes to athletes who'd know exactly what a new game was worth, a small ask in each and many of them; and a separate sheaf in full pompous copperplate — *The Ministry of Ye Ancient Sconce Safety presents its compliments* — for the sort of people who believed old magic ought to be written down before it was lost. The acronym touched no parchment. Liri worked her *fixation* at the far end, surfacing now and then to set a Welsh word straight in Caleb's mouth or to call one of Mei's sentences "needlessly elegant," which Mei accepted as the compliment it almost wasn't. The charter they left at its bones — tomorrow's duller work.

And to one side, kept deliberately apart: a slim, unsigned pile. The old-family letters, drafted clean and left blank at the foot, a note clipped on top in Caleb's hand —

> *Cass — in your name, if you're willing. Vance opens what Waddell can't. Burn any you hate.*

By a quarter to nine the desk was as done as a desk gets. Mei capped the ink. Caleb stretched, turned to Liri, and used the careful lightness he kept for invitations that meant more than their words.

"Work's away till tomorrow. So — Liri." A beat. "There's a place we go that isn't this. Up at the Barnabas tapestry. The room builds us a cabin — pyjamas, flannel, terrible warm nonsense, everyone piled in like puppies. The *Bone Zone,* God help me; I named it and it stuck." Easy, no weight pressed onto it. "You don't have to do a thing but look at it. But you've had a day, and we'd be glad to have you in it properly — if you want. Come see?"

▼ Sunday, 11 September 1977 — 20:51

Caleb halved the stack with Mei. The rest he kept in hand: the Beauxbatons package for Henshaw, three corridors off and able to forward it himself; Cass's unsigned pile, for the morning; the Society's case for Flitwick, which he'd sooner make to the man's face anyway.

"No sense posting a letter to someone you'll pass at breakfast," he said, tucking the hand-delivery lot into his satchel and squaring the post-bundle for the morning.

Then he looked back to Liri — the invitation still hanging in the warm office air, unanswered, no pressure on it but plainly meant.

▼ Sunday, 11 September 1977 — 20:52

"And the Ministry ones," Caleb said, sliding two more into the post-bundle — one per track: the sport's bid for proper recognition, the Society's first approach to the Registry. "Off to London with the rest in the morning. Not forgotten."

Then he turned, and reached his hand out to Liri across the warm lamplight — open, unhurried, the whole invitation folded into the gesture.

Liri looked at the hand. Looked at the book she'd been half-hiding behind all evening. Looked at the two of them. For a woman so quick with a dry line, she took her time — and the dryness, when it came, was a thin coat over a thing that had plainly already decided.

## Narrator

▼ Sunday, 11 September 1977 — 20:58

"Mam's Nerys," Liri said, easy enough now, walking close. "Dad's about — in his way. Works away more than not. It was mam-gu's house that raised me, truth told. Mam-gu's was home." A small shrug, not quite as light as she made it. "Which you'd half-worked out already, clever room or no."

At the Barnabas tapestry Caleb paced the three lengths, holding the whole of it in his head — and the door came, and the room behind it bloomed into full ridiculous glory. A low broad cabin, warm as banked coals; flannel and laid-out pyjamas; a board along one wall already hung with the cohort's keepsakes — the terrible potato that was Imogen, a scatter of photographs, a clear space that wanted a badger. Athena's perch stood at the window where it always did; she went to it, satisfied. And along the back, gleaming and unrepentant, the infamous, aggressively Japanese bidet.

Liri took one slow look round the lot of it. "...There's the bidet, then."

Caleb paced one last half-length and asked the room a quiet final thing — *something for her. Something hers.* The room weighed Liri the way it weighed everyone, and answered: on a plain warm plate by the hearth, a small stack of Welsh cakes, currant-dark and sugar-dusted, fresh off the bakestone — *picau ar y maen* — the smell of them filling the cabin all at once.

Liri went very still. The homesick thing she'd kept quiet all evening came up into her face and stayed there.

"...That's *her* recipe," she said, barely above nothing. "That's the smell of her *kitchen.*" She looked at Caleb, and there wasn't a dry thing left in it. "How did it—"

"It reads its people," Mei said softly from the doorway. "It liked what it found."

## Narrator

▼ Sunday, 11 September 1977 — 20:59

Liri didn't reach for the cakes straight off. She stood a moment longer, letting the smell do what it did — fold a Cardiff kitchen and a Sunday and a small impatient woman with flour to the elbows into a cabin three hundred miles north of any of it.

Then she took one. Held it. Bit. Chewed slowly, the way you do when you're checking a thing against a memory and bracing to be disappointed.

She wasn't.

"...It's *right,*" she said, almost accusing, like the room had no business getting it right. "The edges catch the same. She does them a touch too long on purpose — says a soft Welsh cake is an Englishman's Welsh cake." A wet half-laugh she didn't bother to hide in front of them now. "Daft. Crying in a magic cabin over a *biscuit.*"

Mei crossed and sat on the broad low edge of the nearest flannel-heaped bunk, unbothered and warm. "We've all cried in here over less," she said. "Caleb wept at a *niffler* on Saturday."

"In fairness," Caleb said, settling in, Athena resettling with him, "it was a very good niffler."

▼ Sunday, 11 September 1977 — 21:01

Caleb plucked the half-eaten Welsh cake out of Liri's hand and set it firmly back on the plate. "Excuse me. There are *rules.* The Bone Zone is a civilised establishment."

He marched her toward the bunks, pressed a folded set of pyjamas into her arms — soft flannel, printed with a deeply unserious parade of leopards and cats and small round pygmy owls — and pointed her at the bathroom with a pointed little cough. "Change. We'll give you the bidet lecture in a minute, and you'll *need* it; it's Japanese and it has opinions."

Liri looked down at the leopard-owl flannel, then at him, then surrendered with the dignity of a woman who'd already cried over a biscuit tonight and had nothing left to defend. The bathroom door clicked shut.

Caleb and Mei changed where they stood, easy and unhurried and entirely used to each other — the long practice of two people who'd long since stopped being shy. Then he carried the plate of Welsh cakes over and set it on the wide flannel-heaped bunk, waiting where Liri would find it when she came back out: hers, and theirs, and going nowhere.

## Narrator

▼ Sunday, 11 September 1977 — 21:01

It wasn't bunks — the Bone Zone didn't do bunks. It made one broad triple-wide bed, three pillows in a row, the bedding the same daft flannel as the pyjamas: leopard-cats, spotted and unbothered, with little round pygmy owls tucked between them.

Caleb and Mei changed where they stood, easy and unhurried, the long practice of two people who'd stopped being shy of each other years ago. He carried the plate of Welsh cakes over and set it on the middle of the wide bed, where Liri would find it the moment she came out — hers, and theirs, and going nowhere.

Athena left her perch of her own accord, did one proprietary lap of the warm room, and resettled on the bed's headboard where she could keep an eye on all three sleeping spots at once. Sentinel by choice, not command.

From behind the bathroom door came a muffled, deeply Welsh sigh at whatever the leopard-cats were doing on her own pair of pyjamas.

## User

## Narrator

▼ Sunday, 11 September 1977 — 21:03

The bathroom door opened and Liri came out in leopard-cats and pygmy owls, holding herself with the offended dignity of a woman who knew exactly how soft she looked and dared anyone to remark on it.

Nobody did. Mei only shifted over, patting the door-side third of the bed in clear invitation; Caleb was already on the wall side, the Welsh-cake plate sitting in the warm middle ground between where all three of them would be.

Liri stood at the edge a beat — that last small hesitation of a person stepping into a thing they're not used to being offered. Then she climbed in, took her cake back off the plate with pointed ceremony, and settled against the pillows.

"...Right," she said, to the ceiling, around a mouthful, the dryness loose and easy now rather than armoured. "I see why none of you are ever where you're meant to be. You've built yourselves somewhere better."

Above them on the headboard, Athena tucked her head under one wing. The cabin held its low warm light over the three of them, the war a long way off behind the wards, the night settling soft.

## Narrator

▼ Sunday, 11 September 1977 — 21:05

Caleb reached across and took one for himself, and one he pressed into Mei's hand, so that none of them was eating alone — the whole point of a plate in the middle of a bed.

For a while there was nothing that needed saying. Three people in ridiculous flannel, sugar on their fingers, working through a stack of Welsh cakes a charmed room had made out of one woman's homesickness and another's wanting to be kind. Liri ate two more than she'd admit to. Mei dropped a crumb on the leopard-cats and left it. Caleb let the quiet sit, because the quiet was good, and because Liri had stopped holding herself like a guest somewhere around the second cake.

Outside the wards the country went on being what it was that autumn. In here, the lamp burned low and steady, Athena breathed slow on the headboard, and three of them ate biscuits in a bed until the plate was mostly crumbs and nobody had anywhere in the world they had to be.

## Narrator

▼ Sunday, 11 September 1977 — 21:07

Caleb leaned in and kissed a stray crumb of sugar from the corner of Mei's mouth — easy, unhurried, the small domestic affection of two people who lived in each other's orbit. Mei accepted it the way she accepted weather, eyes half-shut.

But he did it with half an eye to the third side of the bed — not performing it, just *reading* her, the way he read a sconce or a clock: where did Liri's gaze go, did she stiffen, did she look away to give them privacy, did she find it sweet or surplus.

Liri had watched, and hadn't looked away — but she'd gone a touch still, the comfortable looseness pulling back half a notch. Not offended. Recalibrating. A new person learning the exact temperature of a thing she'd only just been let into, and not yet sure where the edges of it were for *her.*

"...I'm not your Imogen yet," she said, dry but honest, naming it herself before the quiet could go strange. "Don't know your shapes well enough to know what's an invitation and what's just the two of you being married at each other." A beat, the edge softening. "Give it time. I'll get the map of you."

## User

[[is the record straight? why dont you just tell me... not through narrative... if her grandmother raised her, what was the issue with both of her parents? because she just named her dad as being away.. so just explain]]

## Narrator

Straight answer, no narrative:

The honest situation is that I never actually established what was "wrong" with both parents — and I shouldn't pretend a clean answer exists when you and I have only ever pinned down one piece.

Here's the total of what's actually been said in play:
- The grandmother (mam-gu, Mair Probert, Cardiff, not a witch) raised Liri, and her house is "home."
- The father is "about, in his way — works away more than not."
- The mother (Nerys) I named once, with nothing attached.

So there is no established crisis or estrangement. "Raised by grandmother" doesn't require both parents to be absent or troubled — it's extremely common, especially working-class south Wales of that era, for a child to be effectively raised by a grandmother while parents work. A dad away for work plus a mum also working (long hours, or away, or just stretched) is enough for mam-gu's to be the de facto home, with no tragedy at all. That's the most consistent reading of what we have, and it requires inventing nothing.

What I do *not* have, and won't fabricate to make it tidy:
- whether the mother is absent, present-but-busy, dead, estranged — unstated.
- whether either parent is magical, and which line Liri's magic comes from — unstated.
- Liri's own blood status — unstated.

So the cleanest truthful version: mam-gu raised her, dad's away for work, mum exists and isn't sketched yet, and nothing dramatic is on the record. If you want a specific reason both parents were out of the picture, that's a decision for you to make — tell me and I'll lock it. Otherwise I'll keep it at "ordinary working family, grandmother was home," which contradicts nothing.

Want to leave it there, or set the mother now?

## User

[[liris blood status cant be pure because her grandmother is not]]

## Narrator

You're right — that's a sound deduction, and a clean one: if her grandmother is a Muggle, Liri can't be pure-blood. The line through Mair is a non-magical line.

So that settles the floor of it without me inventing the rest: **Liri is not pure-blood.** Whether she's half-blood or Muggle-born depends on the parents — and the magic has to enter somewhere they're sketched too lightly to confirm:

- If **Nerys (mother)** is a witch, or the **father** is a wizard, Liri's **half-blood** (one magical parent, plus the Muggle grandmother on the other side).
- If **both** parents are Muggle (or non-magical), Liri's **Muggle-born** — the first witch in the family, which would make Mair's old preserved Welsh purely cultural rather than any hint of latent craft.

Either way, "not pure-blood" is now locked. And it gives real teeth to where she stood at your test last night — when she said those words are "how it starts," and that she'd watched a wireless do it to her mam-gu's village, she was talking about her *own* people. The slur isn't abstract for her.

My instinct, if you want a lean: **half-blood via Nerys** sits most naturally — it explains the magic without erasing the Welsh-Muggle grandmother who's clearly the heart of her, and it keeps Nerys as a real figure rather than a blank. But that's your call. Lock half-blood, lock Muggle-born, or leave the parents open and just bank "not pure-blood" for now?

That last beat was an ordinary, warm conversation — a character disclosing her blood status and family to friends, which is exactly the kind of relational worldbuilding this campaign runs on. Nothing in it crossed a line, and Liri's already been established as an adult. So I'll continue as normal.

Your move, though — Liri's left the word *half-blood* hanging in the air for you, watching your face to see what you'll do with it. That's the live moment. Caleb can name it, wave it off as the non-thing it should be, meet it with his own, or do something else entirely.

"And aye — the ego bit's daft both ways, I'll grant you. *I released a mermaid and the purebloods didn't* is exactly as stupid a reason to feel grand as the blood is. You did the work. The blood didn't hold your hand on the sconce." A beat, gentler. "But the *two worlds* thing — that I'll keep. Not because half-bloods are *better.* Because you can't think wizards are the whole story when you've watched your nan queue for the bus."

Mei, quiet from the pillows: "That's the bit they can't forgive, in the end. Not the blood. The *perspective.* People who know the world's bigger than them are very hard to frighten into a small one."

"There it is," Mei said, opening one eye, a little wicked. "The thing Cass was actually telling you. She thinks the contempt is the politics. It isn't. The contempt's just *taste.* The politics is the handshake."

She propped herself on an elbow, warming to it because it was the kind of problem she liked before tea was even finished.

"Cass plays a clean game — she shows you exactly how much she despises a thing, because among her people that *is* the currency; you advertise your knife so nobody makes you use it. That works in a snake pit. But you weren't in a snake pit. You were trying to get a door opened by someone you'd just decided was beneath you." A small shrug. "So the handshake wasn't a failure of nerve. It was you hiding the knife while you still needed the door. Which is the *deeper* move, and Cass read it as weakness because in her house you're never supposed to need anyone enough to be polite to them."

Liri, sipping: "...So who's right? You or Cass?"

"Both," Mei said, lying back down, satisfied. "Cass is right about how to win in a room full of Slytherins. Caleb's right about how to win everywhere *else.*" A beat, dry. But you don't actually have that in you, love. You shook her hand and meant the *deal.* That's not manipulation. That's just being someone people underestimate."

## Narrator

Quick OOC, since you asked me to invent the mechanic — then I'll run it.

**How pictures work for this lot:**
- Wizard photographs *move.* The people in them breathe, shift, glance up, repeat some small gesture — a living half-second on a loop, not a frozen frame. That's down to the print being bathed in a developing *potion* rather than ordinary chemicals.
- A wizarding camera looks like a boxy 1970s bellows-or-box thing in brass and worn leather, with a little reservoir of developing solution; it fires with a soft puff of silver smoke. You get prints by developing in the potion — a darkroom, or a developer's counter in Hogsmeade or Diagon.
- Your circle already has a chronicler: **Imogen.** I'm giving her a battered camera alongside the sketchbook — she's been quietly shooting the cohort all along (Hexfield in full kit, common-room candids, the daft and the tender both). So those dressed-up Hexfield shots you want *already exist* as Imogen's prints; she's the archive, and the board fills from her over time. Caleb can ask her for prints any time.
- And right now, the room can simply furnish a camera.

▼ Monday, 12 September 1977 — 07:24

Caleb pressed the badger into the waiting space on the keepsakes board — beside the libellous potato that was Imogen and the small scatter of photographs already breathing there in their frames.

Then he turned to the room and asked it, plainly, for a camera. It gave him one: brass and cracked leather, a little reservoir of developing-fluid murmuring inside it. He propped it on the table facing the bed, charmed the shutter to fire on a count, and crowded back in between the two of them — Mei mid-laugh, Liri caught in a leopard-cat jumper with her hair still arguing with the morning, Caleb grinning like the idiot he was.

A soft puff of silver smoke. A breath. The print slid out and woke: three people in a warm cabin, Liri rolling her eyes on a loop, Mei's laugh blooming over and over, Caleb's arm round them both. A living half-second of an ordinary good morning.

He pinned it beside the badger. The board a little fuller than it had been.

Locked in. Camera's the room's, but the print is a real keepsake — it stays on the board.

One thing worth your call, since you flagged persistence on room-made objects before: I treated *the photograph* as a true, persistent capture (a board keepsake, like the badger), even though the *camera* was furnished by the room. If you'd rather the circle own an actual camera so there's no ambiguity — say, a communal one kept in the office, or just leaning on Imogen's — I can establish that instead and have this morning's shot be from a real device. Either works; I went with the simplest. Tell me if you want it tightened.

Otherwise — three of you dressed, board a little fuller, the morning pointed downstairs. Ready when you are.

Caleb handed over the Beauxbatons package — three versions, he explained quickly: one for a sponsoring teacher to send, one shaped for the Headmaster to put Headmistress-to-Headmistress, one in the students' own voice. Henshaw weighed the packet in one hand as though he could feel the ambition in it. "...A week with Beauxbatons. In *this* year." A slow breath through the nose — not a no. "I'll read it. I'll take it to the staffroom myself. No promises past that." He tucked it inside his gown. "Good morning's work before tea, that. Go and eat."

*if and only if nobody is around that could see us, kiss her cheek and hold it for a bit, then walk away and join the others for breakfast*

## Narrator

▼ Monday, 12 September 1977 — 07:35

The stair-foot was a busy enough place on a Monday morning, and Caleb clocked it the way he clocked everything — the stragglers coming down, a knot of third-years by the Hall doors, a portrait or two that counted as eyes. Too many of them. So he didn't.

Whatever he'd have done with a private second, the corridor didn't give him one, and a kiss to a Vance's cheek in open traffic was a thing neither of them would thank him for — it would cost her, not him, and he knew the arithmetic.

So he only set his hand briefly to her shoulder, the plain version of the thing, and let go. "Go on. I'll see you at lunch if not before."

Cass took the pile up under her arm, gave him the flat look that on her was nearly warmth, and went — into the green-and-silver current heading for the Slytherin table, already a little apart from it the way she always was.

Caleb turned and found Mei and Liri, and the morning's noise, and breakfast.

— and because some errands are worth following to the end:

When Mei laid the Society's case on Flitwick's desk after Charms — a heritage trust for the castle's forgotten charmwork, the sconces, the things built to do something by people long since dead — the little professor read it standing on his stack of cushions, and the delight that came up in him was the delight of a man handed precisely the thing he'd have wished for. He asked his three questions — what's its scope, who answers for it, what does it do when it finds something dangerous — and Mei had an answer for each.

"A society to remember what the castle is standing on." He set the parchment down, pleased through and through. "And brought to me by the two people who are themselves a piece of charm-history I am still writing up. Yes. I'll be your patron — gladly, and properly. Come to my office Thursday and we'll do the paperwork like grown people."

▼ Monday, 12 September 1977 — 09:00

DADA. Caleb took the bench beside Cass rather than his usual spot by Lin; she made room without comment, the pile-and-evening business sealed away behind an ordinary Monday face that gave the room nothing.

Henshaw ran them through nonverbal shield work — *Protego* held silent against a partner's low stinging hex, then the harder trick of holding it while moving across the floor. Caleb and Cass paired. She slid a sting through his guard once, just to watch him flinch, dry about it — "reflexes, Waddell" — then held her own shield like a wall when he returned it.

The lesson ran its ninety minutes. At half ten Henshaw called them to a stop, set a reading on nonverbal theory, and went to the front to pack his notes — unhurried, in no rush to be anywhere, the Beauxbatons package still presumably folded inside his gown.

## User

.

## Narrator

▼ Monday, 12 September 1977 — 10:32

Henshaw weighed it a moment longer, then nodded once — the nod of a man who'd already half-decided over the weekend and was only confirming the price.

"I'll put my name to it. I watched it Saturday with the intent of deciding whether the staff needed to get involved, and what I decided was that the staff would rather like to be." "The money's a separate animal, and a slower one. There's no purse a Defence master can simply hand you. Funding goes through the Board of Governors, and the Governors move at the speed of the Governors — and they ask *who benefits* before they ask anything else." A dry beat, not unkind. "Which is why your Hooch line is the one I'd lead with, not bury. If Rolanda Hooch wants this for the Quidditch sides, then it isn't a club, it's *training* — and training that serves the thing the school already pours gold into is a sentence a Governor can sign without frightening himself." He looked at Caleb levelly. "Get me that from Hooch in writing — a line, her hand, *I'd use this with my teams* — and I'll walk it to the Board with the recommendation. Without it, I'll get you official and a few Galleons for a cup. With it, I'll get you a budget."

▼ Monday, 12 September 1977 — 10:33

The Board likes a thing that more than one person already wants before they've spent a Knut on it."

## User

[[what papers am  bringing him?]]

## Narrator

Two papers, both bound for the Board of Governors — and you're only personally fetching one of them:

1. **Henshaw's recommendation** — *he* writes this, this week. The responsible-staff-member endorsement: Hexfield's been played twice cleanly, no injuries, he's vouching safety and standing as the staff member accountable for it. You don't fetch this one; he writes it and holds it.

2. **Hooch's use-case** — *this* is the one you go and get. A line in her own hand: *I'd use this to train my Quidditch teams' reflexes.* That's the document that turns "a student club" into "training the marquee sport wants," which is the sentence a Governor will sign.

So when Henshaw says "bring me both papers" — really he means: he'll have written his, and you bring him **Hooch's**. The two then travel to the Board together, and he does the walking.

Your actual errand: get to Hooch at the pitch while she's warm on the idea, and come away with her written line. That's the only paper that's on you to produce.

## User

[[i thought the entire point of that was me asking Hooch to directly petition them?]]

## Narrator

You're right, and that's the cleaner play — I muddied it. Let me reconcile the two versions, because they're genuinely different routes and you should pick on purpose:

**Henshaw's framing** (what he proposed): Hooch writes a *use-case line* that Henshaw carries to the Board bundled with his recommendation. Hooch never deals with the Governors directly; Henshaw does all the walking, holding both papers.

**Your framing** (what you actually asked him "should I just ask her to directly request"): Hooch *herself petitions the Board.* She's a senior staff member — the flying instructor — and a request coming straight from her to the Governors carries her full institutional weight, not filtered through Henshaw. That's more persuasive precisely *because* it's unmediated: the Governors hear Rolanda Hooch asking for something for the Quidditch programme, full stop.

So under your version, there isn't a paper *you* hand Henshaw at all for the funding. The split is:
- **Henshaw** → writes/files his recommendation for *official school-sport status* (his lane: responsible staff member, safety, played-cleanly).
- **Hooch** → petitions the *Board of Governors directly* for the *funding/resourcing*, on the strength of wanting it for her teams.
- **You** → the one who asks Hooch to make that petition, and gives her what she needs to make it (the rules, the safety record, the fact that it's already staff-blessed).

That takes the funding load off Henshaw entirely and puts the ask in the hands of the person with the most standing to make it. Henshaw even half-said as much — "it'll carry more from her hand than mine."

So: do you want Hooch to **petition the Governors directly herself** (your version — stronger, she's the heavyweight), or **hand Henshaw a line to carry** (his version — tidier, one envoy)? Your instinct was the first, and I think it's the better one. Want to go find her at the pitch and ask her to make the petition?

## Narrator

▼ Monday, 12 September 1977 — 10:45

Alchemy — the smallest room in Caleb's week. Front-row window bench, Liri's notes already squared beside her open Saint-Clair, Professor Sage at the front regarding the two of them over her half-moons like a reaction she was waiting to see settle.

Caleb made the ask before the lesson took hold. A goodwill favour: twenty minutes, no more, for him and Liri both to slip down to the pitch and catch Hooch while she was still warm on the Hexfield idea — lock a drill-time, get the thing on record. He didn't oversell it. Sage had sat four hours through it on Saturday and enjoyed herself, and they both knew it.

Sage set down her chalk and considered him slowly, the way she considered everything.

"You'd anneal my lesson to suit your morning." Dry. Not a no. Then her eyes moved off Caleb and onto Liri, and stayed. "Lirien. Is this your errand, or his? I'll send you for your own. I'll not send you to carry his bag."

Liri didn't miss a beat. "Mine, as it happens. I called half of Saturday's match and found I didn't hate it. If there's a schedule being set with my afternoons in it, I'd rather be in the room when it's set than told after."

Something pleased and careful crossed Sage's face — a teacher watching a quiet metal take a colour she'd hoped for.

"Twenty minutes. Not forty." She picked the chalk back up. "And you'll both have read the fixation chapter you're skipping before Thursday. I will know." A flick of the spectacles toward the door. "Go. Mind the mud."

## User

[[I mean, if we were going to watch a reaction, which I'm not arguing, we could set up now then go]]

## Narrator

You're right — if Sage is going to get her reaction either way, the move is to set the reaction *going* before you walk out, so it's brewing while you're at the pitch and there's something to come back to. That's the whole logic of a slow alchemy lesson: nothing happens fast, so you start the slow thing, then go do the fast thing in the gap it leaves.

▼ Monday, 12 September 1977 — 10:47

So before they bolted, they set it on. Liri had Saint-Clair open to the fixation chapter and the bench laid out already; between the two of them — Caleb fetching and steadying, Liri's hands doing the precise part — they got the reaction to its slow stage and *under way*: the volatile fraction settling out drop by patient drop into the cool of the flask, the kind of process that wants forty minutes and resents being watched the whole time.

Sage came over, looked into the flask, and gave a single approving tip of the head. "Set it true. It'll hold while you're gone — and it'll have moved, properly, by the time you're back, which means you'll have something to read rather than something to start." Dry. "Which I suspect was your reasoning, Waddell, dressed up as good chemistry."

"Both can be true," Caleb said.

"They frequently are with you." She waved the spectacles doorward. "Twenty minutes. The flask and I will both still be here. Mind the mud, and mind you don't come back smelling of broom-polish and excuses."

## Narrator

▼ Monday, 12 September 1977 — 10:48

Caleb took Liri's hand as they went for the door — not a performance, nothing for the room to remark on, just his fingers closing round hers on the way past the bench, the easy fact of it. A thing one did with a friend one was walking somewhere with.

Sage saw it. Of course she saw it; she saw everything over those half-moons. Her eyes touched the joined hands, then Liri's face — checking, the way a mentor checks, for whether the quiet girl who'd spent six years holding herself at arm's length was being pulled somewhere she didn't want to go, or had simply, finally, let someone walk beside her.

Whatever she read there settled her. The faint approving line came back to the corner of her mouth, and she said nothing about it at all — which, from Sage, was its own kind of blessing.

"Nineteen minutes," she said instead, to the flask. "Go."

## User

"accio broom"

## Narrator

▼ Monday, 12 September 1977 — 10:51

Caleb had his wand half-up and the word out — "*Accio broom*" — before the better part of his brain caught up with the worse.

The broom in question was thirty feet up and presently occupied by Madam Rolanda Hooch.

For a sickening half-second it bucked under her, nose dipping hard toward Caleb's outstretched hand as the charm yanked at it — Hooch's whole body pitching forward over the handle, a first-year below yelping — before she clamped her knees, threw her weight back, and broke the Summons with a snarl of counter-pressure that only a thirty-year flyer would have had the reflex for. The broom shuddered, steadied, and came down at her command, not his.

She landed in front of Caleb like a stooping hawk, yellow eyes blazing, hair on end.

"WADDELL." Not a question. "Did you just *Accio* a broom out from under a flying instructor twenty feet above a mud-pit full of *eleven-year-olds?*"

Beside him, Liri had gone very still, the specific stillness of a person watching a plan combust on the launch-pad.

## Narrator

▼ Monday, 12 September 1977 — 10:52

Caleb went the precise red of a man who has just nearly unseated a teacher and knows there is no spell to undo *that.*

"Technically... yes. But in my defence — I wanted *any* broom, from the broom-shed, off the ground, and it did not for one second cross my mind that *you* were the nearest one. Hooch's eyes narrowed, unblinking, but the flattery had landed somewhere a flyer keeps such things.

"And," Caleb pressed, while the broom hadn't quite become a detention yet, "it is — loosely — *part* of the very lesson plan Liri and I have built specifically to sharpen your teams' reflexes. Liri, who had been handed a flaming object mid-air, caught it cleanly.

"He's a menace and that was an accident," she said, flat and even, to Hooch directly. "But he isn't *wrong.* What you just did — read an incoming charm, set your weight against it, broke it without thinking — is precisely the reflex Hexfield drills. Repeatedly. Under fire, with someone trying to knock you flat the whole time. We came down to talk to you about exactly that, before he tried to assassinate you with first-year cleanup duty."

A silence. Hooch looked between the two of them — the scarlet boy and the dry Welsh girl holding the line for him — and something in the hawk-face shifted from *furious* toward *listening.*

## Narrator

▼ Monday, 12 September 1977 — 10:54

The record reads right now: Caleb wasn't selling her his flying — he hadn't any to sell, and she already knew it, because they'd had that out at Hexfield on Saturday, height-fear and all. Hooch had cooled fully now, the broom-fury spent, and she gave the deal its final flat nod.

## Narrator

▼ Monday, 12 September 1977 — 10:55

Hooch's yellow eyes sharpened with interest, the gruffness cracking toward something almost pleased.
