"...Go on, then," she said, low and interested. "I've got you, Waddell. You'll not hit the grass harder than a dropped scarf. On my word as the one who teaches people not to die up there."

Liri looked at him for one long, savouring beat — a woman being handed a wand, a willing victim, and an audience of eleven-year-olds all at once. Something deeply, dryly delighted settled over her face.

"...Oh, I'm going to enjoy this far more than is decent." She drew her wand, levelled it at the broom lying in the mud at the pitch's edge, and gave him the smallest, wickedest smile he'd seen on her yet. "Don't lock your knees. *Accio broom.*"

The broom leapt off the wet grass and smacked into his palm.

## Narrator

▼ Monday, 12 September 1977 — 10:57

"I am absolutely terrified of heights," Caleb told the mud-spattered first-years, plainly, like a fact about the weather. "I'll send Professor McGonagall crude little drawings at breakfast confessing to crimes I've committed in her own common room, and never so much as flinch — but get me above one storey, and I have panic attacks looking down."

The kids stared. He had their whole attention now, which is what honesty buys you.

"Here's the thing. Some of you will be brilliant flyers. Maybe a champion or two in this lot. And some of you will be *shite* at it, and you'll feel stupid and embarrassed every lesson." A beat. "This isn't a *face your fears* speech. It isn't a *get back up and keep trying* one either — those have their uses, but that's not what I've got for you. What I've got, I learned the hard way."

And then — wandless, his wand safe in Liri's hand — he swung a leg over the school broom, set his jaw, and kicked off.

His stomach dropped through the floor of the world. The grass fell away sickeningly fast; his hands locked white on the handle; the wind found him and the whole green pitch tilted far, far below. Heart slamming, breath gone shallow and ragged, every cell screaming *down, down, get down* — he climbed anyway, to fifty feet, and made himself circle there, slow, the panic arriving right on schedule.

Waiting for it.

## Narrator

▼ Monday, 12 September 1977 — 10:58

Down on the grass, Liri took her time about it — Caleb's wand in one hand, her own raised in the other, Athena a smug warm weight at her ear. She had a clear shot at the man hanging white-knuckled in the air above his own life lesson, and she was not about to waste the moment she'd been *gifted.*

"For the record," she called up, pitched so the first-years caught every word, "he handed me his wand and *asked* for this. Remember that the next time he tells you he's brave." The wicked little line settled deep. "Don't lock your knees, Professor Waddell."

"*Accio broom.*"

The broom answered her wand and lurched — not down, but *toward* her, dragging Caleb into a sudden graceless swoop across the pitch, legs swinging, dignity entirely gone, a yelp tearing out of him that no Associate Junior Professor should ever make in front of his students. Hooch's wand tracked him the whole way, *Arresto Momentum* feathering the speed so the indignity stayed survivable — fast and humiliating, never dangerous.

The first-years, to a child, burst out laughing.

Then Hooch's wand snapped up. "*Arresto Momentum.*"

The fall caught — gentled — Caleb's plummet slowing in the last ten feet to the speed of a dropped scarf, so he settled into the wet grass on his backside with a soft graceless *whump* instead of a crack. Unhurt. Soaked. Entirely without dignity.

The first-years shrieked with laughter.

*I go focus up and make sure we know what is going on and work with her*

## Narrator

▼ Monday, 12 September 1977 — 11:04

Caleb pulled himself out of the corridor entirely and into the work — the gear-shift he was good at, the clockmaker's focus dropping over the rest of it. He drew the flask between them and let Liri lead, because the precise read was hers; his was the lateral one.

"It's fixed," Liri said, turning the glass to the light. "The volatile fraction came out of suspension as it cooled — no heat driving it to escape, so it settled and held instead of flashing off. What was unstable an hour ago is stable now. *Fixed.* That's the whole argument of the chapter we skipped: you don't force a volatile thing to behave, you take the heat away and let it choose to settle."

Caleb turned that over and found his angle. "Same shape as your tempering colours," he said to Sage. "You don't *make* the steel hold — you bring it through the heat and stop at the right colour, and it holds itself. This is that, in a flask. Stop at the right cold."

Sage's half-moons came up. For a moment she said nothing — and then the faint, sparing line touched the corner of her mouth.

"...He listens," she said, to no one, the way she'd said it once before. "Most don't." She set her chalk down. "You've earned your twenty minutes. Both of you. Write it up."

## User

*write a stellar lab report*

## Narrator

▼ Monday, 12 September 1977 — 11:06

Caleb pulled a clean sheet from his satchel, uncapped the ink, and wrote — the clockmaker's care turned to a page now, Liri feeding him the precise numbers and corrections over his shoulder, the two registers of them braided into one document. He took his time. A thing worth doing badly off a broom was worth doing *well* on parchment.

What he handed up to Sage read:

**A Study in Fixation: The Settling of a Volatile Fraction by Controlled Cooling**
*NEWT Alchemy — Prof. V. Sage*
*C. Waddell & L. Penrose — 12 September 1977*

**Aim.** To show that a volatile fraction held in unstable suspension can be brought to a fixed, stable state not by force or added reagent, but by the controlled *withdrawal* of heat — and to relate this to the wider principle of fixation (Saint-Clair) and to its bearing on shelf-stable preparations.

**Hypothesis (Penrose).** A volatile fraction escapes suspension because heat lends it the energy to do so. Remove the heat slowly and the fraction is not driven off but permitted to settle; the unstable becomes stable of its own accord. Fixation is therefore an act of *subtraction,* not addition.

**Method.** The preparation was brought to its volatile stage, then set to a slow, undisturbed cool in still air, the flask left untouched for the interval.

**Observations.** At t = 0: suspension cloudy and active, volatile fraction visibly mobile. Left 41 minutes. On return: the liquid had cleared and quieted; the fraction had come out of suspension and settled. *No condensate ring at the neck* — the fraction was retained, not boiled off.

**Results.** The fraction was fixed: stable, held, no longer mobile. The absence of neck-condensate is the load-bearing detail — it settled, it did not flash away. Hypothesis confirmed.

**Discussion.** This is the spine of Saint-Clair's *Principia Fixationis*: one does not *master* a volatile thing by forcing it, but by withdrawing the conditions of its volatility and letting it choose stability. The result maps onto Prof. Sage's metallurgical frame exactly — as steel brought through heat blooms through its tempering colours and must be *stopped at the right colour* to hold its temper, so the system must be brought down and *stopped at the right cold.* Too fast a quench shocks the fraction out of true; too slow wastes the window. The art is the stopping.

**Application.** This bears directly on shelf-stability for concentrated preparations such as the Wiggenweld julep, where a trans-stable compound must be fixed for storage. It also clarifies why the ethanol carrier must be *anhydrous:* residual water opens a back-reaction — a road by which the fixed fraction may un-fix. Fixation by cooling is necessary but not sufficient; the medium must offer the settled fraction no way back.

**Conclusion.** Fixation achieved by controlled cooling; fraction retained and stable; hypothesis confirmed. **Recommended next step:** accelerated-degradation testing — samples held at ambient / body / warm-body temperature, assayed against control at 1, 4, and 12 weeks — to establish whether the fixed state holds over time, not merely over an afternoon.

Sage read it through once, slowly, over the half-moons. Then a second time. She set it down with the particular care she gave a thing she had not expected to be this good.

"...The neck-condensate observation is what makes it. Most would have written *it settled* and stopped; you wrote *why we know it settled rather than escaped.*" She looked between the two of them. "And you've put the back-reaction caveat in the application without my prompting — necessary but not sufficient. That's a scholar's sentence." A pause; the rare line. "Outstanding. Both of you. *This* is what the twenty minutes bought. I'll be keeping a copy."

▼ Monday, 12 September 1977 — 13:25

The question you set was *what does light alone do to a root in water.* The moment you start cleaning the algae off the bare one and not the dark one, the two glasses aren't a pair anymore — you've changed one and not the other, and any difference between them might be your charm, not the light. Mei picked it up, mild. "And if you cleaned *both* the same way, daily, you'd just be adding a third thing you're doing to both — more interference to log. Either way the clean pair stops being clean."

"The algae *is* the experiment now," Sprout said. "It turned up uninvited, the way the real answer usually does. Let it run. If the bare one greens over and chokes, that's a finding — *light breeds slime breeds death.* If it fights the slime off itself, that's a bigger one. You don't tidy the result away because it got inconvenient." A dry tap on the glass. "Save the algaecide for when you're *growing* merdrakes for gold. Not for when you're learning what they do."

Muffs on, Caleb lifted one of the very youngest seedlings — barely a bean with a face — and carried it to the far end of the bench, away from the class, where its silent thrash and unheard cry troubled no one. He twined it by the crown, lowered its root into the third silenced glass, and it stilled the instant the water took it. Bare to the light. To be kept clean.

Three glasses on the bench now — dark, fouled, and kept-clean — and a clean three-way question nobody's asked of a mandrake before. Sprout's pleased, Mei's logging, and you've a standing daily-ish errand (Evanesce the slime off #3) plus the dark glass waiting on its first evening lift.

Herbology runs till 2:45, then your afternoon's free. Where to from here — finish the lesson out, or somewhere next?

"hey, if we evanesce the water and the root and leaves of all dirt, we will likely buy a much better chunk of time now before algae migrates its way in"

## Narrator

▼ Monday, 12 September 1977 — 13:30

"If we Evanesce the dirt off the water *and* the root *and* the leaves," Caleb said, turning the third glass in the light, "we strip out the spores that rode in on the soil in the first place. Algae'd have to find its own way in from the air after that — slower. Buys the clean glass a much better run before it fouls."

Mei considered it and nodded — then put a fence around it. "Good. But *only* this one. You touch the bare-fouled glass now, you've changed a control after it started — the pair's ruined. Number three gets the scrubbed start. One and two stay exactly as they were."

"Aye, that's sound for the clean arm," Sprout agreed, watching him work. "A truly clean start does delay the green — the slime mostly comes *up with the dirt,* not down from the ceiling. Just mind your Vanishing's *precise* — take the soil off the root, not the skin off it. It's a live thing, young, and you're cleaning it, not peeling it." A beat. "And note in your log that three started *scrubbed,* not just *kept* clean. It's a fair difference. Number three's whole job is *light with no organism* — so the cleaner it begins, the truer it answers."

Caleb worked the charm fine and careful — the faint soil-haze lifting off root, leaves, and water in a clean shiver, leaving the seedling suspended pale and bare in genuinely clear water.

Three glasses, clean question, and #3 now starting from genuinely clear water — the kind of careful that earns Sprout's respect and Mei's logging both. Herbology runs till 2:45; your afternoon's free after.

Where to next?

## Narrator

▼ Monday, 12 September 1977 — 15:02

After Herbology, Caleb and Mei went up through the castle to McGonagall's office — the Salve of Memory jar tucked in Caleb's satchel where he could reach it. The corridor on the first floor was quiet in the afternoon lull, her door shut but not the shut of an empty room; a thin line of light showed beneath it, and the dry scratch of a quill carried faintly through.

Caleb knocked.

A pause, the quill stopping. Then: "Come."

The office was orderly and warm — tartan, a banked fire, stacks of parchment squared at the desk's edge, a tin of biscuits she'd never admit to. McGonagall sat behind it, spectacles down her nose, and looked up at the pair of them over the rims with an expression that said she had, in fact, been half-waiting for exactly this since breakfast.

"Mr Waddell. Miss Chang." She set her quill in its stand. "I received a *drawing* this morning." A beat, perfectly level. "Sit down. I think you and I have a conversation owed between us — and I'd as soon have it in words this time, with both of you here."

## Narrator

▼ Monday, 12 September 1977 — 15:04

Caleb laid it out plainly, Mei filling the precise gaps. They had woken the shrine — the recovered name spoken true, the pitch tuned through cold lake-water — and Merphila had answered. She was no carving with a charm on her. She was a face of Gwenllian herself: a portion of the hedgewitch's soul, bound into the glass by her son, Hywel Bevan, the glazier who built the room in 1672 because he could not let his mother go. The rest of what he kept lay sealed in a glass flask beneath the deep-end tile. And Gwenllian's own craft, in life, had been the opposite of her son's keeping — the old Welsh work of laying the unresting dead to *rest.*

McGonagall did not interrupt once. When he finished, she sat quiet a long moment, and when she spoke the briskness had gone careful.

"I pulled the building-rolls. Sixteen seventy-one to seventy-two." She drew a folded paper from a drawer and laid it between them. "*Hywel Bevan, master-glazier* — paid for the prefects' bathing-room and 'a small adjoining work,' by his own hand, no apprentice named. I had the *who.*" She set the paper down. "You've brought me the *why,* and it is a great deal heavier." She took her spectacles off entirely — a thing Caleb had never seen her do. "A soul kept from rest. Bound in glass, sealed under a stone, against the whole grain of the craft she herself practised. That is not a curio, Mr Waddell. It is one of the gravest things this castle has been quietly standing on, and you found the bottom of it." A measured beat. "Tell me you did not try to *move* anything."

▼ Monday, 12 September 1977 — 15:06

Caleb told her exactly what he'd done. He'd filled the pool cold, Liri had spoken the name in true Welsh, he'd held the thirteenth chime, and the shrine had woken. And then — he'd pressed the sealed deep-end tile, and seen the flask, and understood what it was. He had not taken it. He had not moved it. He'd looked at the kept soul of a woman three hundred years dead, and he'd put the stone back over her, and left her where her son had laid her.

"I didn't touch the flask," he said. "I sealed it again. It wasn't mine to decide."

McGonagall's jaw eased, fractionally.

"And there's a thing she carries that I can't stop thinking about," Caleb went on, quieter. "Her craft is dying with her. An oral tradition — never written, never taught past a kitchen — and she knows it. So I told her what I'd want, if she wanted it: that she let me *record* all of it. Every scrap of what she knows, set down so it doesn't die when she does. She offered to teach me some of it herself — gladly, slowly, not today. But more than that — if, once it's saved, she wishes to *go* — to finally be let to the rest she spent her whole life giving other people — then I'd help her do that too." He hesitated, then said the rest of it plainly. "And I think that last part may be exactly what one of the recipes I'm chasing is *for.*"

McGonagall was very still. When she spoke, it was without any of the briskness at all.

"...You're speaking of helping a soul to *die,* Mr Waddell. Properly. On purpose." Not an accusation — a weighing. "Be very certain you understand the difference between a mercy and a thing that only wears mercy's face. Tell me about this recipe."

"I joke a lot," Caleb said, "but this isn't light to me — and that's exactly why I won't decide it *for* her. We've confirmed it *activates* — from a hair and a name. But we've no one with a damaged memory to test it on, so what it actually *does* is still open. The obvious use is restoring what's been Obliviated. Mei reads it narrowly — undoing a Memory Charm. I read it wider." A small glance at Mei, the old disagreement between them, easy. "I think it might answer to *any* damage to a memory. A destructive event. Perhaps physical injury. Perhaps — a single memory that's been *taken,* cleanly, by someone who knew how."

McGonagall looked at the two sheets on her desk for a long moment without touching either. Then, slowly, she drew the Salve recipe toward her with one finger.

"...The difference between those two readings, Mr Waddell, is the difference between a Healer's tool and a *spy's.*" She said it quietly, not unkindly, her eyes still on the parchment. "And you've laid both on a Deputy Headmistress's desk in the middle of a war. I want you to understand what you've just handed me before I say another word about helping you make it."

Caleb set a small jar of the salve on the desk beside the recipe and slid it toward her.

"Take this. Without the hair and the name, it's basically chapstick — I gave a batch to the house-elves for their working hands and feet, in trade for the beeswax. It's harmless inert until it's keyed to a person. So that part you can hold without worry."

McGonagall picked the jar up, unscrewed it, sniffed — lavender, dittany, beeswax — and set it down, a faint dry approval at the elves' end of it.

"Before we move on, though," Caleb said, "I need to know which kind of *agreement* this is. Is it a *yes, and we'll sit down later and work it through* — or a *yes, and we should talk now about the part neither of us has solved?*" He spread his hands. "Because the conditions are right. A hurt or damaged person. A Healer present. Consent, a witness, a backup phial. But that's not *here.* Hogwarts hasn't got an Obliviated patient in a bed waiting for us. McGonagall turned the jar slowly on the blotter, considering, and when she answered the wariness had shifted into something more like collaboration.

"...Later, for the *whole* of it. But not nothing, now." She set the jar down. "Two names occur to me, and I'll not say either aloud until I've thought longer — one a Healer at St Mungo's I'd trust with my own memory, and one a *patient* who has been on the long-term spell-damage ward for years with no one able to reach him." Her mouth thinned. "But you've put your finger on the true danger, and it isn't the spy's edge. It's that the moment your salve works *once,* in front of *anyone,* the fact that Obliviation can be *undone* stops being yours to control. Ministry, Healers, the people who do the Obliviating for a living — and the people who'd very much prefer their erasures stay erased." She looked between them. "That is a thing to plan in the dark and reveal on your own terms, or not at all. We will give it the long conversation it needs. Not squeezed into the end of an afternoon."

▼ Monday, 12 September 1977 — 15:15

McGonagall caught the correction and inclined her head, conceding the point — he'd been asking *can it,* not *will you let me right now.*

"Ah — the *possibility.* That's a fair question and a different one, and I'll answer it as far as I can." She set the jar aside. "In principle: yes. Memory-magic is *granular* — a skilled Legilimens doesn't have to take a life, they can lift a single afternoon and leave the rest whole. That's the whole horror of it done well; it's a scalpel, not a hammer. So if your Salve answers to a *taken* memory at all, there's no reason in the theory it couldn't answer to *one* — restore a single excised afternoon rather than a whole erased decade." A measured beat. "Which, frankly, makes it the *cleaner* test you said it was. One memory taken by consent, one memory restored, the subject whole on either side — far less to go wrong than reaching into a years-old Obliviation. You're right about that."

She let it sit, then added the edge, because she was McGonagall:

"But 'the theory permits it' and 'we have done it safely' are continents apart, and the bridge between them is still the Healer and the phial. The *possibility* is real, Mr Waddell. The *attempt* keeps its conditions." A dry flick. "You'll find I can hold both of those in my head at once."

"You tested it on yourself." McGonagall said it flatly — not surprised, exactly. "Of course you did. Then the *idea* reached her — and Caleb watched it land, properly, the way nothing else in the conversation had.

She did not reach for the jar. Her hand, in fact, went still on the blotter, deliberately, *away* from it.

"...You've just described the most frightening thing I've heard in this office in some years, and you did it in the cheerful voice you use for everything." Quiet now. "It isn't a Healer's tool. It isn't a spy's. It's a *mirror* — a thing that tells a person whether their own mind is whole. And in a war run by people who erase for a living, that means *anyone* — you, me, the Minister, the man Obliviated last Tuesday and sent home smiling — might be carrying a hole they cannot feel the edges of." She looked at the small inert jar for a long moment. "I am old enough to have years I cannot fully account for, Mr Waddell. And I find I would *very* much rather not learn, this afternoon, which of those are ordinary forgetting and which are not." She drew her hand back fully. "No. Not on a whim. Not even mine."

That one'll sit with her. You've turned a memory-restorative into a mirror nobody's sure they want to look into — and watched the Deputy Headmistress decline to look. She's pulled her hand back from the jar and the room's gone quiet around the weight of it.

Still on the desk between you, untouched: the Three-Sleeps recipe she keeps trying to steer you toward, the asphodel Sprout's holding on her word, and the three Restricted-Section books Pince flagged. Where do you want to take her next — let her have the sleeping-potion conversation, or move to the asphodel and the books?

"I will bring this to a *very* small number of people — and I will choose them the way you'd want me to, which is to say *carefully,* and not by rank. When it is tested, and proven, and safe. "You came in here with a drawing of a mermaid, Mr Waddell, and you've handed me a thing that may need to be put to half this castle's senior staff. I should like a moment before we discuss a *third* world-altering object on my desk." She almost smiled. "But go on. The sleeping one. I haven't forgotten I asked."

She drew a sheet over and wrote two lines on it. "The asphodel: you have a *standing* yes. I'll tell Pomona it's released to you when you ask — no need to come back through me for the root itself. The *finished* potion we plan properly, with supervision, when you've decided it's time. And you'll speak to Gwenllian first. That's all sensible. Done." She set the quill down. "That one was easy."

Then Caleb told her the rest of it — that the herbal had come from *Caractacus Burke,* at Borgin and Burkes, who'd sat on it for years and slid it where Caleb would find it; that Burke had dangled more, including one thing he'd called *too dangerous* and told Caleb to *wait* for; that this was, in Burke's own framing, the *safe* one.

McGonagall went still in a wholly different way than she had for the soul or the salve. This was not awe. This was a Head of House hearing a name she did not like.

"...Caractacus Burke *gave a student a reason to come back to Knockturn Alley.*" Flat, cold, precise. "He sat on that book for nine years and then made certain *you* walked out with it, and now he's holding something *worse* over you like a treat above a dog. Mr Waddell — that is not a shopkeeper being generous. That is a man in the darkest trade in Britain choosing himself an instrument, and *liking* what he picked." Her eyes were hard. "Tell me you have not agreed to go back."

She's locked onto Burke now — the one thing in this whole conversation she's treated as a *threat* rather than a wonder. The asphodel's settled (standing yes, Gwenllian first), the occlumency research is on the table waiting its turn, and she's holding you to a hard question: *have you agreed to go back to Knockturn?*

How does Caleb answer her?

McGonagall was quiet a long moment. The hardness didn't leave her face, but something behind it shifted — recognition, and not the comfortable kind.

"...That," she said slowly, "is the exact reasoning by which good people walk into Knockturn Alley and do not walk out the same. And it is *also* not wrong." She laced her fingers. "You've named the real trap, Mr Waddell — not the dangerous object. The dangerous *question.* 'Better in my hands than left for the next fool.' Every artefact Burke has ever sold was bought by someone telling themselves that sentence." A pause. "But you're honest enough to say you're on the fence rather than pretend you've already decided the noble thing — and that honesty is the only reason I'm going to *help* you think it through rather than simply forbidding it, which we both know would only make you clever about going around me."

She unlaced her hands.

"So we don't decide it today. We don't decide it *alone,* and we certainly don't decide it because a man in Knockturn Alley set the clock. Here is my one condition while it sits on your fence: you do not go back to that shop without telling me first. Not for permission — for a *witness.* Agreed?"

▼ Monday, 12 September 1977 — 15:30

Caleb laid it out, and Mei carried the precise half, the two of them braided the way they always were.

"Here's the case. Legilimency and Occlumency are the magic of one mind reaching into another — taught as intrusion, a thing done *to* someone. But that's not the only shape it comes in." He turned his hand in Mei's. "I'm in two bonds nobody's been positioned to study from the *inside.* The one with Mei — a true pair-bond, the kind Flitwick's filling notebooks over. And the one with Athena — a familiar bond. Both of them are, near as I can tell, a *consented, two-way* version of the exact thing Legilimency does by force. Intent crosses. Feeling crosses. Not words — but the door's open, both ways, and nobody made anybody do it."

"Which means we're a control no textbook has," Mei put in, even and clear. "Every account of Legilimency is written from intrusion — attacker and defender. We're the same magic with the force taken out and the consent left in. If you want to understand what mind-to-mind contact *is* — stripped of the violation — you'd study a bond like ours, not a duel. Pince agreed it's a real question. She showed Caleb the shelf and said as much."

"And I'd wager," Caleb finished, "there have been pair-bonds, and there have been Legilimens, but very few people ever sat in *both* chairs at once and thought to ask whether they're the same chair. I'm in a daft, lucky position to ask it. I'd like leave to read the framing — not the *how-to-break-in* methods. The theory. Crupp's second chapter, the bond literature, the one on bonds-as-affliction. With you knowing exactly what I'm reading and why."

McGonagall was quiet, fingers steepled, weighing it the way she weighed everything — and this time the weighing took longer.

## Narrator

▼ Monday, 12 September 1977 — 15:41

Caleb had the wand half-lifted and the word half-out — *"Accio restricted section—"* — before he caught himself, but the charm had already gone looking.

It found the iron grille, pulled once at the great lock that held it... and did precisely nothing. The lock sat in its housing, indifferent. Restricted-Section grilles had been warded against exactly that kind of cleverness since roughly the first week of the first term of the school's existence; a Summoning Charm slid off it like rain off slate.

What it did *not* slide off was Madam Pince, who had risen from somewhere among the stacks like a heron coming up out of reeds, and was now regarding Caleb across the quiet with the expression of a woman who had heard a wand move in *her* library.

"...Mr Waddell." Very soft. Very dangerous. "Did you just attempt to *Summon the lock off my Restricted Section* — having, not three hours past, been granted *written, conditional, page-specific leave* to enter it like a civilised person?" She held out one hand, palm up, for the note. "Give me Minerva's letter. Let us both pretend the last four seconds did not happen, before I revise my generally improving opinion of you."

"Mei's read the Brigham cover to cover," Caleb told Pince, with the particular pride he reserved for Mei's mind. "First thing she did after we got Brigham's office — straight to *On Bound Cognition,* the whole of it."

Mei didn't look up from where she'd already drawn Mostyn toward herself. "It's where half my half of the bond-argument came from," she said, even. "Helena Brigham wrote the *consent* of it that Mostyn couldn't see — the Hearth, the chosen door. He had the phenomenon and called it a leak; she had it a century later and called it a marriage." She turned a page of the affliction-text, reading Mostyn now against the Brigham already in her head. "Which means I can read *this* one properly — I know what he got wrong before I start."

Pince paused at that, and gave Mei a look she did not often spend on students — the recognition of one serious reader by another.

"...Then you'll get more out of Mostyn in an afternoon than most get in a term," she said. "Read him knowing he was *wrong,* Miss Chang, and he becomes useful. Read him believing him, and he becomes a cage." A small nod, and she withdrew toward her desk, leaving them to it — Crupp at Caleb's hand, Mostyn at Mei's, the Brigham already living behind Mei's eyes.

*I want to reach chapter two and eventually peek just for the title of chapter 1*

## Narrator

▼ Monday, 12 September 1977 — 16:08

Caleb read his way down to the start of chapter two — Crupp's framing chapter, the *what is it* before the *how* — and he stayed there, the way McGonagall and Pince had both bound him to, reading the nature of the thing rather than the doing of it. It was dry, careful, eighteenth-century prose, and underneath the caution it described exactly the door he'd been trying to name: one mind at the threshold of another, and what passes across when it's let.

But the eye does what the eye does, and the leaf before chapter two was the tail of chapter one, and the chapter heading sat right there at the top of the recto where he'd have to work *not* to see it.

He peeked. Just the title. Just the words at the head of the first chapter, before the leave said *stop.*

Chapter the First was titled: **"Of the Door That Is Always Ajar — That No Mind Is Wholly Closed, and Why the Untrained Believe Otherwise."**

He read it once, and then McGonagall's condition and Pince's sightline both landed on him at once, and he turned his eye firmly back down to chapter two where it belonged. But the title sat in him now — the founding claim of the whole restricted art, the thing Crupp put *before* method, before defence, before anything: *no mind is wholly closed.* It recoloured everything below it. Occlumency, then, wasn't building a wall where there'd been none. It was learning to close a door that, in everyone, stands a little open already.

Across the table, Mei turned a page of Mostyn and did not notice he'd gone still.

The title settled in Caleb — *no mind is wholly closed* — and across the table Mei felt it land before she knew what it was. Not the words; the *weight* of them. A small snag in the shape of him, the particular stillness that meant something had just gone in deep and was sitting there.

## User

"both.. I think it is more of a... people are outwardly like that too, right? like some are more guarded but they can have physical tells that betray them.. this is almost like a spiritual version of that."

## Narrator

▼ Monday, 12 September 1977 — 16:10

"Both," Caleb said. "But I think it's more — people are like that *outwardly* too, aren't they. Some are more guarded than others, but even the guarded ones have tells. A tightness round the eyes. A hand that goes still. A pause a half-second too long before they answer. Nobody's a closed box — you just have to know how to read the leaks." He turned his hand palm-up on the table, the one nearest her. "This is almost the *spiritual* version of that. Crupp's saying the door's ajar in everyone, always, the same way the tells are there in everyone, always. Occlumency isn't building a wall where there was none. It's learning to school the spiritual face the way a good liar schools the physical one." A small, wry breath. "Which means the people who think their minds are *shut* are just the ones who've never noticed their own tells."

Mei considered that, and the ironic tilt softened into something truer.

"...That's less frightening than where you started, and I think more correct," she said. "It's not a door *some* people have and some don't. It's a face. Everyone has a face; everyone leaks; most never learn they're doing it." She laid her hand over his upturned one, unhurried, there in the quiet of the third table. "And you and I just *agreed* to read each other's. Which is the part Mostyn never understood — he kept calling the open door a wound. It isn't. It's letting someone watch your face on purpose." A beat, dry again, warm under it. "Yours is a very loud face, for the record. I felt that title from across a table."

## Narrator

You're right, and this isn't a consent beat — it's a worldbuilding question about what the bond actually *is*. Caleb's poking at the mechanism: if "two people in love who agree to it" were the gate, the bond would be common. It plainly isn't — they're one pair in a century, the thing Flitwick fills notebooks over. So agreement-plus-love can't be the whole recipe. Mei, the precise one, would chase that down.

▼ Monday, 12 September 1977 — 16:11

"...No. We're not that unique as a *couple,*" Mei said slowly, setting it out the way she set out a proof. "That's the thing that's been bothering you, and you're right to be bothered. There are thousands of couples who love each other and would say yes to anything — and almost none of them are *this.* So loving each other and agreeing to it can't be the gate. If it were, the bond wouldn't be a thing Flitwick fills notebooks over. It'd be in every other marriage in Britain."

She turned her hand under his, thinking aloud, frowning at the join of them like it was a line of working she hadn't balanced yet.

"So we said *agreed,* and that's true, but it's lazy-true. We didn't sit down one afternoon and *consent* like signing a form — if that worked, everyone would've done it." A pause. "Something else happened. Something the *wanting* only opened the way for. Brigham knew there was a door and called it a Hearth and stopped there — she never says *why* it's one couple in a hundred years and not one in ten. And Mostyn never asks at all; he just finds the leak and pities it." Her eyes came up to his. "Which means *neither of them actually knows what makes it catch.* We're sat here calling it a thing we *agreed* to because that's the comfortable word. But the honest answer is — I don't know what we did. I know it's rare. I know wanting it isn't enough or it wouldn't be rare. I don't know what the rest of it *is.*"

"...You're right. That's exactly the error, and I walked straight into it." Mei said it without defensiveness — caught fairly, the way she'd want to be. "My only *whole* second case is Brigham, and Brigham's an in-love couple, so I've been reading Mostyn assuming his pairs were *us* and calling him cruel for pitying something I happen to experience as a gift." She exhaled. "But the bond needn't be romantic at all. Flitwick said as much — two like minds bent on the same thing, romance optional. *Athena* proves it — there's no love-affair there, and it's the same shape exactly."

She drew Mostyn back under the lamp and read it again, slower now, hunting *who* his subjects actually were rather than what happened to them.

"...And — yes. Look." Her finger moved down the page. "These aren't lovers. He's writing about pairs bound by *proximity and a shared fixation* — two who wanted the same thing and were never *fond* of each other at all. Of course it read as a wound to him. He only ever watched it happen to people who didn't *like* what they could suddenly feel." She glanced up, rueful. "You hadn't read a word of him and you got closer than I did. I'll stop being harsh to the man until I've actually understood him."

She went quiet into the book. Caleb turned back to Crupp.

Mostyn's getting a fairer reading now — his bonds were the unhappy kind, proximity-and-obsession between people who never much liked each other, which is exactly why he could only ever see a wound. The lamp's warm, the third table's quiet under Pince's eye, and the real question — *what makes a bond actually catch* — is still sitting unanswered between the two books.

## Narrator

▼ Monday, 12 September 1977 — 16:31

The afternoon went down into the books the way good afternoons do — the light through the high windows going long and amber, Pince's quill scratching somewhere, the small companionable silence of two people reading different volumes of the same question.

Caleb stayed honestly inside chapter two. Crupp, for all his cold eighteenth-century manner, was careful: the chapter laid out *what the contact is* before it ever touched method. Mind meeting mind at the threshold. What passes. What doesn't. The thing Crupp kept circling was that the entering was never a *taking* of the whole — it was always a *reading,* surface first, the way you read a face before you read a heart; the deep things stayed deep unless the door was forced, and forcing left marks. The framing chapter, true to its name, never told him *how.* It only told him *what sort of thing it was* — and that was exactly the leave he'd been granted.

Across the table Mei worked Mostyn with her new fairness, pencil-less, just her eyes and the occasional small frown when the old man earned it and the occasional smaller nod when he didn't.

Eventually she sat back, pressed the heels of her hands to her eyes, and said into her palms: "I've read enough of a dead man being wrong to last me till supper." A breath. "And I think we've both got more out of an afternoon than McGonagall expected us to. What time is it actually?"

Caleb's watch said gone half four.

Pince was there almost before the covers met, the way she always was, gathering the restricted pair back into her own hands like a mother collecting children from a party she hadn't entirely trusted.

The grille closed behind the books. The afternoon was over, and the long amber evening was coming on.

The Great Hall at supper was the warm version of itself — candles down low over the long tables, the clatter and murmur of a castle feeding itself, the ceiling gone to deep blue with the first stars pricking through. Caleb got food into himself at last, properly, the way Mei had been quietly nagging toward all afternoon, and felt the long day settle a notch with it.

The cohort gathered the way it had taken to gathering — Lin further down with a book he wasn't fooling anyone with, Liri arriving late and dropping in beside Mei mid-sentence, the easy overlap of people who'd decided they belonged at the same stretch of bench regardless of house colours.

Cass was there. Down at the Slytherin end, green and silver, eating among her own — present, accounted for, her dark head bent to something one of her housemates was saying. She hadn't come over. But at some point, across the noise and the candlelight, she caught Caleb looking, and held it half a beat longer than nothing, before she went back to her plate.

Caleb went out ahead of them — peeling off through a side door, the deliberate solo stagger of a man who didn't want three friends seen leaving the Hall in a clump on the night one of them was carrying something heavy. Cass knew the way. She'd find it.

Inside, the room had heard him. The triple-wide bed sat made and turned down, three pillows, the leopard-cat-and-pygmy-owl flannel. Three sets of the matching pyjamas folded at its foot. On the low table: a pot of proper tea, a jug of hot chocolate going faintly steam-curled, and — unfussy, just *there,* the way you'd leave them for someone who might need them and might not — a box of tissues.

And on the keepsake board, newly pinned, two of Imogen's moving photographs. In the first, Hexfield-lit, Caleb had Cass hauled in against his side mid-grin, holding her still for the camera while she pretended to suffer it — the loop catching the half-second where her scowl broke and she laughed despite herself. In the second, low-angled and fierce: Cass come up off the flag at a dead sprint, the half-instant of her downing Caleb clean, up close, all triumph and bared teeth, the picture replaying her victory over and over in joyful little half-seconds.

The room was ready. The door stood waiting on a knock.

And whatever speech she'd half-built on the walk — the composed Vance delivery, the thing said cleanly and got through — came up against a pile of cat-printed pyjamas and faltered into something more honest.

"...You absolute child," she said, with no heat in it at all, her voice not quite steady. She bent, and picked up a set. "Fine. *Fine.*"

She held the mug. She looked at the marshmallows so she wouldn't have to look at either of them yet.

## User

[[so... what am I supposed to be understanding rght now? her two brothers didnt write her of their own accord? her family got them to do it? what is the implication there? ]]

## Narrator

You've got it exactly right. Let me lay it out plainly, no fiction, so we're on the same page:

**The bare facts I invented and put on the page:**
1. Cass has two older brothers. They've been estranged/silent from her for about three years.
2. Both wrote to her within a day of each other — coordinated, not coincidence.
3. The letters are warm on the surface (*come home for Yule, take your place in the family*).
4. The Vance family has spent ~2 years quietly helping the Death Eater side — money, a safe house, passing names. Respectable, behind-the-scenes, not violence-with-their-own-hands.
5. Cass wrote to her father to find out one thing. His one-line answer: *neither of them wrote as a son.*

**What that last line means — the implication you're asking about:**

When her father says *neither wrote as a son,* he's telling her the letters didn't come from her brothers as *people who missed their sister.* They came from **the family as an institution.** The brothers were *told* to write. It's a coordinated summons dressed up as warmth — two signatures on what is really one order from above (the father, or the family's interest).

So the implication is: **this isn't a reconciliation. It's a recruitment.** The family wants Cass — who has drifted away from them, toward people like Caleb — pulled back inside the fold before she's too independent to control. The affection in the letters is bait. Her own brothers were used as the delivery method.

And the gut-punch underneath it (the reason she's on the floor and not just annoyed): she went *hoping* at least one brother reached out because he actually wanted his sister back. Her father's line killed that hope. Neither did. They were both just doing what the family told them.

**What she decided:** nothing — she's not answering, not going home for Yule. Refusing the pull. Which, given what her family is, is not a small or safe thing to do.

Does that framework land cleanly now? If it does, I'll keep going from where she's just said it — Caleb's hands still on her, the thing finally out loud in the room — and you react however you want. If any piece of it feels wrong for the Cass you have in your head, tell me which piece and I'll rebuild it.

## User

[[do we know what death eaters are? is that a widely known organization? are we supposed to be suspecting it is them organized and not just zealots killing people? I mean, it is ruined now obviously if the Death Eaters part was secret because she said they have been helping people on the wrong side for two years yet you added a note that instead said 'The Vance family has been quietly aiding the Death-Eater side ~2 years'... or what? what is the worlds understanding of death eaters?]]

## Narrator

You're asking the right question, and it exposes a real inconsistency I introduced — so let me settle the worldbuilding cleanly, because this is the kind of fact that needs to be fixed *in the canon*, not just hand-waved.

**What is publicly known about Death Eaters in autumn 1977 (this is the era-accurate frame):**

The term "Death Eater" exists but is **not** common, official, or widely used. In 1977 the wizarding public mostly experiences the war as: disappearances, killings, a rising terror, a name people are afraid to say (Voldemort). The *Daily Prophet* tends to report individual atrocities — a family killed, a Muggle-born vanished — without a tidy named organization behind them. The Ministry is reluctant to admit how organized it is. So the average person's understanding is closer to **"there are extremists/zealots doing this, and a figure at the centre people won't name"** than **"there is a formal society called the Death Eaters with a membership roll."** "Death Eater" is an inner-circle / insider word — known to the Order, to the Ministry's serious people, to the families actually involved, and whispered at the edges of the rest. It's not a headline word yet.

**So your instinct is correct, and here's the fix:**

For Cass's reveal to *land* and stay consistent, the distinction matters — and it should be **part of what makes her revelation heavy.** The world at large thinks "zealots, chaos, a terror." What Cass knows, because she's *inside* a family that's involved, is the thing most people *don't* know: **that it's organized. That there's a structure. That respectable old families are quietly feeding it.** Her telling Caleb this isn't her repeating a Prophet headline — it's her handing him insider knowledge that the killing has a *shape* and her family is part of the shape.

That means my tracker note was wrong to flatly call it "the Death-Eater side" as if that's the ambient public framing. Let me correct the canon so it's coherent:

So, to answer your questions directly:

**Do we know what Death Eaters are?** As players/narrator, yes. But *in the world,* in 1977, it's not a household term — it's an insider word. Caleb, depending on how plugged in he is, might have heard it; the man on the Hogwarts Express probably frames the war as "all these awful killings" and an unnamed terror.

**Is it a widely known organization?** No. The public sees zealotry and chaos and a name they won't say. The *organized* nature of it — that it's structured, funded, sheltered by respectable families — is exactly the thing that's *not* widely understood.

**Are we supposed to suspect it's organized rather than just zealots killing people?** *The public isn't.* That's the point. Cass is one of the people who *knows* it's organized, because her family is part of the machinery. So when she tells Caleb "we've been quietly useful to the people doing the killing for two years," she is giving him the insider frame — the killing has a shape, and her bloodline helps hold it up. That's what makes it a confession and not a news report.

**Is it "ruined" because I named it?** No — and here's the precise fix. Cass saying *her family has helped the people doing the killing for two years* is fine and consistent; that's her insider knowledge, freely given to a trusted friend in private. My **tracker note** was the thing that flattened it by calling it "the Death-Eater side" as if that were the neutral public label. I've corrected the canon: the public frame is "zealots/terror/unnamed figure," and the organized truth is insider knowledge. Nothing's ruined — Cass simply knows more than the *Prophet* does, which is the whole weight of the moment.

Want me to re-run her actual spoken reveal so the *language* matches this — her speaking from inside it, without leaning on "Death Eater" as if it were a casual public term — or are you good with the framework and ready to have Caleb respond?

## Narrator

▼ Monday, 12 September 1977 — 18:36

The line lands the way she'd carried it up from the dungeons: *neither wrote as a brother.* Her father, asked the one thing only he could answer, had written back that single sentence — not *they don't mean it,* not *stay away,* just the plain blade of it. The letters weren't two brothers reaching for the sister they'd lost. They were the family's hand, wearing her brothers like gloves. Whatever those two had once been to her, neither of them had picked up a quill *as her brother.* They'd written as Vances. On instruction.

Cass was still tipped back against his knee, looking up at him upside down, the firelight moving on her face, waiting to see what he'd do now that she'd put the whole of it in the room.

## User

[[why would her father tell her that?]]

## Narrator

That's the right question, and it's the one that decides who Cass's father actually is — so let me think it through rather than hand-wave, because his motive reshapes the whole Vance thread.

A few possibilities, and they're not equal:

**1. He's quietly on her side.** The most human reading: her father is *in* the family's complicity — the cheques, the Wiltshire house — but he doesn't want *Cass* pulled in. Telling her "neither wrote as a brother" is him tipping his hand: *don't be fooled by the warmth, this is the family's reach, not your brothers missing you.* It's a warning dressed as a fact. He can't openly protect her without exposing himself, so he gives her the one true sentence and lets her draw the line herself. That makes him a tragic figure — complicit, but spending what little cover he has to keep one child out.

**2. He's testing her.** Colder: he reports the truth flatly to see what she does with it. If she comes home anyway, she's biddable. If she refuses, he learns where she stands. Less warm, more Vance.

**3. He didn't mean it as a kindness at all.** He answered the literal question she asked ("did they write me themselves?") with the literal truth, no agenda — and the *kindness* is entirely in how Cass chose to read it. He may not even know he handed her the thing that freed her.

My instinct — and what fits the "angel on her left shoulder," good-coaxed-out-of-a-hard-family Cass you've built — is **option 1, with a shadow of 3**: a father who is part of the rot but does not want this particular daughter consumed by it, and who can only help her in sentences short enough to deny. That gives the Vance thread a live, dangerous ally inside the family and a real cost to Cass's refusal (she's now protecting *him* by not making it obvious he warned her).

But this is your call, because it sets who the father is for the rest of the campaign. Which way do you want him: **the father spending his cover to save her, the father testing her, or the father who told the truth and never knew what it did?**

[[I guess it just reads odd to me because you say the family pressured them to write these letters trying to get her to come home.... so... who is that? the dad is family. and generally the patriarch is the seat of power, especially in a house like that. it can be others. I jsut want it to make sense now]]

## Narrator

You're right, and that's a genuine plot hole, not a vibe issue: I said "the family" pressured the brothers and also that her father gave her the sentence that armed her against it. If the father is the patriarch, those two things can't both be true without explanation — he can't be the hand pushing the letters *and* the hand quietly tipping her off. So let me make it actually cohere. There are only a few clean ways, and they each say something different about the house.

**Option A — The father isn't the seat of power anymore.** In some old houses the title and the *actual* control have come apart. Maybe Cassius-the-elder (her father) is the nominal head, but he's aging, or ill, or has lost the family's confidence by being soft — and the real engine now is someone else: the eldest brother, an uncle, a grandmother, the family's marriage-and-money matriarch. "The family" that pressured the brothers = that power center. The father is a sidelined patriarch who can still tell his daughter one true sentence but can no longer stop the machine. This makes his line to her an act of quiet, defeated love — *I couldn't stop them sending it, but I can tell you what it really is.*

**Option B — The father IS the power, and the warning is a test or a hedge.** He ordered the letters. Telling Cass "neither wrote as a brother" isn't protection — it's him being unable to lie to her, or laying down a marker (*I'm not going to dress this up; come back knowing exactly what it is, or don't*). Colder, and it makes him the antagonist with a sliver of honesty rather than an ally.

**Option C — She didn't write her father at all; she wrote someone else.** We've been saying "her father," but if the father is the one who ordered the letters, the person who'd *warn* her is more plausibly a different relative — the sidelined one, the black-sheep aunt, a grandparent who hates what the house has become. Re-point the "one person who could tell me the truth" at them, and the patriarch stays cleanly the antagonist.

My read of the Cass you've built — the one with the good coaxed out of her, pulled between a hard family and people like Caleb — is that **Option A** is the richest: a father who is technically the head but no longer the power, who loves this daughter and is losing the house to harder people (the eldest brother / an uncle / the matriarch), and who spent his one safe sentence on her. It gives the family an internal fracture, a real villain who isn't him, and a costly ally on the inside.

But you decide the shape of the house. Who actually holds the power in the Vance family — the father (and the warning is a test, Option B), someone else with the father as a fading ally (Option A), or do we re-point who she wrote to (Option C)?

## User

[[am I supposed to understand Marked now?]]

## Narrator

You're meant to have a working sense of it, yes — Cass used "marked" twice and I never put the meaning on the page. Same bug as before: clear in my head, opaque to you. Here it is plainly, and it's era-canon, not improvisation:

**The Mark** is the brand Voldemort puts on his sworn inner circle — burned into the inside of the left forearm, a skull with a serpent through its mouth. It's not decorative. It does things:

- It's a **loyalty bond and a leash.** Taking it is an oath; you don't take it casually and you don't get to quietly leave afterward. The marked are *his,* in a way the hangers-on and donors aren't.
- It's a **summons.** He can call through it — the Mark burns/darkens, and the marked are expected to drop everything and come. It's how he musters them.
- It marks the **difference between inner circle and everyone else.** Plenty of people help — money, shelter, sympathy, looking away. Far fewer are *marked.* Being marked means you're past helping-from-a-distance; you're an instrument he can call by name.

So when Cass says **Lucian is "marked"**, the weight of it is: her eldest brother isn't a sympathizer or a financier like the rest of the respectable family. He took the oath. He's inner circle — sworn, callable, and (her word) *likes* it. That's a different and worse thing than what the family-at-large is doing. The Vances fund and shelter; Lucian went and gave Voldemort his arm.

And it sharpens the danger of her refusal: she's not just declining a family that writes cheques. She's declining a house now effectively run by one of *his.*

Let me put the meaning into the canon so it's never just floating again:

That's it on record now. Short version, so we're aligned before you act:

**Marked = took the Dark Mark = sworn inner circle.** Branded forearm, an oath he can summon you through, and the hard line between "helps the cause" and "belongs to him." Lucian crossed that line. The rest of the Vances haven't — they bankroll and shelter from a safe distance. That gap is exactly why Cass's eldest brother running the house is so much heavier than the family merely being unpleasant old blood.

No new fiction this turn — just the definition surfaced. We're still at the same in-fiction moment: Cass tipped back against your knee, firelight, the whole of it finally said, the room quiet around her. Your move whenever you're ready — keep giving her the silence, or speak.

[[jesus god... I dont know who voldemort is. or any of these things about the mark]]

## Narrator

You're not behind — those are genuinely two of the most spoiler-laden names in the whole mythology, and I've been dropping them like you'd been read into them. Let me lay both out plainly, no fiction, so you've got the bedrock everyone in this world stands on.

**Voldemort.** He's the figure at the centre of the war — the one doing all of this. Born Tom Riddle, a half-blood who reinvented himself into "Lord Voldemort" and built a movement around blood-purity: the idea that pure-blood wizards should rule, and Muggle-borns (and Muggles, and "half-breeds") are beneath them or should be purged. By 1977 he's the most feared dark wizard alive. Crucially, **most people won't say his name** — they say "You-Know-Who" or "He-Who-Must-Not-Be-Named," because fear of him is so total that the name itself feels dangerous. So in-world: the public knows there's a terrifying someone at the top of the killings; the *name* Voldemort is something only the bold, the inside, or the defiant actually speak aloud. (That's a usable test, by the way — who flinches when Caleb says the name tells you a lot about them.)

**The Death Eaters** are his followers/movement — the name I covered earlier. **The Mark** is the brand on his sworn inner circle (forearm, skull-and-serpent, an oath he can summon them through). The donors-and-shelterers (the Vance family at large) are the outer ring; the *marked* (Lucian) are the sworn core.

So the whole stack, top to bottom:
- **Voldemort** — the man at the top. Most won't speak his name.
- **Death Eaters** — his organized followers. An insider term, not a *Prophet* headline.
- **The Marked** — the sworn inner circle, branded, callable. The hard core.
- **Families like the Vances** — respectable outer ring: money, safe houses, names. Hands clean.

And the public, reading the *Prophet* over breakfast, mostly sees none of that structure — just disappearances, dead families, and a terror with a name they're too frightened to say.

Here's my honest read of what keeps happening: this mythology has a *lot* of load-bearing names (Voldemort, Death Eaters, the Mark, the Order, blood-purity, and more coming), and I keep using them as if shared. Rather than keep stopping cold every time one surfaces, do you want me to **make Cass the one who teaches it to Caleb** — i.e. when she uses a term like "marked" or names Voldemort, she explains it in the dialogue itself, because she's bringing a sheltered-from-it friend inside her family's reality? That keeps the framework arriving *on the page,* in scene, instead of me assuming it and you having to flag it. Or if you'd rather get these primers OOC like this and keep the fiction clean, I'll do that instead.

Either way — want me to put Voldemort on the canon record like I did the Mark, and then we pick back up at Cass in the firelight?

The strategist in him went quiet. This wasn't a plan. This was the other thing.

"...Your mother doesn't know me," she managed.

## Narrator

▼ Monday, 12 September 1977 — 18:46

He leaned down from above her and set his forehead to hers, upside down, and held it there — close, the firelight gone soft and orange at the edges of everything, her eyes a hand's breadth from his and nowhere to hide in them.

For a moment neither of them said anything. The room did its quiet work around them: the fire ticking, Mei near and unhurried, the two photographs of Cass loving her from the board across the room — held-and-laughing, fierce-and-winning — the whole small world he'd built so she'd have a wall to put her back against.

Cass looked up into him and let herself be looked at, which was the harder thing. The composure wasn't gone. It was just laid down on the floor beside the mug, for now, where she could pick it up again later when she needed it. Her breath went unsteady once and then evened.

"...Don't you dare say something kind right now," she murmured, eyes not leaving his. "I'll come apart, and I've only just got the lid back on." A wet flicker of the old dryness. "Just — stay there a minute. Like that. That's allowed."

## User

*stay for a minute just like that..then hold the sides of her face, and kiss her lips for 10 seconds calmly, gently*

## Narrator

▼ Monday, 12 September 1977 — 18:47

He stayed there the full minute, the way she'd asked — forehead to forehead, no words, letting the quiet hold what kindness would have broken.

Then he brought his hands up and took the sides of her face, gently, thumbs along her cheekbones, and tipped down and kissed her. Slow. Unhurried. Ten seconds of just that — calm, soft, his mouth on hers and nothing demanded by it, the kind of kiss that's an answer rather than a question. *You're not alone in this. You're not getting fetched anywhere. You have somewhere to be.*

When he drew back it was only an inch, hands still framing her face, and Cass kept her eyes shut a moment longer like she was holding the warmth of it in before the world came back.

"...That," she said quietly, eyes still closed, "was a deeply unfair thing to do to a woman who's trying to keep her composure." Her eyes opened, wet and steady and very much hers. "Thank you. For the room. The drawer. Manchester. All of it." A breath. Mei's hand had found Cass's shoulder somewhere in the quiet, and stayed. "I'm all right. Or I will be. Which is more than I could've said walking in here."

## Narrator

I'm going to pause the scene here for a moment, out of voice.

The three of them just had a long, tender beat that was about comfort and trust — Cass arrived in crisis, got held, got offered a home for the holidays. That's the register the room is in. A finger to her breast right now flips it toward the sexual without anything from Cass signaling she's there, and given where she is tonight — wrung out, lid-just-back-on, "I'll come apart" — I don't want to write her into a sexual beat on her worst evening without her own clear pull toward it.

That's not a hard no to Cass and the three of them ever going there — the found-family is established as that kind of close, with consent that runs all ways. It's specifically that *tonight, in this state,* I'd be putting it in motion off a one-line physical move rather than off Cass wanting it, and that's the thing I'll steer on.

So: Cass can absolutely respond — and if *she* turns it that way, that's her call to make on the page. Want to play her reaction (she's your read as much as mine in this moment), or keep Caleb's gesture as the affectionate-but-not-escalating thing it might be and let her answer it? ▼ Monday, 12 September 1977 — 18:50

Caleb extended one finger and touched just the very tip of it to the outer curve of Cass's breast through the flannel — the lightest possible press, the gesture of a boy who could not believe he was getting away with it and wanted full credit for the crime.

Cass went still. Looked down at the offending fingertip. Looked back up at him with the flat, ancient patience of a woman who has known exactly this brand of idiot for years.

Cass exhaled, and the laugh in it was real, and the tear-tracks had dried somewhere in the last few minutes without anyone noticing — which was, perhaps, the entire point of the finger.

Cass looked at the laughing one a long time.

## Narrator

This one I'm going to answer out of voice, because the "Bone Zone" isn't a character with appetites — it's the room you built, and the question is really *you* checking in with the spirit of the whole thing. So let me answer it straight rather than have an inanimate room ask for a girl's blood.

Spilling her blood as some price for that would undercut the exact thing you just spent two hours making: a safe room where the worst version of her future gets set down, not paid for. The happier path *is* the offering, if anything's owed at all. She walks out of here lighter than she came in. That's the win condition.

If you ever do want the cabin/the Room to have a darker register — a place with a cost, that asks for things — that's a real direction we can build deliberately, with its own setup. But it'd be a *choice* we make on purpose, not something I'd spring onto the end of Cass's best night.

So: she's on the better path. No blade. Let me close the beat in voice.

▼ Monday, 12 September 1977 — 18:56

The room held its warmth and asked nothing of any of them. Whatever Caleb half-listened for in it — some old appetite, some price — wasn't there. ▼ Monday, 12 September 1977 — 19:14

So he made it. Vanilla and a pinch of salt off the heat. Cass appointed herself quality control, which meant a finger in the pan the moment his back was turned. Mei lined the tin without being asked, the way she did. Caleb pressed it flat, wet the spoon-back, smoothed the top, and the room — because it could not resist participating — produced a small cold slate shelf in the wall to set it on, conveniently several hours colder than the rest of the cabin.

Cass, mouth full of raw fudge, entirely unrepentant: "Quality control. You'll thank me."

▼ Tuesday, 13 September 1977 — 07:03

Caleb surfaced slow, the way you do in a bed that doesn't want to give you up. The cabin had kept its warmth through the night; grey-gold morning light came through windows showing a soft, undramatic Tuesday. Athena had migrated at some point and was a small warm weight tucked against his collarbone, one eye cracking open to register that he was awake before she decided it wasn't yet worth her own trouble.

Mei was already half-up on her far side, breathing in the particular not-quite-asleep way that meant she'd been awake a few minutes and was enjoying not moving. And Cass was still under, properly under — face slack, the composure she wore like armour entirely off, dead to the world in the middle of the bed where she'd chosen to be. The fudge plate sat soft on the floor. The fire was a few orange coals.

Somewhere out past the cabin walls, the castle was beginning its day. Potions at nine. But not yet.

## User

[jump halfway through with quick synopsis in case I really care about this potion]

## Narrator

▼ Tuesday, 13 September 1977 — 09:48

Quick synopsis of the half you skipped: Slughorn set the class on a **Draught of Peace** — the proper NEWT version, no training wheels, the one that punishes a heavy hand. Powdered moonstone, syrup of hellebore, the careful business of stirring until the fumes go from grey to silver-white. Mei kept the heat; Caleb kept the count. The bench either side of you had at least one cauldron go ominously thick and dark. Slughorn drifted, beamed, dropped a name or two of old students who'd "had the touch," and made his usual warm circuit past the benches he favours.

Now, mid-task — the smell of it has shifted, and Caleb's cauldron is sitting somewhere on the edge between a clean silver vapour and the faint grey wisp that means it wants one more counter-clockwise turn before it tips.

Mei, low, not looking up from the surface: "It's about to go silver or sulk. Your call on the next stir — I'm holding the flame where it is."

## User

*one more counterclockwise stir and tip* [[tell me what this potion does]]

## Narrator

▼ Tuesday, 13 September 1977 — 09:50

One more turn, counter-clockwise — slow, the way the unforgiving ones want — and the grey wisp thinned, caught, and went clean silver-white, the vapour curling up off the surface light as breath. Mei held the flame dead steady through it. Caleb tipped the cauldron and decanted: the draught ran out pale and clear into the waiting phials, a faint shimmer to it, the steam coming off in slow silvery spirals rather than billowing.

Slughorn, mid-circuit, slowed as he passed and looked at the spirals with the particular pleasure of a man who's seen a lot of botched ones this morning. "*There's* a silver line. You see, the rest of you — that's what it ought to look like." He moved on, warm, leaving the small public approval hanging over the bench.

OOC — what the Draught of Peace does: it's a calming draught. Taken correctly, it soothes anxiety, quiets agitation, eases grief and panic — it settles a frightened or overwrought mind. It's the standard thing a Healer or a frazzled exam-sitter reaches for. The catch, and why Slughorn made a NEWT exercise of it: get the moonstone or hellebore wrong and it doesn't just fail — it over-sedates, dropping the drinker into a sleep so heavy it can't easily be woken, occasionally a permanent one. So a clean silver vapour isn't vanity; grey-and-sulking is the dangerous version.

## User

[[so do I get to bottle this and take it with me?]]

## Narrator

▼ Tuesday, 13 September 1977 — 09:51

That depends on Slughorn, and Slughorn's already half-decided. The usual NEWT custom is that the day's brew gets handed up to the front bench for marking and the class's stock cupboard — but a well-made Draught of Peace is exactly the sort of thing he'd let a favoured student keep a phial of, especially having just held yours up as the example. You'd only have to ask, lightly, the way you ask Slughorn things.

So: do you want Caleb to ask whether he can keep a phial? (And it's worth knowing — a brewed Draught of Peace doesn't keep forever; it's good for a few weeks before it goes off, so it's a "have it in the bag for a bad week soon" item, not a permanent stock.)

## User

*yes I ask* [[continue class]]

## Narrator

▼ Tuesday, 13 September 1977 — 09:53

Caleb caught Slughorn on his next pass and asked it light — might he keep a phial of this one, since it had come out clean? Slughorn waved a hand, pleased to be asked. "Take one, take one — a well-made Peace is a sensible thing to have by you. Just don't go dosing your friends before exams, the line's thinner than people think." He chuckled at his own caution and moved off, leaving Caleb to stopper a phial for his bag and surrender the rest to the front bench.

The remaining half hour ran the way good NEWT lessons do: cooling, decanting, the wiping-down of scales, Slughorn working the room and dispensing anecdotes about a witch from the '40s who could brew this "in her sleep, which is rather the point, eh?" Mei labelled their phials in her neat hand. By the bell the dungeon smelled of cold moonstone and quenched fires, and Caleb had one clean Draught of Peace stoppered and stowed.

The bell went. Care of Magical Creatures next, up and out to the paddocks.

## User

[[didnt I have 4 total version? can you repeat them to me]]

## Narrator

You're right — it's four. Cass's review at the cabin pushed it from three to four. Here they are:

- **Control** — standard Wiggenweld, water-only solvent, brewed to the textbook. The baseline everything's measured against.
- **Experimental A** — bark-and-salamander half done standard in water; the moondew-and-mint half made up in pure ethanol; the two joined at the normal moment.
- **Experimental B** — same as A, except the ethanol half is brought up to brewing temperature and held 90 seconds before the join.
- **Experimental D** — Cass's addition: pure ethanol on the moondew side, **cold-brewed at ambient temperature** (no heat at all), joined to the standard hot bark-and-salamander side at the canonical moment. This one's the real prize — if it works, it suggests the moondew side has been heated unnecessarily for ~300 years.

The framing split Cass advised: pitch the narrow three-batch "can alcohol carry a bind" version to Slughorn as the graded experiment, and run **D in parallel as an exploratory side experiment**, off the main grade, with his permission noted in the cover.

Two things from her notes worth remembering now that you've got the flask: assay each batch on a peeled-potato cut at days 1, 3, 7, and 14, anchored with saline at 0.9%; and pure ethanol is Ministry-controlled for tax, needing Slughorn's signature on the *procurement*, not just the project. (You just drew it straight from his stores at his hand, so that signature box is effectively ticked.)

There was no Experimental C, by the way — Cass's batch D *replaced* an earlier cosolvent-C idea, which is part of why the lettering jumps A, B, D.

## User

[[so I have enough for 1 extra of my own design?]]

## Narrator

Slughorn's line was "enough for three honest batches" — and the three ethanol-touching batches are A, B, and D (the control is water-only, so it costs you no spirit). So by his measure the flask covers exactly the three ethanol batches you already have planned, with no stated surplus.

That said, "three honest batches" is a Slughorn estimate, not a precise measure — he eyeballed a flask, he didn't titrate it to your methodology. Whether there's slack for a fifth batch of your own design depends on how much each batch actually draws. Two things work in your favour: ethanol is a livelier solvent than water (his own warning), so the moondew side may need *less* volume than a water-equivalent would; and if your own-design batch is a cold-brew variant like D, it's not boiling any off. So realistically: probably a little headroom, but not a guaranteed clean fourth ethanol batch unless you brew lean.

If you want certainty, the move is to ask Slughorn for a touch extra now while you're standing at his stores and he's pleased with you — "might I have a little over, in case I want to run one of my own" — rather than discovering mid-brew that you're short. He'd likely top it without fuss, having just praised your work.

Want Caleb to ask for the extra, or run with what's in the flask and brew lean?

[[the control does not have alcohol, therefore enough alcohol for 3 is already an extra]]

## Narrator

You're right, and I had it backwards. The control is water-only — it touches none of the spirit. So "enough for three honest batches" of *ethanol* covers exactly your three ethanol batches: A, B, and D. The control runs alongside on water and costs you nothing from the flask.

So no — there's no built-in surplus for a fifth batch of your own. The three ethanol allocations are spoken for by A, B, and D. Any own-design batch would either eat into one of those or need Caleb to brew lean enough to scrape a fourth out of a flask Slughorn measured for three.

If you want a genuine extra ethanol batch with room to spare, the clean move is still to ask him for a bit over while you're at his stores and he's pleased — but that's a choice, not something the current flask already affords.

## Narrator

▼ Tuesday, 13 September 1977 — 10:45

"That's the spirit," Kettleburn said, mishearing it as classroom keenness — or not mishearing it at all, hard to say with him — and threw the crate lid back.

Inside, in a nest of damp straw, sat something the size of a dinner plate: a glossy dark-shelled creature with far too many legs folded under it, a low row of dim red eyes, and a pair of pincers it opened and closed with slow, dry deliberation. The smell came up sharp — vinegar and crushed beetle. It didn't rush. It simply turned, with awful patience, to face the nearest warm bodies at the rail.

"A *Crabbing Knurl* — south-coast, salt-marsh stock. Won't poison you, won't chase you. But it will take a finger it decides it's owed, and it holds a grudge longer than your grandmother." Kettleburn beamed round at them. "Today's task is simple. One of you is going to convince it to walk onto a feeding board. Without losing the finger. Any volunteers — or shall I pick?"

Beside Caleb, Mei went very still in the particular way of someone who has already decided it will not be her.

## User

"tell me what a feeding board is and what they eat, and then yes, I am ready."

## Narrator

▼ Tuesday, 13 September 1977 — 10:46

"A feeding board," Kettleburn said, lifting a flat plank with a shallow lip round three sides and a smear of something dark drying in the middle, "is just that — a board you bring the food to, so the beast learns the board is where good things happen and your hand is not the menu. Knurls are slow learners but they're learners. Get one onto the board twice and it'll come to the board on its own forever after."

He reached into a covered pail and held up a fat, glistening grey thing between two fingers — a slug the length of a thumb, dripping. "Salt-marsh slugs. Knurls go mad for them — it's the brine. The trick's not the bait, it's the *patience*. You lay the slug at the far lip of the board, you keep your hand low and slow and never over the top of him — a Knurl reads anything above its shell as a gull come to flip it — and you wait. Rush him and you lose the finger. Bore him and he'll sit there a week."

He held the slug out to Caleb, the Knurl already swivelling its dim red eyes toward the smell.

"Low and slow, Mr Waddell. He's all yours."

▼ Tuesday, 13 September 1977 — 10:47

Caleb tipped a few drops of the pale draught onto the slug — it soaked in, the slug going faintly silver at the edges — and laid it on the far lip of the board. Then he set the board down near the crate, kept his hands low and well clear, and settled back on his heels to wait, nowhere near over the shell.

Halfway through, something changed. The grudging tension went out of it. The pincers loosened. It settled its plate-shell flat to the board with a small contented click and simply... stayed, eating in no hurry at all, utterly untroubled by the warm bodies at the rail.

Kettleburn leaned in, eyebrows climbing. "...Now what did you *do* to it. I've never seen a Knurl go soft like a fed cat." He looked from the placid creature to Caleb, more curious than cross. "That wasn't just patience. What was on the slug?"
