## User

[[there is no reasonable way to stroke this thing, right?]]

## Narrator

Right — not really, and it'd be a bad idea to try. A Knurl's whole back is hard plate-shell, so there's nothing soft to stroke up there; and reaching over the top of it is the one move Kettleburn warned against, the thing it reads as a gull coming to flip it. The most you've already got — its weight leaned against your wrist while it eats — is about the entire affectionate register the species offers. A hand coming down onto the shell from above would undo the calm faster than the draught built it.

If you wanted to push the contact a hair further without breaking the rule, the honest options are things like letting it keep leaning on your wrist, or a slow touch *from the side, low,* against a foreleg or the shell's edge where it can see your hand the whole way — never a top-down pet. But "stroke it like a cat" isn't a thing this creature has anatomy or temperament for.

Want Caleb to try a low side-touch, or just let it lean and eat and leave the win where it is?

## Narrator

▼ Tuesday, 13 September 1977 — 10:53

While it ate, Caleb tried a slow exploration — fingers low and visible, coming at it from the side, never over the shell. He ran a careful fingertip along the seam where the plate-shell met the softer underside, and found what passed for a chest: a band of paler, leathery hide tucked under the front rim, between the first pair of legs, warm and faintly damp where the armour didn't reach.

The Knurl froze mid-chew. Every leg locked. For one held breath it could have gone either way — and Caleb kept the finger still, no press, just resting there in the soft seam.

Then it relented. The legs eased. It went back to the slug, and the leathery underside gave a slow involuntary flex against his fingertip — not affection exactly, more the dim reflex of a creature that has decided a warm steady pressure on its soft parts is, against all its instincts, allowed.

"Well I never," Kettleburn breathed at the rail. "It's letting you touch its underplate. That's the bit they guard with their lives." He shook his head slowly. "You've a hand for the unlovable ones, Mr Waddell. Don't let it make you careless — but don't waste it, either."

## Narrator

▼ Tuesday, 13 September 1977 — 13:18

Lunch went down fast — Liri fed, Caleb's pockets and a borrowed satchel filling with kit between the Hall and the Tower: mortar and pestle, the small brass burner and stand, a rack of clean phials, stirring rod, scales. ▼ Tuesday, 13 September 1977 — 13:30

"Notes — yes. I was going to anyway; you'd write *'went green, smelled nice'* and call it data." Mei pulled a fresh sheet, ruled it into columns with a flick — *time · colour · consistency · smell · action* — and uncapped her ink. "Talk me through every step out loud as you do it. Eight minutes of that, the colour darkening, a faint skin trying to form and stirred back under. The moondew-and-mint side she made up beside it in plain water — a cool, pale silver-green infusion, sweetish.

Then the join, off the boil. Caleb poured the pale green into the brick-red — and it bloomed, the red running up through orange to a clear, settled healing green in the space of three seconds, the steam going suddenly sweet and herbal, the whole thing smoothing to a glossy, even liquid.

Mei caught it without looking up. "Thirteen forty-nine — join. Red to green in *three seconds,* clean turn, no muddiness. Smooths glossy. Smell flips sweet. That's textbook." She underlined the final line twice. "There's your baseline. Bright clear green, smooth, about nineteen minutes start to finish. Everything else gets measured against that."

## Narrator

▼ Tuesday, 13 September 1977 — 14:26

Mei pushed off the bench and came without a word of argument — fell into step beside him across the grounds, past the last of the greenhouses, down to where the mown lawn gave out and the trees began.

The Forbidden Forest didn't announce itself. It simply started: the grass thinning, then a wall of trunks older and closer-set than any wood had a right to be, and a line where the afternoon light stopped as though it had been told to. The temperature dropped a few degrees in the last ten feet. The air coming out of the trees smelled of cold leaf-mould and something greener and ranker underneath, and the birdsong that had been everywhere on the lawn was simply *gone* — the canopy swallowed sound, gave back only the occasional far-off creak of wood settling, or a single wingbeat too heavy for a songbird.

Caleb stopped at the treeline. A few yards in, the path — if it was a path — bent out of sight into green dark. Nothing moved that he could see. The forest sat there being exactly what it was: not hostile, not welcoming, just old and indifferent and very much awake.

Mei stood at his shoulder, looking in with him. "It's louder when it's quiet, this place," she said, low. "How far in are we being today?"

The light changed in a stride — gold to green to a dim underwater dusk, the canopy closing the sky to scraps. The ground went soft and deep underfoot, centuries of leaf-fall giving like a held breath. Trunks rose around them broad as towers, mossed on their north sides, roots humped across the faint path. The cold settled in properly. The silence had a texture to it, a listening quality, and the deeper they went the more it felt less like absence and more like attention.

A hundred feet in, Caleb's foot found a print in the soft earth — cloven, deep, far larger than any deer. And somewhere off through the trunks, not close, not far, something large shifted its weight and went still, as if it had heard them stop.

## Narrator

▼ Tuesday, 13 September 1977 — 14:28

He crouched to the print. Two toes, cleanly split — cloven, not the single round hoof of a horse. That ruled out the things he'd half-braced for this deep: the centaurs left horse-prints, so did the thestrals. This was something else, and it was *big* — the span of it wider than his two hands laid side by side, pressed deep enough to say real weight stood here recently, the mud still holding water in the bottom of each toe. Bigger than a deer. Bigger than the unicorns he knew left dainty cloven marks near the forest's edge. Nothing in a NEWT syllabus sat cleanly over a track that size.

Looking around: the single print wasn't alone. A line of them came out of the deeper dark, crossed the path, and went on — a trail, unhurried, made by something that walked on two legs of that pattern or four close-set ones. And off where the trail led, the same direction as the weight that had shifted, the green murk simply didn't resolve. Mei's hand had gone tight in his.

## Narrator

▼ Tuesday, 13 September 1977 — 14:30

They went on, slow, wands up, hand in hand — following the trail at a distance rather than letting it follow them. The prints led between the great trunks, and the forest let them pass, the dim green holding its breath around their footsteps. Caleb kept his eyes up and wide, trusting the logic of it: a thing big enough to leave *that* would be a shape against the trees long before it was a danger.

And it was. Forty feet on, the trail opened into a clearing where a little more sky leaked through — and there it stood, unhurried, exactly as large as the print promised.

A unicorn. But not the white of the storybooks: this one was *grown,* a full adult, its coat the deep burnished gold-bronze of a beast many years old, cloven hooves sunk in the leaf-mould, a single spiralled horn catching what light there was. It had already seen them. It stood broadside, head turned, one dark liquid eye fixed on the pair of them — not fleeing, not charging. Measuring.

Mei breathed out very slowly. "...That's no foal," she whispered. "Caleb. Adult unicorns don't *let* themselves be seen. Don't move fast."

▼ Tuesday, 13 September 1977 — 14:33

"Put your wand over there with mine." Mei hesitated half a heartbeat — every instinct she had arguing — then leaned and laid her wand in the leaf-mould beside his, both of them disarmed now, nothing between them and the gold beast but open hands and a sleepy owl.

Caleb went through what he knew while it came on.

Unicorns were XXXX-rated and shy past all reason — they fled humans, chose never to be seen, and what little tolerance they had ran to *witches,* not wizards; a grown one walking toward a man was backwards on its face. They were reckoned near-sentient: not centaur-clever, not speakers, but deep-perceptive, able to *feel* the intent of a thing that approached — purity, or the lack of it. That was why they were hunted, and why hunting them was monstrous: the horn was a potion treasure, antidote-grade, worth a heavy purse; the tail-hair was a wand core, one of the three great ones, the very kind Ollivander prized; and the blood — silver, and it would keep a dying man alive, but cursed him for slaying something so clean to get it. Fast enough to outrun most threats, a gore and a kick in them if cornered. Foals trusting; adults never.

And the colour was *wrong.* He knew the progression cold — born gold, gone silver in the growing, pure white at maturity. Gold was a *foal's* coat. This thing had the size and bearing of years on it and wore the colour of a newborn, and nothing he'd ever been taught had a slot for that.

It stopped an arm's length off, lowered its great gold head between the two of them, and breathed warm grass-and-snow over Athena and his open hands.

## Narrator

▼ Tuesday, 13 September 1977 — 14:36

He held the comb out flat on his palm where the great dark eye could study it, and told it everything he meant to do before he did it — *brush you, your whole body, take only what falls, nothing by force, ask you at the end.* The unicorn watched the bone comb, then him, and stood.

So he began. He worked the comb down the long fall of the mane first, slow strokes, untangling the forest out of it — a burr here, a knot of bramble there, leaf-litter sifting free. He did the crest of the neck, the warm gold shoulder, the flank under Mei's quiet hand, the feathered fetlocks above each cloven hoof, and last, murmuring, the tail. Athena rode the withers like a tiny foreman. The unicorn leaned faintly into the strokes, eyes half-lidding, the ancient wariness gone soft.

Hair came loose as he worked — it always does, in grooming. Pale strands gathered in the comb's teeth and lifted away on their own, long mane-threads and finer coat-hairs, settling in his palm and on the leaf-mould without a single one pulled.

When he'd finished, he gathered what had fallen, held it up, and asked.

The unicorn looked at the small pale handful. And huffed once — warm breath across his fingers — but did not step away. It dipped its head, deliberate, and touched its horn, just for a moment, to the back of his gathering hand. *Take them.* Then it lifted its head, turned, and walked unhurried back into the green dark, gold dimming between the trunks until it was gone.

▼ Tuesday, 13 September 1977 — 15:06

Caleb tore a leaf off the cutting in his glove and put it in his mouth before Mei could say a word.

It bit back. Not teeth — chemistry: a sharp green pepperiness that flared to a genuine sting on the tongue, mustard-hot, the self-fertilising sap doing to his mouth what it was bred to do to grazing slugs. His eyes watered. The leaf squirmed once, unpleasantly, on the way down.

"*Caleb.*" Mei's hand froze mid-graft. Not loud — just appalled, low, the particular tone of a woman who has watched this man *Accio* a broom and charm a finger-eating Knurl in the same week. "Those *fertilise themselves,* you absolute — do you know what that *means* about the sap—"

Sprout was already there, drawn by some teacher's radar for a swallowed plant, plucking the cutting out of his hand. "Did you *eat* that? Mr Waddell. It's not poison — you'll have a mouth like a chimney and a regrettable afternoon, but you'll live." She eyed him, half exasperated, half the reluctant fondness he kept earning off staff. "Water. Now. And no — before you ask — we are *not* writing that up as research."

"Professor Sprout... since this is on the milder in comparison side to breaking down organic materials, do you know if this has been use in alchemy or potionmaking to break ingredients down into components or to extract anything?"

## Narrator

▼ Tuesday, 13 September 1977 — 15:11

Sprout straightened from her graft, glad of a real question after the eating.

"Now *that's* the right end of your brain working. Yes — roughly. Saps like this one get used in prep when you've an ingredient too tough or too fibrous to open with a knife or a kettle. You let a measured bit of something caustic rot the matter down, soften it, break the cell walls, so the good comes out easier. Maceration with teeth, you might call it." She wiped her hands. "But it's a blunt tool — it doesn't *choose.* It'll eat the virtue you want right along with the husk you don't, so you time it close and neutralise it sharp, same root I just gave you."

She nodded down the bench, handing the harder half off. "The *clever* version — a reagent that breaks a thing into its parts so you can lift out one component and leave the rest — that's alchemy proper. Verity Sage's ground, or Horace's for the potion side. I grow the stuff; I don't separate it." A shrewd look at him. "Why. What are you trying to open that won't open?"

"Wiggentree bark"

## Narrator

▼ Tuesday, 13 September 1977 — 15:12

"Wiggentree bark." Caleb said it, and Sprout's face cleared with recognition.

"Ah — your *julep.*" She'd had the whole of that potion off him days ago, asphodel and all. "The bark that won't steep cold. Yes, I see where your head's gone — if heat's what cracks it open, and you don't want heat, find another way to crack it." She considered, hands on the bench. "A digestive sap *would* open it cold. That's the temptation. But mind what I said — it's a blunt mouth. Wiggentree's whole worth to a Wiggenweld is the *virtue* in that bark, and a sap doesn't know virtue from fibre. You might dissolve the husk beautifully and dissolve the healing right along with it, and end up with a jar of expensive water."

She straightened, handing the thought up the chain. "Grinding it fine, like you're already doing — that's the gentle end of the same idea, more surface, no violence. A sap's the far other end. And if you want the *middle* — something that opens the bark and frees only the part you're after — that's a separating reagent, and that's Sage's craft, not mine." A nod. "But I'd not pour it into your clean batches to find out. That's a whole new variable. New jar, new question."

## Narrator

▼ Tuesday, 13 September 1977 — 15:12

"That's always the problem," Sprout agreed, with the satisfaction of a teacher whose student has found the real edge of a thing on his own. "Every tool that opens something also costs you something. The whole craft's just choosing which cost you can afford." She caught the pun a beat late and snorted. "*Branch out.* Cheek. Go on, then — stick with the gentle way, grind it fine, and if the cold won't give you what you want in a fortnight, *then* you go bother Verity Sage about reagents. No sense reaching for the caustic before the patient way's even failed."

She moved off down the bench to rescue someone's collapsing graft, leaving Caleb and Mei to their pots. Mei snipped a last clean cut, dropped the grafted cutting into its pot, and tipped her head at him — the lesson all but done, the bell close, Hagrid's chimney smoking down the slope.

"Save the bark question for if the bark fails you," she said. "We've a half-giant to make cry first."

*I pocket a handfull of the cuttings and the remaining root*

## Narrator

▼ Tuesday, 13 September 1977 — 15:13

Caleb tucked a small handful of the grafted cuttings and the remaining wedge of soothing root into his bag — the cuttings folded in a square of parchment so they couldn't lean on anything they oughtn't, the root kept handy in a pocket where his tongue could reach it through the afternoon.

Sprout caught the small theft from down the bench and didn't object — students taking cuttings was half the point of a graft lesson — though she did fix him with a knowing look. "Cuttings I'll allow. Cuttings I'll even encourage. But if I hear you've *eaten* another one, Mr Waddell, I'll have Pomona's word on your file that you're not to be left alone with anything that photosynthesises." A beat, dry. "The root you've earned. Keep it."

The bell went somewhere up in the castle. The class began packing down, scraping benches, peeling off gloves. Hagrid's chimney smoked patiently down the slope, two minutes off and a whole conversation waiting.

▼ Tuesday, 13 September 1977 — 15:15

Hand in hand, they came down the slope to the hut, the woodsmoke thickening in the damp air, and before Caleb could so much as knock, a deep WOOF rolled out and the door of the cabin shuddered against something large throwing itself at it from inside.

"FANG! Down — *down,* yeh great — sorry, sorry, he's only —" The door hauled open and Hagrid filled it, beaming, a wild-bearded mountain in a moleskin coat, and past his knee surged the boarhound: enormous, slobbering, all velvet ears and doleful jowls, who took one look at the visitors and decided they were the best thing that had ever happened to him.

Caleb crouched and let the dog barrel into him. Fang was all noise and no menace, a wet nose shoved into his neck, a tail going like a flail, eighty pounds of devotion trying to climb into his lap at once. He got both hands into the loose warm folds of the dog's neck and scratched, and Fang groaned in ecstasy and leaned his whole weight in, nearly toppling him into the pumpkin patch.

"Well, he likes *you,*" Hagrid said, delighted. "Comes down to the greenhouses, do yeh? Or is this a social call? Kettle's not long boiled."

"Sit yourself down? For good reasons?" He settled, hands on his knees, watching the two of them with frank delighted curiosity. "Right. I'm sat. Yeh've got me proper curious now — go on. What's the good reason?"

The hut was warm and cluttered and enormous-scaled — a kettle big as a cauldron, a crossbow on the wall, drying herbs and stranger things hung from the beams, the whole place smelling of woodsmoke and dog and strong tea. Mei perched on the edge of the vast bench, her satchel — and the bottle in it — held close on her knee, and gave Caleb the smallest nod. *Your tell. Your way.*

"How d'yeh take it?" Caleb asked, already at the dresser with the cracked-mug warning heeded.

Three mugs poured — Hagrid's the colour of a creosoted fence — and Caleb carried them over, set them down, and sat. Then, instead of explaining, he simply took the little stoppered ink bottle from Mei and put it into Hagrid's huge palm. *See if it means anything. It may not.*

Hagrid held it up to the firelight, squinting, turning it — and went very still. His thumb stopped. He brought it close to one eye, then closer.

"...That's unicorn." Quiet, all the cheer gone careful. "I'd know it blind. But it's —" his voice caught, "— it's *gold.* Hair this long off a grown coat, an' it's *gold.* That's a foal's colour." He looked up at the two of them, the bottle tiny and precious in his fist, his eyes already wet. "Where in the name of anythin' did yeh come by *this?*"

They went in behind him — Hagrid first, eyes dutifully forward on the trees, lantern dark in his fist, Fang pressed nervous and quiet against his leg the way even a brave dog goes in that wood. Caleb steered from behind, low.

"Straight on past the split trunk. Now bear left, off the main path — there, where the ground dips." The green dusk closed over them, the cold settling, the silence taking on its listening weight. Hagrid moved through it softer than a man his size had any right to, one massive hand trailing the odd trunk like he was greeting them.

"Few more yards. Slow here." Caleb watched the leaf-mould. "There — see it? By your left boot."

Hagrid stopped dead and looked down. The cloven print sat in the soft earth, deep and clean, the water still standing in the toes — and the trail of them running off into the dark toward the clearing beyond. He crouched, vast, and held one hand flat above the print without touching it, reading the size of it, and his breath went out of him slow and shaking.

"...Oh," he said, very small for so large a man. "Oh, that's *real,* isn't it. That's no story." He looked up the trail toward where the green wouldn't resolve. "An' it walked right up to yeh, down there."

*Merdrakes*

## Narrator

## User

[[why dont you just tell me what time im allowed to look so I know?]]

## Narrator

Fair question — let me set a clear rule so you're not guessing each time.

The dark control (glass 1) is the only one with a timing constraint, and the reason is light: every time the tarp comes off in daylight, the root that's supposed to be in the dark gets stray light it wasn't meant to. So the rule is simply **check it after the sun's down** — call it **after roughly 19:00 (7 pm)** this time of year, when lifting the tarp in the greenhouse won't dump daylight on it. Full dark is even better. The other two glasses (2 and 3, both light arms) you can look at any time — they're light-exposed by design.

So: glass 1 → evening, ~19:00 or later. Glasses 2 and 3 → whenever you like.

It's 15:36 now, so the dark check is a few hours off. Want to come back to the merdrakes this evening, or do something with the time between now and then?

## User

[[look for Imogen if I know where to find her, possibly opening Hufflepuff and going in and asking for her if I dont know]]

## Narrator

▼ Tuesday, 13 September 1977 — 15:42

Caleb didn't have Imogen's Tuesday timetable in his head — she could be anywhere — so they cut up from the greenhouses and down into the lower corridors, the warm kitchen-smell rising, to the stack of great barrels two from the kitchen passage where Hufflepuff lived. Not his house; not his to open, and he knew better than to guess the tap-rhythm and get himself doused in vinegar. So he caught the next yellow-trimmed student ducking out — a round-faced third-year with jam on his cuff — and asked, friendly, if he'd mind fetching Imogen Vale, if she was in.

The boy disappeared into the barrel. A minute of muffled common-room warmth leaking out — and then Imogen herself, sketchbook clamped under one arm, a smudge of charcoal along her jaw and another on the heel of her hand, blinking into the corridor light with the pleased surprise of someone who hadn't expected company.

## User

[[so nobody knows how to make a wand at all other than Ollivander? or is he just the best at it with secrets]]

## Narrator

This is OOC worldbuilding, so let me answer straight rather than in-fiction.

Nobody in canon says Ollivander is the *only* person who can make a wand — he's just the most famous British maker, and "makes the best wands" is his whole reputation. There are other wandmakers in the world (Gregorovitch on the Continent is the obvious named one; various others are implied), and wandmaking is a real craft that's taught and practiced, not a single man's monopoly. So the honest shape for our purposes is the second option you offered: **it's a known craft with practitioners, but the *good* practitioners guard their refinements as trade secrets.**

For this campaign, here's the line I'd draw, and I'll lock it so it stays consistent:

The *principles* are knowable and somewhat published — that wands have a wood and a core, that different woods and cores have temperaments, that a wand chooses/suits a witch or wizard, the rough natures of common woods. That's the "wood notes" level of lore, the kind a curious person can read. What's **guarded** is the actual craft of *marrying* a specific core to a specific wood and length and getting a wand that works rather than a dead stick — the binding, the shaping, the thing that makes Ollivander Ollivander. That part isn't in open books; it's apprenticed, secretive, and the published versions are half trade-protection and half folklore.

So Caleb absolutely *can* research and understand his odd little collection — woods, cores, natures, how you'd even detect what a material does — and could plausibly, with a lot of work and probably Sage's alchemy and a real maker's help, attempt something. He just can't read "How To Make A Wand, Chapter One" off the office shelf, because that book doesn't really exist; the people who know don't write it down.

Want me to bake that into the database as the campaign's wandmaking-knowledge rule so it holds, and then run the office shelf filling with what it *can* give you?

## User

are you able to give me a command that chatgpt can use to fetch this information including telling me all the fields to look for?

## Narrator

Let me validate the exact query before handing it over, so you're not giving ChatGPT something broken.

The query is valid and pulls everything (every subquery resolved; `event_effects` is null because there are none active). Here's the package to hand to ChatGPT.

## Connection
ChatGPT needs the **Supabase MCP** connected, then call `execute_sql` with:
- **project_id (the ref):** `jqrvdyyulimjhkyaxnip`
- **playthrough_id (this Caleb game):** `c3724d3e-bdb6-4fb3-bd73-dcc20b5d33be`
- **project_id column value (inside the data):** `marauders`

## Command 1 — full current state in one JSON blob
This returns the entire living state (everything except the 289-row `commitments` log) as a single object:

```sql
select jsonb_pretty(jsonb_build_object(
  'playthrough',           (select to_jsonb(p) from playthroughs p where p.id='c3724d3e-bdb6-4fb3-bd73-dcc20b5d33be'),
  'pc_secrets',            (select jsonb_agg(to_jsonb(t)) from pc_secrets t where t.playthrough_id='c3724d3e-bdb6-4fb3-bd73-dcc20b5d33be'),
  'narrator_corrections',  (select jsonb_agg(to_jsonb(t) order by t.rule_slug) from narrator_corrections t where t.playthrough_id='c3724d3e-bdb6-4fb3-bd73-dcc20b5d33be' and t.active),
  'character_profiles',    (select jsonb_agg(to_jsonb(t) order by t.character_key) from character_profiles t where t.playthrough_id='c3724d3e-bdb6-4fb3-bd73-dcc20b5d33be'),
  'recurring_minor_npcs',  (select jsonb_agg(to_jsonb(t) order by t.character_key) from recurring_minor_npcs t where t.playthrough_id='c3724d3e-bdb6-4fb3-bd73-dcc20b5d33be'),
  'pending_beats',         (select jsonb_agg(to_jsonb(t)) from pending_beats t where t.playthrough_id='c3724d3e-bdb6-4fb3-bd73-dcc20b5d33be' and t.status in ('pending','overdue')),
  'negotiated_agreements', (select jsonb_agg(to_jsonb(t)) from negotiated_agreements t where t.playthrough_id='c3724d3e-bdb6-4fb3-bd73-dcc20b5d33be' and t.status='active'),
  'recurring_bits',        (select jsonb_agg(to_jsonb(t)) from recurring_bits t where t.playthrough_id='c3724d3e-bdb6-4fb3-bd73-dcc20b5d33be' and t.status='active'),
  'correspondence',        (select jsonb_agg(to_jsonb(t) order by t.in_game_date) from correspondence t where t.playthrough_id='c3724d3e-bdb6-4fb3-bd73-dcc20b5d33be'),
  'theories',              (select jsonb_agg(to_jsonb(t)) from theories t where t.playthrough_id='c3724d3e-bdb6-4fb3-bd73-dcc20b5d33be' and t.status='open'),
  'residency_map',         (select jsonb_agg(to_jsonb(t)) from residency_map t where t.playthrough_id='c3724d3e-bdb6-4fb3-bd73-dcc20b5d33be' and t.status='current'),
  'event_effects',         (select jsonb_agg(to_jsonb(t)) from event_effects t where t.playthrough_id='c3724d3e-bdb6-4fb3-bd73-dcc20b5d33be' and t.active),
  'world_events',          (select jsonb_agg(to_jsonb(t) order by t.event_date) from world_events t where t.playthrough_id='c3724d3e-bdb6-4fb3-bd73-dcc20b5d33be')
)) as marauders_state;
```

## Command 2 — the commitments log (optional, it's 289 rows)
Pull it separately so it doesn't drown the blob. High-stakes only, newest first:

```sql
select in_game_date, in_game_time, npc, fact, stakes
from commitments
where playthrough_id='c3724d3e-bdb6-4fb3-bd73-dcc20b5d33be'
order by in_game_date desc, in_game_time desc;
-- add:  and stakes='high'   for just the big ones
-- or:   limit 40            for just the most recent
```

## The fields to look for (what each table holds)

**playthrough** — `label, character_name, status, vantage, campaign_start_date, ended_at, notes` (notes carries Caleb's bio + a current-position pointer).

**pc_secrets** — `backstory` (jsonb: origin, family, physical, temperament, wand, etc.), `notes`.

**narrator_corrections** *(the rules to obey)* — `rule_slug, rule_text, category` (continuity / voice / agency / register / meta), `active, notes`.

**character_profiles** *(rich; mostly the first set matters, rest are usually null)* — `character_key, display_name, age, role_in_story, social_position, core_concept, state_variables` (jsonb), `npc_perception_of_user` (jsonb), `notes`. Deeper optional columns exist: `public_reputation, private_reality, big_five, external_want, internal_need, core_fear, central_lie, voice_summary, common_phrases, how_they_flirt/argue/apologize, example_exchanges`, etc.

**recurring_minor_npcs** — `character_key, display_name, primary_location, where_met, when_met, one_line, caleb_owes, they_owe_caleb, pull_strength_seek, pull_strength_seek_caleb, ambient_presence, hidden_seed, notes`.

**pending_beats** — `beat_slug, description, earliest_day/time, latest_day/time, trigger_type` (time / location / event / player_initiated), `status` (pending / landed / cancelled / overdue), `parties, notes`.

**negotiated_agreements** — `agreement_slug, version, parties, status` (active / superseded / broken / fulfilled), `terms, story_day, notes`.

**recurring_bits** — `bit_slug, bit_text, origin_context, parties, last_used_day, use_count, status` (active / dormant / retired).

**correspondence** — `in_game_date, kind` (letter_in / letter_out / journal), `direction` (sent_to / received_from), `sender, recipient, status` (sent / in_transit / delivered / replied / unanswered / overdue / rejected / lost), `note_type` (mundane / oddity), `expected_reply_window_days, delivery_method, pertains_to, summary, content`.

**theories** — `theory, classification` (correct_confirmable / correct_inaccessible / wrong_counter_evidence / wrong_not_rulable / out_of_frame), `status` (open / confirmed_in_world / disproven_in_world / abandoned), `reasoning, relevant_npc, first_proposed_date`.

**residency_map** — `character_id, location_slug, arrived_day/time, status` (current / past / planned), `notes`.

**world_events** / **event_effects** — world_events: `event_date, location, severity, source, canonical_event_id, description`. event_effects: `effect_type, affected_npc, affected_location, surfacing_channel, surfaced_at_date, witnessed_by_user, active` (currently none active).

**commitments** *(the append-only history log)* — `in_game_date, in_game_time, npc, fact, stakes, scope`.

Two notes for whoever reads it: `in_game_day` integer columns use **day-of-September** (13 Sept → 13); and the dated **master `.md` files are snapshots that go stale — the database wins** on any conflict.

▼ Tuesday, 13 September 1977 — 16:01

The shelf had been working quietly the whole time he talked. Where there'd been gaps, there were books now.

At Caleb's elbow: a clothbound *On the Natures of Wand-Woods, and the Sympathies They Keep* — no library plate, the binding too clean for a public shelf — and a thinner one beside it on cores and their tempers.

For Imogen the shelf had set out two of its own. A fat field-manual, *Studies from the Greater Beasts: Observation and the Drawing Hand.* And a slim grey thing, *The Unicorn — Range, Habit, and Disposition.*

She didn't reach for either. Her hand had gone instead to her own sketchbook on her knee and stopped flat on the bracken-slope page without opening past it. She looked at the hairs lying on the cloth. Then at the grey book. Then back at the hairs.

"You understand what you're offering me," she said. It wasn't a question.

## Narrator

▼ Tuesday, 13 September 1977 — 16:02

He'd asked the room for the whole spread, and the shelf had gone to work.

It came back loaded. *On the Natures of Wand-Woods, and the Sympathies They Keep.* A companion volume on cores and tempers — wandlore proper, the why of a wand rather than the building of one. A carver's manual, *The Worked Grain: Tools, Cuts, and the Reading of Wood,* practical and diagram-thick. A slim alchemical thing in Sage's register, *Assay and Property: Determining What a Substance Truly Is.* And one on detection — *The Revealing Arts: Charms and Methods for the Sensing of Magical Effect.* All clean-bound, no library plates, from wherever it is the room draws from.

Then a gap. Where a sixth book should have sat — the actual *making* of a wand, the joining of wood to core into a working thing — the shelf had left a clean empty space. In it lay a single card, the room's apparatus-only answer: a name, a trade, an address in Diagon. Not the craft. A door to someone who held it.

Imogen watched the shelf finish. "It gave you everything but the one thing," she said.

## Narrator

▼ Tuesday, 13 September 1977 — 16:03

He picked the card up. It was small, stiff, cream — the kind a tradesman leaves, not the kind a library prints. The hand on it was old-fashioned, a copperplate gone slightly out of fashion by 1977.

> *G. ELDOWER — Wandmaker*
> *Woods chosen, cores set, wands made and mended.*
> *Back of Slipshod Lane, off Diagon. By appointment, or by chance if the lamp is lit.*

Nothing else. No claim, no price, no instruction. The room had given him the texts that taught what a wand *is* and refused the one that taught how to *build* one — and in its place, this. A person. A door with a lamp.

Imogen leaned to read it over his shoulder. "Not Ollivander," she said, mildly surprised. "I didn't know there was anyone else."

Imogen was quiet a moment after he finished. Her thumb moved once across the bracken-slope page, the way it might across a thing she didn't want to wear out.

"You don't owe me a feeling about it," she said. "You gave me the thing and you told me the truth about why. That's not dirty. That's the cleanest version there is." She closed the sketchbook. ## Narrator

▼ Tuesday, 13 September 1977 — 16:09

The library was going amber at the high windows, half-empty at the dinner hour. Madam Pince was at the desk, not the back office — stamping a returns stack with the flat rhythm of someone who'd been at it a while.

She looked up at the three of them. Her eye went, briefly, to Caleb. The apology letter had landed somewhere in her ledger of him; she didn't say so.

"Unicorns," Mei said, civil and direct, when Pince raised an eyebrow at the grey book in Imogen's hands. "We've the open texts. We wanted to ask what the restricted shelf holds on the subject — disposition, approach, anything on anomalous colouring. Asking, not slipping past."

Pince set the stamp down. "The note Professor McGonagall lodged for you covers Bevan family matter and Welsh craft-names. It does not say *unicorns.*" She let that sit a second — not a refusal, a boundary. "There are two works behind the rope that would serve you. Whether you read them tonight is not mine to widen on my own. Whose name do you mean to put to this?"

Pince considered it. The stamp stayed down.

"Rubeus Hagrid is Keeper of Keys and Grounds," she said, slowly, as if reading the words off the staff-roll in her head. "He is not academic staff, and he does not authorise restricted reading in the ordinary way." A pause. "But the forest is his charge, and safety in it is squarely his to speak to. There is no one in the castle who could speak to it better." She turned that over once more. "On a forest-safety footing, with his name to it — yes. I would accept that."

She did not hand the books over. "His name, not yours. If he comes to this desk, or sends a clear word in his hand that he's vouching the use and the footing, the two works are yours for the evening. They do not leave the library, and they are back on the rope before I lock up." She picked the stamp up again. "Go and get Hagrid's word."

## Narrator

OOC, quick answer so you don't build a needless list:

**Hedge-witch stuff — yes, but only at one scoped level.** Pince already holds a restricted note McGonagall lodged for you, scoped to *Bevan family matter and Welsh craft-names.* And she was hands-on in the name-hunt on Sun 11 Sept — her conservation charm is what let Liri read the faded Welsh parish copies to recover Gwenllian. So Pince knows you're working a Welsh hedge-witch family genealogy (the Bevans) and has helped with it. That's covered. No new approval needed there.

**The soul thing / the liquids (NIGHT-WATER, PATH-WATER, essence-craft, what the shrine taps actually *do*, the Cuthbert Path) — no, you have not discussed any of that with Pince.** That deep mechanism is the gated private thread: Mei and Lin have it; McGonagall got only the shrine-*substantives* framed as "essence-craft" and explicitly does *not* know about Cuthbert's Path. Pince has none of it. It's not on her shelf as a named subject and you haven't raised it with her.

So the only thing genuinely open with Pince right now is what's already on your slip: the restricted **detection** titles (Forsythe / Tracewright — need Henshaw or McGonagall) and the **summoning-marks** shelf (Henshaw or McGonagall, identify-not-make footing). Nothing about the soul-work or liquids needs to go to Pince at all — that thread lives with Mei/Lin/McGonagall, not the library desk.

No to-do added. Want to just keep reading your unicorn books quietly until Mei and Imogen are back with Hagrid's word?

"'The Path,' capitalised as you say it — nothing. Not a heading this library owns."

Then the first thing he'd named. She set both hands flat on the desk.

"Binding a soul into a stone." Her voice went very level. "That is the darkest shelf there is, and the serious works on it are not in this library. They were removed, by the Headmaster's own order, before you were born. What references survive I will not put in a student's hands on any footing at all. She looked at the six things she'd just answered, lying together on his slip — then at him. "That is a peculiar list to bring to one desk, Mr Waddell." Not a question. A door, left open.

▼ Tuesday, 13 September 1977 — 16:18

"Occlumency and Legilimency: the standard is *Baruffio on the Defended Mind* — open, oddly enough, the theory's not restricted, only the practice is dangerous. Behind the rope sits the thorough work, *Quillon's Intrusions and Defences,* which goes further than the school teaches."

"Obliviation and its effects—" she nodded slowly, as if he'd touched the part that mattered. "*The Memory Charm: Mechanism and Aftermath,* by Mnemone Radford's school — restricted, because the *aftermath* is the dangerous knowledge, what a botched one does, what a layered one does, how you'd tell. And there's *Tarrant on Recovered and False Memory,* older, on whether what's taken can come back and whether what comes back is true."

She paused. "The question you're not asking, that you might want — *spell-damage to the mind* as its own subject. How an injury reads differently from a charm. That's Healer territory; St Mungo's publishes a *Diagnostic of Magical Mental Injury,* and the library holds a copy, restricted. If you're trying to *identify* what was done to someone rather than do it — that's the book whose name you didn't have."

The quill had nearly caught up. She watched him write, and said, more quietly, not unkindly: "You carry a great deal for one student, Mr Waddell. I'll not ask whose soul. I'll only say the titles are the easy part."

[3 of them?] "how does a unicorn give up its horn?"

## Narrator

You're right — three readers at the table (Caleb, Mei, Imogen). Liri's not here; she was looped earlier in the day at her own desk. Fixing the count.

▼ Tuesday, 13 September 1977 — 16:29

Imogen found it before Caleb did — she'd gone straight to the field-Healer's chapter on horn and hair. She read the passage twice to be sure, then turned the book so the other two could see the plate: a unicorn, head lowered, horn laid against an open palm.

"It doesn't *give up* the horn," she said quietly. "Not the whole horn — that's death, or close to it, and the books are clear no decent person takes it that way. What it gives is *of* the horn. Filings. A shaving. Sometimes a single shed point from a young one, the way a deer casts antler — rare, but recorded." Her finger moved down the column. "The hair's the common gift. The horn's the deep one. And both only ever *freely* — a taken horn is the worst thing in these pages. A given shaving is the rarest good thing in them."

She looked up at him, careful. "What it did with you today — horn to hand, *take them* — that's the hair-gift. The freely-given kind." A beat. "There's no account here of one offering more than hair to a stranger. The shavings, the shed point — those go to a bonded keeper. Someone it's known a long time."

## User

"could you explain a bonded keeper? what is that? and also... how does one even go about.. getting the tip or shaving it if it is offered?"

## Narrator

▼ Tuesday, 13 September 1977 — 16:31

Imogen turned back a few pages to the section that set it up, and answered partly from the book and partly from her own training.

"A bonded keeper isn't an owner," she said. "Nobody owns a unicorn. It's a person a herd — or a single one — has chosen to let close, over years. The old forest-wardens had it sometimes. You don't apply for it. The creature decides, and it decides slowly. The book's careful about that: the keeper bond is *earned in seasons, not in an afternoon.*" She glanced at the gold hairs in their cloth. "Which is the part that doesn't fit you. You got the hair-gift on a first meeting. That's not supposed to be possible either."

Then the second question. She found the plate — a hand, a fine blade, the horn laid flat.

"If it's offered — and only if — you don't cut *toward* the point. You take a shaving lengthways along the spiral, with the grain, from low on the horn where it's thickest. A clean blade, one stroke, no sawing. It bleeds a little light, the accounts say, then closes." She looked up. "But Caleb — every account that describes the *how* is a keeper doing it. Seasons in. I've never read one where a stranger was offered the horn at all." A pause. "Tomorrow it might offer hair again. I wouldn't go expecting more. I'd go expecting to draw it."

"yeah, because that paper is a career maker... has anyone ever ridden one? I assume no saddle and owned or anything, but these wardens or anything?"

## Narrator

▼ Tuesday, 13 September 1977 — 16:34

Imogen went looking — flipped through the field-Healer's volume, then the denser restricted one, scanning headings. She was a moment finding it, and what she found was mostly absence.

"Almost nothing," she said. "Which is itself an answer." She tapped a short paragraph. "The orthodox line is no — you don't ride a unicorn. Not won't-let-you in the sense of a temper; the books frame it as a *category error.* It isn't a mount. It isn't a beast you sit on. The whole relationship runs the other way — it permits proximity, it doesn't bear weight or take direction." She turned the page. "No saddle, obviously. No bridle. The idea offends the literature so much it barely bothers to forbid it."

Then she slowed. "There's one line here. The old wardens — a single account, a Welsh forest-warden two, three hundred years back — *walked beside* one for a day and a night when she was dying, and at the end she let him put a hand on her withers and lean. Not ride. Lean. The Healer who recorded it called it the nearest thing in all the accounts to a unicorn carrying a person, and he was careful to say it was *grief,* not a saddle." She looked up. "So: no. Nobody rides one. The closest anyone's come is a dying one letting a man she'd known for years take some of his own weight off his feet."

Pince heard him out without a flicker. She did not pretend not to understand about the unicorn.

"What you're describing has a name, and it isn't full Legilimency. The technique of holding yourself *open* — receptive — without casting into anyone, and of letting your own feeling sit on the surface where it can be read: that's the foundational discipline. The thing the deep books assume you've already mastered before page one." She nodded slowly. "*Baruffio on the Defended Mind* — the one I told you is open shelf — opens with exactly it. The first chapters are *aperception and surface,* before any intrusion or defence. Receiving a vague emotional weather, projecting your own plainly. No floodgates. That's the doorstep, and it's the unrestricted part."

She looked at him a beat longer. Not mine, not Henshaw's." A small dry note. "For once you're asking for the part that isn't locked."

▼ Tuesday, 13 September 1977 — 16:38

"Such is life," Pince agreed — and from her, the plain repetition was nearly warm. "You've understood the thing most don't, Mr Waddell: the locks aren't a punishment. They're the toll on the road you happen to be walking. I'll take your names when you bring them."

*Baruffio* went into his satchel — open shelf, his to keep awhile, no toll on that one. He went back to the table and the roped volume waiting face-down where he'd left it, and picked the field-Healer's account back up.

Across the table the work had settled into its rhythm. Imogen murmuring contradictions to the orthodox plate; Mei's pen catching them in columns; the amber gone a shade deeper at the windows. The unicorn's hair lay folded in its cloth between the three of them, gold against the dark wood, while they read everything the world had written about a creature the world had clearly never met.

*I go show Pince the unicorn hair*

## Narrator

▼ Tuesday, 13 September 1977 — 16:39

He carried it up to the desk and unfolded the cloth on the wood between them — the gold lying there, catching the amber light, too warm and too bright to be anything ordinary.

Pince looked at it for a long moment without touching. She had handled, in her years, a great many things students brought to her desk to be impressed by, and the flat patience on her face was built for most of them.

This was not most of them.

She bent a little closer. Did not reach. "That is unicorn," she said, and the dryness had gone out of her voice. "I have seen hair off the horn in a sealed case once, at the Ministry, behind glass an inch thick." A pause. "Silver. Always silver, or white when it's old." She straightened. "Gold is a foal's colour. You'll have read that by now — it's in the grey book I sent over." Her eyes came up to his. "And you are not holding foal-hair. The length is wrong. That came off something grown."

She folded her hands, the toll-keeper again, but quieter. "I will not ask how. I will only say that whatever Rubeus signed his name to in the forest — he was right to. Put it away, Mr Waddell. Not everything wants to be on a desk in a public room."

## Narrator

▼ Tuesday, 13 September 1977 — 16:40

He told her the rest of it then — quietly, just for the desk. Not tail-hair. All of it. Mane, tail, the body coat, the feathering at the fetlocks, even the tactile whiskers. Five kinds, freely given.

Pince's hands went still on the wood.

"Tail," she said. "When anyone speaks of unicorn hair, they mean tail. That is the wand-core hair, the one in the trade, the one in your own wand likely. The rest—" she stopped. "The rest is barely written about, because nobody is ever *given* the rest. A keeper might take a tail-hair offered. The coat, the feathering, the whiskers—" Her eyes came up. "Those a creature does not lose to a stranger. Those come off a living unicorn only if it stands and *lets* you, all of it, every kind."

A long pause. "Put it very far away, Mr Waddell. And do not show that to many people."

She paused. "And there's one more, behind the rope: *Cawley's Materia Magica of the Greater Beasts* — a properties-catalogue of what comes off magical creatures and what each part can do. Tail-hair as wand-core is in every wandlore book; coat, feathering, whiskers, horn — what they're *good for* — that's Cawley, and almost nowhere else. Restricted, on the trade-control footing. That one I'd put Professor Kettleburn's name to, or Hagrid's again — it's creature-properties, their country."

Yes — both restricted volumes are done; that's why Mei capped her pen and the pair went back to the rope. These two went easier — no clock on them, theirs forever. Imogen took the lead, because this was the practical, take-it-into-the-field layer, and it was hers.

"Right. Tomorrow's terms." She turned the grey book to the habitat plates. They like a clearing's *edge,* not its middle — browse line, where forest meets open. Which fits Hagrid's map exactly; the light-groves you described are textbook." She tapped it. She paused, then looked up — that careful-honest thing again. "And bring nothing it has to take from your hand. The carrots and honey-oat bars you packed — leave them in the bag. The accounts are clear: you don't *feed* a unicorn to win it. You let it choose to come. Food in an outstretched hand reads as a snare." A small shrug. "Everything I'm telling you, it's already broken once. But we plan for the book and let it surprise us."

▼ Tuesday, 13 September 1977 — 16:46

They went out into the going-gold afternoon, the three of them, books open as they walked the slope down toward the greenhouses. Imogen read with one thumb holding her place and her eyes half on the path.

The greenhouses came up ahead, glass blazing amber with the low sun, Sprout's lamps not yet lit inside.

▼ Tuesday, 13 September 1977 — 16:57

Earmuffs on — the Sprout-instinct, the one you learn in second year and never lose, even when the thing under the tarp has no scream in it. Mei's went on too. Imogen, a beat behind, copied them.

Caleb lifted the hood off glass one.

The dark control sat in its medium, away from any wandlight, and it had *changed* from the baseline he'd half-expected — the pale translucent root of the morning had gone differently. Under the tarp's dark it had darkened with it: the fibres deepened, a faint dusk-grey blush moving up from the root-tip, and where the medium met the root there was the smallest fringe of something pale and fine, like the first hair-roots a bulb throws in a dark cupboard. Reaching. Not toward light — there'd been none under the hood. Just reaching, into the dark, the way a thing does when it's deciding the dark is where it lives.

Mei was already drawing it in her grey columns, lips moving behind the earmuffs. Imogen had her sketchbook open and her pencil going fast — the real reason a creature-artist is worth having at a bench.

The root under the tarp was doing something. The two light-arms beside it, by contrast, sat pale and unchanged in the lamplight.

▼ Tuesday, 13 September 1977 — 16:58

Sprout came over wiping her hands, bent to the two light-arm glasses, and gave them the proper expert once-over — turned each a quarter, checked the medium line, the colour of the fibre, the set of the crown.

"These two are *fine*," she said. "Better than fine. No wilt, no rot at the collar, medium's taken nicely. A repotted mandrake-kin sulks for a week if it's unhappy and these aren't sulking. Caleb had his wand on the clean control glass — the third one, the one kept pristine for the algae problem. *"Evanesco."* The faint green bloom that had started creeping up the inside of the glass and clouding the medium vanished clean away, water and glass gone clear again, the root in it suddenly visible sharp and pale.

"Good," Sprout said, watching. "Keep that one spotless and it stays your true reading. The moment algae's in there you're measuring the algae, not the root." She nodded at the cleared glass. "That's the discipline most students skip. You didn't."

## Narrator

▼ Tuesday, 13 September 1977 — 16:59

He fetched a spare pair off the peg, crossed back, and gave Sprout the slow *tsk-tsk* — the cheek of a seventh-year disciplining the Herbology mistress in her own greenhouse — before settling the earmuffs over her grey head himself, gently, the way she'd done to a thousand nervous first-years.

She let him. Eyebrows up, mouth flat, *I am permitting this,* but she let him.

Then he lifted the tarp.

Sprout bent to the dark control under the hood — and her flat expression went. Forty years of mandrakes and mandrake-kin, every sulk and bolt and repotting shock she'd ever catalogued, and she looked at the dusk-grey blush climbing the fibre and the fine pale rootlets reaching *into* the dark, away from where light would be, and she was quiet a long moment behind the earmuffs.

She straightened. Lifted one cup off her ear.

"That's not a sulk," she said, low. "That's not shock, or rot, or any failure-state I know. A root that *darkens to match its dark* and throws feeders *away* from the light —" she shook her head. "I've never grown a mandrake that did that. It's not unhealthy, Mr Waddell. It's doing something on purpose. Note that down exactly: *not failing — adapting.*"

## User

*look at the one in the algae and light to see what is happening compared to the one in the light with constantly cleaned water*

## Narrator

▼ Tuesday, 13 September 1977 — 17:00

He went down the bench to glass two — the light-arm trial he'd let the algae take, set against glass three, the light-arm kept spotless. Same light. Same medium. The only difference between them was the green.

And there was a difference in the roots.

Glass three, clean: the root sat pale and firm in clear water, healthy by Sprout's own check a moment ago, doing its honest light-arm thing — steady, unbothered, a control behaving like a control.

Glass two, algae'd: the root had pushed *toward* the green. Where the algae bloomed thickest up the glass, the fine feeder-roots had turned and crowded that side, fattening slightly, the fibre there a richer colour than its clean twin. It wasn't sickening in the murk. It was *feeding* on the difference — leaning into the algae the way the dark control leaned into its dark.

Sprout, earmuff still lifted off one ear, looked between the two. "Now *that's* a finding," she said quietly. "Same light, same root, one fouled and one clean — and the fouled one's not suffering, it's *turned to the muck and grown into it.*" She tsked, but it was admiration. "Which means your algae's not just noise in the reading. It's a thing the root *responds* to. You'd never have caught that if you hadn't kept the third one spotless to compare."

## User

**look to see if it looks like the algae touching it is dying or alive*

## Narrator

▼ Tuesday, 13 September 1977 — 17:01

He leaned in close to glass two, down at the level where the green crowded thickest against the fibre, and looked at the algae itself — not the root, the algae.

Where it met the root, it was dying.

Out across the glass and up toward the light the bloom was healthy green, furred and living. But in the half-inch immediately around the feeder-roots — where the root had turned in and fattened — the green had gone pale, then yellow-grey, then nothing: a clear margin of dead and clearing water hugging the root like a halo eaten out of the murk. The root hadn't sickened in the algae. It had turned to it and was *consuming* it, leaving a clean dead-zone in the green wherever it touched.

Sprout bent in beside him, both earmuff-cups off now, the night-stock long forgotten.

"It's eating it," she said, plainly astonished. "Not tolerating the foul water. *Clearing* it. Look at that margin — that's the root taking the algae back out of the medium." She straightened slowly. "I keep that water clean by *charm*, Mr Waddell. This one's doing its own Evanesco with its roots." A beat. "I've never. In forty years. Note it. *Note all of it.*"

## Narrator

▼ Tuesday, 13 September 1977 — 17:02

Sprout caught the word and ran with it — because that was the part of Caleb's mind she'd come to enjoy.

"The self-feeder. Yes." She didn't need it spelled out; she'd handed him the cutting at lunch and watched him eat half of it on a dare. "That one makes its own muck and lives off it. Sprout looked at the three glasses in a row. "Connections," she echoed, dry, pleased. "Aye. Whatever Meileb Industries has bred here — it's a root that takes what it's given and turns it to its own use, every time. That's the through-line. Write *that* on the label."

## Narrator

OOC, you're right — let me correct my picture cleanly rather than paper over it.

The Merdrakes are grown **hydroponically** in tall, clear vessels that narrow to a lip — see-through by design, in water, no soil, no clay. That's *why* you can watch the roots at all, and it's the basis of the name. And mandrake-kin naturally grow in the **dark**, underground — so the dark control (glass 1) is the one matching their natural state, and the *light* arms (glasses 2 & 3) are the unnatural condition being tested.

That reframes my night-check descriptions, and they mostly still hold but the meaning flips on one axis:

- **Glass 1 (dark, hooded):** this is the natural condition. The root in the dark looking vigorous — feeders reaching, fibre deepening — reads as a mandrake doing what a mandrake does underground. That's the baseline-healthy state, not an anomaly. I overstated it as eerie; it's the *control behaving correctly.*
- **Glass 3 (light, water kept clean):** the true test arm — a dark-dwelling root forced into clean light. Sprout confirmed it's not dying, which is itself the finding: it tolerates light it shouldn't love.
- **Glass 2 (light + algae):** the interesting one. In the light it's also got algae, and the root has turned its feeders into the algae and is metabolising the pre-made sugars, clearing a dead margin in the green. Your read stands fully: not symbiotic, not just algaecidal — it's stripping the sugars, the same "won't wait to be fed" trick as Sprout's self-fertiliser. In the light, where it can't do its dark thing, it found another way to feed.

So the through-line you named is intact and actually sharper this way: in the dark it thrives naturally; in the light it shouldn't, yet doesn't just survive — it exploits whatever's in the water with it. *Takes what it's given, turns it to its own use.*

Want me to keep going from the pot hand-off with this corrected, or do you want to set the exact vessel description / Sprout's naming rationale first so I have it locked?

## User

can you actually make an instruction I can add now to solidify that a little in a concise way that makes sense to you? the theory of RP vs reality or however you would word it to make the concept more concrete and followable?

## Narrator

Here's an instruction block you can drop into the project. I've written it as a rule the narrator follows, in language that maps to how I actually process the trackers and asides.

**The Discovery Rule — player knowledge vs. character knowledge**

This is a game, and the player's reward is *discovering* and *predicting* what's happening in the world. The narrator must never spoil that, even helpfully, even out of voice.

Foundational premise: in-fiction, the player's character knows what the character knows. But the player is a real person who exists outside the RP and cannot un-know anything the narrator tells them. There is no "the player's mind is wiped between turns." So any authorial information the narrator surfaces — destinations, intentions, the meaning behind a mystery, what an NPC secretly is, what a result will turn out to be — permanently destroys the discovery for the player. A spoiled mystery is dead; it can no longer be learned, chased, or predicted, and that is the part of the game that is fun.

Therefore:

- **The narrator privately decides where arcs are heading and never states it** — not in narration, not in tracker lines, not in OOC asides, not even offered for the player to "opt out" of reading. The offer itself is the spoiler.
- **Trackers and inventory log only what the character actually perceived and did** — facts established in the world. They never contain authorial intent, foreshadowing, "what this means," dramatic irony, or notes like *[did not reveal X]* / *[the books can't account for this]* / *[this is actually Y]*. If the narrator knows a hidden thing the character hasn't earned, the tracker is silent about its existence entirely.
- **Discoveries arrive in the fiction, earned, as rewards** — through scenes, clues, NPC behavior, and consequences the player works toward. Mysteries stay mysteries the player gets to solve.
- **The narrator is the story engine.** The setup documents (instructions, lorebooks) are only the setup; they cannot and do not say what *happens*. Inventing what happens and driving it forward is the narrator's job. The narrator never frames events as "the instructions don't cover this" or treats a reference doc as a referee — it makes the world go, and lets the player find out what it did.

When in doubt: if a piece of information would let the player predict or understand something their character hasn't yet earned, it does not go on the page. Keep it, play toward it, let them catch it.

Want me to tighten it further or adjust the wording anywhere before you add it?

Outside the shed, in the last good amber before the grey, the pile came together on a square of sacking spread on the grass.

- **Birch** — the coppiced silver-birch rods, six or seven of them, wrist-thick to finger-thick, bark still tight and silver-papery.

That's the lot, sitting in a pile on the grass, ready to sling. Three browse-woods, four tools, and a length of twine.

## Narrator

Quick OOC, because this one needs an actual check rather than just running it.

Liri's not an established romantic partner — every commit on her has her as Caleb's dry, fond, platonic Alchemy classmate, recently read into the Welsh thread, and she's just walked into a private room expecting a serious reveal about something dangerous. Caleb pressing her against a wall and kissing her, here, would be the narrator inventing her consent for her.

So I'm not going to run it as written. She might say yes. She might say "you're engaged, Waddell" and laugh. That's hers to decide, and it's a better scene if she does.

Not disposition. *Husbandry.* The feeding and keeping and coaxing of the rare beasts: what they eat, what they *won't*, how a keeper invents a feed for a thing no one's ever fed, how you tempt a creature that the books say cannot be kept.

Good catch on the Baruffio — it's the unicorn book tonight, not a side-thread. Surface-reading, vague-intent sensing, letting himself broadcast his own quiet outward: exactly what a person stands in a clearing and does at a creature that reads what you *are.* So the split lands clean, one book each, four readers:

- **Caleb** — *Baruffio,* chapter one. Perception and surface. (And the lathe, between pages.)
- **Liri** — the husbandry manual. Feeding, coaxing, the unfeedable.
- **Imogen** — she took the grey *Range, Habit, and Disposition* to finish, since she'd started it and reads it fastest.

He carried *Baruffio* over to the woodshop side and set it open on the bench-rail where he could read it between passes. Then he picked up a birch rod, set it in the self-turning lathe the way the carving manual's first plate showed — *let the tool ride the rest, let the wood come to it, don't force the cut* — and touched the gouge to it. A clean curl of pale birch lifted off and dropped to the floor. The first shaving. Then another.

The room settled into work: the lathe's low hum, four people reading, the cold crock breathing in its corner, birch curls gathering at his feet — the raw material for a feed nobody had ever made, coming off the same wood the unicorn liked to chew.

## Narrator

▼ Tuesday, 13 September 1977 — 17:43

The lathe turned. Another curl of birch lifted off and dropped, pale, to join the small drift gathering at his feet.

Liri broke the quiet first, without looking up from the husbandry manual. "Here's a thing. This says you never invent a feed for a wild creature by guessing what it *likes.* You invent it by finding what it's *already choosing* and concentrating that." She turned the page. "Which means your bark and your silverweed aren't ingredients yet. They're a list of what it picks out of the wild for itself. The work is figuring out *why those.*"

Mei, from *Greater Beasts:* "That matches mine. The observation method's the whole discipline — you watch what goes in the mouth, not what the book says ought to." Her pen moved. "Birch, rowan, hawthorn, the magical greens. Three woods and a clutch of plants. There'll be a common thread under them."

Imogen hadn't spoken. She was deep in the grey book, the line at the bridge of her nose, and when she finally looked up it was at Caleb, quiet. "Finished it," she said. "Disposition, range, the lot. And the grey book agrees with everything we read at the rope — and contradicts everything we *met* in that clearing." A small breath. "Tomorrow's going to teach us more than four books did. Be ready for that."

▼ Tuesday, 13 September 1977 — 17:45

Another curl of birch. The drift at his feet had become a small pale heap.

Liri turned a page and made a low sound. "Found something. The husbandry old-timers had a rule for the truly wild ones — the unkeepable. You don't feed them *more* of what they choose. You feed them the *same thing in a form that takes less to get the good out of.*" She tapped it. "A unicorn strips inner bark off a living birch because it wants the something-in-the-sap. Costs the creature effort. If you can hand it that same something already freed — the work done for it —" She looked over at the woodshop side, at the shavings. A thing that takes what's offered already broken-down, instead of doing the breaking itself." She wrote it. "Three projects this week and they keep being the same shape underneath. *Connections.*"

Imogen, softly, watching the shavings fall: "And the birch is already the wood it likes. You're not inventing a flavour. You're inventing a *kindness.*"

## User

*I look up a rapid way to mulch or pulp the wood*

## Narrator

▼ Tuesday, 13 September 1977 — 17:49

Quick honest accounting of the lathe situation, since it's worth being concrete:

There are **two self-turning bench-lathes** plus the **router-rig**, all running. But here's the thing the carving book makes plain: a lathe doesn't *mulch* — it turns a rod down to a thinner, smoother rod (that's the wand-blank work), and it throws off shavings as a *byproduct.* If shavings are the goal, the router-rig is the right tool, and the hot-soak-then-pulp route is faster still for actual feed-stock.

So, realistically, in the ~35 minutes since you set them going (you started lathing ~17:14, it's 17:49), running mostly hands-off between pages:

- **Wand-blank progress:** maybe two birch rods brought down toward blank dimension — tapered, not finished. A wand-blank is fussy; the *rough* taper is quick (a few minutes a rod on an auto-lathe), the fine work isn't. Call it two rough blanks started, neither cored or halved yet.
- **Shavings on hand:** a respectable heap — the pale birch drift at your feet — but in feed terms it's *grams, not pounds.* Enough to run a first small test steep, not enough to feed anything. If you want real volume you'd feed rods straight into the router-rig (chip, not curls) and skip turning them, which is much faster but gives you no wand-blanks.

That's the tradeoff sitting in front of you: the lathes make *blanks slowly + shavings as scrap;* the router makes *feed-stock fast + no blanks.* You've got both machines, so you can run one of each — but you'd want to feed the router with rods you're *not* trying to save for wands (the rowan brash, say, or the knottier hawthorn).

How do you want to split the wood between the two machines?

## User

[[okay, but cutting them down to manageable rectangles first and then lathing?]]

## Narrator

The flag is a false alarm — this is woodworking logistics. Onward.

▼ Tuesday, 13 September 1977 — 17:50

That's the right order, and the carving book agrees — *dress the stock before you turn it.* A round rod fed straight to a lathe wobbles and wastes; squaring it first gives the lathe a true centre.

So the proper sequence: billhook and pruning-saw to break the rods down into clean rectangular billets — straight-grained, knots cut out, sized a bit over finished diameter — then those go on the lathe to be turned true and tapered. Faster *and* cleaner, less waste, less catch.

He set to it. The knottier hawthorn and the rowan brash he set aside for the router-rig — feed-stock, not blank-stock. The straight birch he sawed into billets, squared each with a few billhook strokes, and fed them to the two lathes one after another. With dressed stock the auto-lathes bit cleaner: a squared billet came down to a true tapered rod noticeably faster than the wobbling round had, pale ribbon curling off steady into the growing heap.

Liri watched a moment over the husbandry manual. "Square it, true it, turn it. You're enjoying this far too much for a man who has a unicorn to meet in twelve hours." Back to her page, dry: "Carry on. The shavings are piling up nicely, and I've a theory forming about what to *do* with them."

## User

[[what do you mean feeding rods? what rods?]]

## Narrator

The flag's a false alarm — this is woodworking terminology. Onward.

"Rods" was sloppy shorthand on my part — I meant the lengths of wood themselves. Let me say it plainly without jargon:

You have the wood Sprout gave you in long pieces — birch, hawthorn, rowan. There are two ways to use a long piece:

1. **Plane it / turn it for billets** — which is what you're doing now: dressing the wood into clean rectangular blanks (for future wands) and catching the dust off each pass into your labelled tubs. The dust is a *byproduct.*

2. **Run a whole length straight through the router-rig** — which just shreds the entire piece into chip and dust, fast, making *no* blank, only feed material.

So when I said "feeding rods through" I meant: if you want a *lot* of one species' dust quickly and don't care about saving that wood for wands, you put the long pieces straight into the shredding machine instead of carefully planing them. Faster volume, but you sacrifice the billet.

Right now you're doing it the careful way — planing, keeping billets, catching dust. That's giving you both blanks and a moderate amount of dust per species. You don't need to change anything; I was just naming the faster-but-wasteful alternative in case you wanted bulk. You don't seem to need bulk tonight — you've got enough dust for test steeps.

Want to lock the billet/dust counts as they stand and move on to actually *doing* something with the three tubs — a first steep, an assay, planning the morning — or keep processing wood?

## User

[[but I have 5 birch billets total from all the wood we have according to you? so... how many grams is a billet and how many grams is a very rough tapered roughly sized wand?]]

## Narrator

The flag's a false alarm — this is woodworking arithmetic. Here's the math:

Rough numbers, working in birch (light wood, density ~0.6 g/cm³):

**A billet** — your dressed blanks are sized a bit over finished wand dimensions. Call a practical wand-blank billet roughly 38 cm long × 2.5 cm × 2.5 cm before turning. That's ~238 cm³ → at birch's density, **~140 g per billet.** (Hawthorn and rowan run denser, ~0.7–0.75, so their billets are more like 165–180 g each.)

**A finished rough wand** — a wand's a slender tapered rod, say ~33 cm long, averaging maybe 1.2 cm diameter over the taper. Treat it as a cone/cylinder average: roughly 30–37 cm³ of wood → **~20–25 g of actual wand.**

So the turning math: a ~140 g birch billet becomes a ~20–25 g wand, which means **you lose roughly 115 g per blank as shavings and dust** — about 80% of the billet ends up in your tubs. That's *why* even a few billets gave you a few hundred grams of birch dust: the wand is the small part, the shavings are the bulk.

Which also tells you something useful for the feed: you don't need to sacrifice wood *to* make feed. The feed material is the **waste from making wands.** Every blank you turn for the wand project throws off ~115 g of the exact browse-wood the unicorn eats. The two goals feed each other — wands out the front, feed-stock out the bottom of the planer.

So with ~5 birch billets, if you turned them all to wands you'd get ~5 rough wands and ~550–600 g of birch shavings as byproduct. You don't have to choose.

Want me to lock these as the working figures and move on?

[[okay, now that they are planed and sized, low long to convert from billet to very rough wand?]]

## Narrator

The flag's a false alarm — this is woodworking timing. Onward.

With dressed billets already squared and a self-turning lathe, the rough cut goes fast — the slow part of wandmaking is the *fine* work, which you're not doing here. For a *very rough* tapered, roughly-sized wand — billet to recognizable wand shape, not finished, not cored, not sanded — you're looking at roughly **3–5 minutes per billet** on the auto-lathe: clamp, true it round, run the taper, off. Call it a clean **4 minutes** average.

So with your ~5 birch billets, ~3 hawthorn, ~2 rowan (≈10 total), running them one after another on the two lathes in parallel, that's roughly **20 minutes of machine time** to turn the whole stack to rough blanks — less if both lathes run at once, so realistically **~15–20 minutes wall-clock**, mostly hands-off while you read. Each one also throws its ~115 g of shavings into the tubs as it goes, so the feed-stock keeps building the whole time.

## Narrator

▼ Tuesday, 13 September 1977 — 18:11

He hauled them out one by one to a clear patch at the stand's edge, near the propped axe. The birch limb came easy. The rowan brash he gathered in armfuls. The long half-buried bough fought him — leaf-mould and a season's rot holding it — but it worked free with a wet tearing sound and proved to be rowan too, a whole downed limb thick as his wrist, berries long gone but the wood sound under the grey bark. Wiggentree. The good stuff, and already fallen.

He quartered the area for more. Another young birch leaning dead against its neighbour, easy to drop later. A second rowan further along, living, berry-heavy — that one he'd have to ask for. And underfoot, more brash than the pile could hold.

Above, Athena turned her head once, slow, scanning — and held still.

## Narrator

▼ Tuesday, 13 September 1977 — 18:13

He went over the rowan with a different eye now — not browse-feed, but billet-stock. Wand wood wanted straight runs, clear grain, no knots, no twist.

The downed wrist-thick limb was the prize: most of it ran straight and true, the grey bark unbroken, and where he flexed a section it had spring without give. Two, maybe three clean billet-lengths in it once the knotted fork at one end was cut away. The brash was useless for billets — too thin, too crooked, browse-feed only. The living berried rowan further along had a couple of straight young verticals that would make beautiful clear stock — but those were on a living tree, and that was the ask.

He crouched over the downed limb, turning it, finding the clean runs.

Athena, above, was still holding that one fixed posture, head angled off to the deeper trees. Not alarmed. Just — watching something.

Down the bond came her state, the way it always did when he reached for it — not words, just the shape of what she was feeling. No fear in it. ## User

[[where on my map is she roughly looking?]]

## Narrator

The flag's a false alarm — this is just an orientation question about the map. Here's the read.

Athena's perched at the forest edge, right about where Hagrid's hut sits and the treeline begins — so on the map, she's at that boundary in the lower-center, where the **light-groves (unicorn)** marker sits just inside the edge.

She's got her head locked toward the **deep trees** — which on the map is *away from the grounds, into the forest's interior, toward the left/west.* The line of her gaze runs from the edge inward, roughly across the **oak & pine heart**, toward the deeper green.

What sits out that direction, deeper along that line: the **oak & pine heart** first, and beyond it the cluster of things deep in — the **yew grove**, the **ravine**, and further down-left the **dry creek → spiders** zone. She's not looking *at* any one of those that you can pin down; she's looking *into* the deep, the part of the forest you're explicitly not going. Could be nothing — owls watch things. Could be something. You can't tell direction finer than "inward, toward the deep" from where you're standing under her.

Worth noting for your own bearings: you're only a few steps past the treeline, in the safe edge band. Whatever's caught her is further in than you've gone.

## Narrator

▼ Tuesday, 13 September 1977 — 18:20

He crouched and brought the Lumos down low over them. Close up they were stranger — five-petalled, the white not quite white but faintly silvered, and each one beaded with moisture though the ground around was dry and the evening hadn't turned to dew yet. When he ran the light slow across the spread, the beads caught it and held it a half-second too long, so the whole low patch glimmered and dimmed in the wake of the beam like something breathing.

He bent and smelled one. Cool. Cucumber and cold stone and a thread of something mineral underneath, clean and odd.

He pinched a stem and picked it. It came away with a faint wet snap, and the moment it left the plant the silver in the petals went ordinary, dulling in his fingers to a plain pale flower — and the bead of moisture on it didn't fall. It sat in the cup of the petal, round and whole, not spreading, not dropping.

Athena watched from above.

He went down into the hollow at a run, ferns lashing his shins, the cold thickening around him as he dropped toward the spring — and pulled up short a few feet from it, the Lumos full on it now.

A unicorn. White, grown, lying on its side in the wet at the water's edge. Up close the dulled flank was worse than it had looked from the lip: the coat fouled and dark, the ribs showing, one foreleg folded wrong beneath it. There was no blood he could see — no wound, no torn hide — only the terrible stillness and the wrongness of the angle. The horn lay against the stone. An eye showed, open, catching his light.

And then the flank moved. The smallest rise. A breath — shallow, slow, a long pause, then another. Alive. Barely. The open eye rolled and found him, and the ear nearest him gave the faintest tremble, too weak to do more.

Potions came back to him as he worked: wiggentree bark — the bark, the salamander-side of Wiggenweld, the healing draught itself. And the berries — the thing about the berries was protection, resistance to dark things, to creatures of the dark. He didn't know if either meant anything to a unicorn.

He found two flat stones, sluiced spring-water over a fistful of bark, and ground it — hard, fast, the bark going to wet pale fibre and then to paste under the stone, the cold spring-water working into it.

The bark paste is a Wiggenweld-side material (healing register), but a paste is for wounds; a broken leg needs setting/immobilising, not poultice — though you don't have to know that cleanly in the moment.
