## User

[[does it feel colder than the other one in general? if so, can I do anything to warm it?]]

## Narrator

That's a fair thing for Caleb to register, so let me answer it straight rather than narrate ahead of you.

Yes — it's notably colder than the gold one was. The gold unicorn's flank was *warm* under his and Mei's hands, a calm warmth he specifically felt. This one is the opposite: the coat is cold and damp, the heartbeat slow, and it's lying in the wet at the spring's edge with that cold mist coming up off the water. Whether that cold is the illness or just from lying soaked in a cold hollow, he can't tell — but a downed animal losing heat into wet ground is a real, plain problem on top of everything else, and that part he *can* read without needing to be a Healer.

What Caleb could actually try, with what he has and knows:

He could get it *off* the cold wet ground and stop the heat-loss — but it's a full-grown unicorn and he's a slight 5'9"; he's not lifting it. He could get something between it and the ground, or build warmth around it.

Things in reach: a drying/warming charm on the coat and the ground beneath it (he knows the household-tier warming and hot-air charms); his own outer layer/robe laid over it; gathering dry leaf-mould or fern to bank against it; a small conjured or kindled warmth nearby (he'd be wary of fire near a panicked or fragile animal, and near dry forest). The Gryffindor sconce trick that "warmed the room" isn't here, but the *principle* — gentle, heatless warmth — is the register he'd want.

Want him to try drying-and-warming the coat with a charm, get his own robe over it, bank fern around it — or some combination? Tell me which and I'll run it.

He worked fast and methodical now, the crying gone quiet into purpose.

Hagrid made a sound that started as a sob and ended, helplessly, as a laugh — the big wet bark of a man who needed somewhere for all of it to go.

"Yeh great — *lad.*" He scrubbed his eyes with the back of a hand the size of a dinner plate. "Aye. Two pale skinny bodies in the dark an' me heart near stopped twice over." He shook his head, still half-laughing, the tears not gone. "Athena did right. *Yeh* did right. Look at this — fern an' warmin' an' yer own clothes off yer back. Most folk'd have run."

Then the laugh fell out of him and the gamekeeper came up, steady, his eyes going over the unicorn proper now — the breath, the gaunt sides, the leg, the cold spring. His face changed.

"Let me look at her. Don't yeh move from where y'are — yeh're the warm she's got. Just let me look."

## Narrator

That's a fair read, and it's well-grounded — the bond is canonically a two-way runaway-loop that runs hot on intense feeling, and Mei was in the RoR workshop with Imogen and Liri when Caleb went out. Fear, grief, the manic problem-solving spike of the last twenty minutes — she'd have felt all of it climbing and not stopping. Mei doesn't sit on that. She'd move, and she wouldn't leave the two of them behind when one of them is a creature-Healer and the other's the sharpest bench mind in the school.

So I'll run it that way: Caleb knowing, in his bones, that she's already coming — and likely with Imogen and Liri. That's not me deciding the plot for you; it's honoring what the bond and Mei's established character actually do. What I won't pre-decide is *where they are* right now or *how close* — that arrives in the fiction (Athena finding them en route, or them reaching the treeline, or Mei's pull sharpening down the bond as she nears). The discovery stays earned.

One thing that follows: if Caleb's certain Mei's inbound with Imogen, then Athena's job shifts — she might not need to go hunt the castle for Imogen at all; she might just need to fly to *Mei* (whom she can find down the same bond-sense) and lead the three of them in, the way she led Hagrid. That's cleaner and faster than searching the school.

Want Caleb to say that to Hagrid — "she's already coming, I can feel it, with Imogen and Liri; Athena just needs to go meet them and bring them straight here" — and send Athena to intercept? Or play it some other way?

[[we are waiting? for sprout and liri and dumbledore and pennycuik?

## Narrator

Good question — let me sort out who actually needs to be where, because the plan's got a couple of moving pieces and "wait at the hut" wasn't really a decision you made.

What McGonagall set up: the hut is the **muster gate** — her Patronus told Dumbledore (and by extension Pennycuik, Sprout, Liri, Tolley) to converge *there* so nobody wanders the forest alone in the dark. She posted herself at the hut "to hold for the cavalry." But she also just told *you* "lead on" — meaning she's ready to walk in *with you now,* ahead of the others, and let the rest follow the path once they arrive.

So there's a genuine fork, and it's yours to pick:

**Option A — you and McGonagall go in now,** straight to the unicorn (Mei, Imogen, Hagrid are already there working). The advantage: you get back to the patient fast, and McGonagall sees it firsthand. The others follow when they arrive — but then *someone* needs to guide them in, or they wait at the hut for a marker, since the hollow is 30 min deep off the path and easy to lose.

**Option B — you hold at the hut** until Pennycuik (the actual Healer) arrives, then lead the whole expert party in together in one trip. Advantage: the Healer doesn't get lost or delayed; one clean march. Cost: minutes standing still while the unicorn's down, and you're not at her side.

**Option C — split it:** McGonagall holds the gate for the incoming party, you go in now to the unicorn (you know the way and Mei needs you/you need to be there), and Athena or a sent Patronus bridges them in when Pennycuik lands.

Worth knowing for timing: Pennycuik has to be Flooed to the Headmaster's grate, then walk down to the hut, then 30 min in — he's realistically a fair while away. Mei, Imogen, and Hagrid are *already with the unicorn.* Sprout/Liri/Tolley are gathering the kit.

Which way do you want to play it?

Picking it straight up where it stands:

▼ Tuesday, 13 September 1977 — 19:01

He led, and McGonagall came, and the treeline took them — the gold seam of the hut swallowed behind, the dark closing cool and close, two wandlights moving through the wide-spaced trunks. He set the pace he'd promised: quick, even, sustainable, the way the ground had taught him on the way out. McGonagall kept it without complaint, her cloak gathered, her Lumos steady, her eyes everywhere — reading the forest the way she read everything.

The young birch gave way to the older trees. The cold deepened, and the smell of wet stone came up. And ahead, off the line, low against the dark, the first scatter of them showed — small, five-petalled, lit from within with that exact silver, beading their impossible cold water, blooming where nothing should.

He slowed and put his light on them. "There. Those."

So they waited — McGonagall silent, Caleb silent, the two of them standing at the hut in the cold blue dark, breath smoking, the forest a black wall a few yards off.

Footsteps up from the kitchens path: Liri, with Professor Sprout beside her — the squat witch in a coat thrown over her gardening things, a stuffed canvas satchel at her hip and Imogen's brown leather kit-roll clutched to her chest, fetched on her own Head-of-House authority. They drew up and didn't waste words. Sprout gave Caleb one sharp, unsurprised, assessing look — *of course it's you* — and held her peace.

And then, up the slope, a light: two figures coming down fast from the castle. The tall thin unmistakable shape of the Headmaster, robes a deep midnight blue — and beside him, hurrying to keep the pace, a stranger with a Healer's bag.

## Narrator

▼ Tuesday, 13 September 1977 — 19:03

"Tell me if this means anything before you see it." Low and fast, while the Headmaster's light came down the slope. "Deep in, full dark under the canopy — a low spread, five petals, blooming *now,* at night, in September. White, but self-lit: a faint silver glow from inside themselves, not catching my wandlight, *making* their own. Each one beaded with a drop of water that's colder than it has any right to be — and it won't fall, won't wet you; touch it and it draws to a thread and pulls back whole into the petal and leaves your finger dry. Smells of cucumber and cold stone, a mineral thread under it. Tastes—" a beat "—the same. Clean, cold, green-sweet, like springwater off the rock. And the instant you pick one the glow dies. Ordinary in your hand."

Sprout's chin had come up and stayed up. "You *tasted* it." Not quite a scold — a gardener cataloguing a reckless act she half-approved of. Then, slower, the easy answer not coming: "Self-lit. Cold that repels water. Glow that dies on cutting." She shook her head once. "No. I've names for most things that grow in that wood, and that's none of them. Show me. I'll not guess at a thing like that in the dark."

## Narrator

▼ Tuesday, 13 September 1977 — 19:03

"*Waddellia rorilucens,*" he offered.

Sprout barked a single laugh, surprised out of her — *dew-glowing Waddell-flower,* and she'd caught the Latin, of course she had. "Cheek of it. You find a thing for *one evening* and you've already hung your own name on it." But her mouth stayed crooked, and she didn't say no, exactly. "Earn it. You bring me a live one in that pot, rooted and breathing, and we'll see whose name goes where. The man who *grows* the unknown gets the binomial. Not the man who licked it in the dark."

And then they were down — Dumbledore arriving at the hut with the long unhurried stride that still somehow covered ground faster than anyone's run, half-moon spectacles catching the wandlight, and at his side the stranger: lean, weathered, white-whiskered, a battered Healer's bag in one hand and forty years of creatures in his face. He went straight past hello.

"Which way, and how bad?"

Patient appears weak and minimally responsive but breathing spontaneously. Recommend immediate assessment for shock, limb stabilization, and pain control."

## Narrator

▼ Tuesday, 13 September 1977 — 19:04

The change in Pennycuik was total and immediate. Whatever he'd braced for from a half-dressed seventh-year, it wasn't *that* — and he looked at Caleb properly for the first time, recalibrating fast.

"...Well. That's a clinical handover, not a panic." He said it half to himself. "Recumbent, bradycardic, hypothermic, BCS on the floor, suspected fracture at the fetlock, breathing spontaneous, no external bleed. Pain control and stabilise for shock." A short nod, filing it whole. "You've described it better than half the wizards I've trained. We'll do exactly that, in that order — warmth and shock first, the leg second, because a fractured fetlock won't kill her tonight and the cold will."

He hitched the bag across his chest, already turning to the black trees. "How long's she been warming, and what did the heat — charms, fire, what?" The questions came easy and fast, the questions of a man who'd start treating her from thirty minutes away if he could. "Walk and talk, lad. Lead. Everyone behind him — quiet feet, no crowding the patient when we're close."

The compound was used for dual topical comfort application and voluntary oral browse supplementation: offered by hand near the muzzle without force, while a small amount was gently applied around the injured forelimb without traction, setting, or manipulation. Patient demonstrated weak voluntary licking/swallowing, mild olfactory interest, and partial relaxation.
For hypothermia/exposure management, responder removed water from the coat and ground with Evanesco, applied warming charms to the patient and surrounding area, insulated the body with personal garments and dried ferns, and maintained browse within reach. Responder then initiated unclothed dorsolateral thermocompressive contact rewarming — colloquially and in sport: bareback unicorn spooning — while providing calm vocal and tactile reassurance."

## Narrator

▼ Tuesday, 13 September 1977 — 19:05

Pennycuik listened to all of it on the move, head down, bag swinging, and his eyebrows climbed steadily through the wiggentree slurry and the Evanesco and the ferns — a man hearing a student reinvent half of field triage from first principles in a dark wood.

"Bark, berries, spring water, browse, and a calming draught, ground to a slurry, dual-use topical and oral, offered without force—" he repeated, almost greedy for it. "That's not nothing, that's *thinking.* Wiggentree's sound. We'll talk about the rest."

Then: "—initiated unclothed dorsolateral thermocompressive contact rewarming, colloquially—"

"*Bareback unicorn spooning,*" Caleb supplied.

There was a beat. Then Pennycuik laughed — a short, astonished bark of it — and somewhere behind in the dark Sprout made a noise into her sleeve and even McGonagall's exhale had something in it. Dumbledore's voice came mild and amused out of the gloom: "I find I have no notes."

"Best thing you could've done," Pennycuik said, recovering, brisk again. "Skin-to-coat on a hypothermic recumbent. Textbook, if the textbook had any nerve. Keep leading."

## Narrator

Two good catches — both things Pennycuik would absolutely register. The owl-summoning is genuinely abnormal (owls don't come to a silent mental call down a bond; that's the kind of thing that'd make a forty-year creature man's head turn), and you flagged earlier you wanted Imogen's presence made explicit to him. Let me fold both in. I'll back the clock up a touch to before the column split off, since both belong in the walk-and-talk with Pennycuik still beside you.

▼ Tuesday, 13 September 1977 — 19:16

"One more thing before she gets here," Caleb said, still leading, Pennycuik at his shoulder. "Imogen Vale's the one with the patient now. Your track — magical-creatures healing, your apprentice line. She's been steady with her the whole time I've been gone."

"*Imogen's* in there." Pennycuik's voice changed — something between pride and alarm. "Good. She'll not have put a foot wrong. Then I've two reasons to hurry."

And then Athena came down out of the black canopy and lit on the branch ahead, called once, and Caleb hadn't broken stride to whistle or raise an arm — he'd done nothing a man could see.

Pennycuik stopped dead for half a second. "...You *called* that owl. Just now. I watched you do nothing and she came." Not a question. The sharp, hungry look of a man who'd just seen a thing he didn't have a name for. "We are going to talk about you, lad. After. At length." Then, moving again, brisk: "But not now. Now she leads, and you fetch Pomona her flower. Go."

## Narrator

▼ Tuesday, 13 September 1977 — 19:18

"Remind me to tell you about my other friend," Caleb said. "The adult gold unicorn."

Pennycuik, three steps after Athena already, stopped as if he'd walked into glass. He turned. Looked at Caleb. Looked at the dark. Looked back.

"The—" His mouth worked. "There is no such thing as a—" He stopped himself, because clearly there was, because the boy in front of him had kept one alive with mashed weeds and his own bare skin and called an owl with his mind. "*Gold.*" He pressed two fingers to the bridge of his nose, a man rationing his own astonishment to get through the next hour. "After. *Everything,* after, you tell me. If I stop now to hear it I'll never reach the one that's dying." And he went, fast, after the owl, the others' lights trailing into the trees.

Beside Caleb, Sprout looked at him sidelong. "Gold," she said faintly. "Of course it was." She knelt by the silver flowers and unrolled a cloth of small tools. "Right. Show me. Quick and gentle."
